Mishneh Torah, Heave Offerings
משנה תורה, הלכות תרומות
Mishneh Torah, trans. by Eliyahu Touger. Jerusalem, Moznaim Pub. c1986-c2007
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001020101/NLI

Mishneh Torah, Heave Offerings



Chapter 1

According to Scriptural Law, [the obligation to separate] the <i>terumot<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>The plural term is used in this halachah and in many instances in this text, because there are two types of terumot: a) "the great terumah," i.e., the terumah separated initially, and b) terumat ma'aser, the terumah the Levites separate from the tithes that are given them. See Hilchot Matanot Aniyim, ch. 6, for details regarding the separation of these allocations.</i></i></i> and the tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Here also the plural term is used because there are several tithes: the first tithe given to Levites, the second tithe which must be brought to Jerusalem, and the tithe for the poor. See <i>Hilchot Matanot Aniyim, loc. cit.</i></i> applies only in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 36b states that all the mitzvot involving the produce of the earth apply only in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.</i> [It applies] whether the Temple is standing or not.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Although the agricultural laws apply even when the Temple is not standing, there is a restriction to the time when they apply according to Scriptural Law. The entire Jewish people must be located in our holy land, as stated in Halachah 26. (See also <i>Hilchot Shemitah VeYovel</i> 10:8-9.)</i> The prophets ordained that these obligations should be observed in Babylon as well, because it is adjacent to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and the majority of the Jewish people journey to and from there.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Even though Babylonia is further away than the other lands mentioned in this halachah, it had a larger Jewish community and there was far more contact between its populace and the Jewish community of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Hence, the prophets gave it precedence over the other lands (Radbaz).</i> The Sages of the early generations<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages of the generation that followed Ezra [Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura (<i>Yadayim</i> 3:3).</i> ordained that they also be observed in the lands of Egypt and the lands of Ammon and Moab, because they are on the peripheries of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.
Whenever <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> is mentioned, the intent is the lands conquered by the King of Israel or a prophet with the consent of the entire Jewish people. This is called "a conquest of the community."<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See also <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 5:6.</i> If, however, an individual Jew, a family, or a tribe go and conquer a place for themselves - even in the land given to Abraham<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam speaks of the land being given to Abraham, not merely being promised to him, since from the time God said that the land was his, spiritually, it became his possession. Moreover, as the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Challah</i> 5:1) states, from Abraham's time onward, even though the land was still possessed by the Canaanites, the Jews were already its legal owners. See also <i>Bava Batra</i> 119b.</i> - it is not considered as <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> in the sense that one is obligated to observe all the mitzvot there.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the agricultural mitzvot that must be observed in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.</i> For this reason, Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">A king and a prophet.</i> and his court divided the entire Land of Israel into tribal portions<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since Joshua and his court had made this division, when the tribe went to conquer its portion, it was acting on behalf of the entire Jewish people.</i> even though it was not conquered [entirely] at that time. In this way, when every tribe would ascend and conquer its portion, it would not be considered as merely an individual conquest.
The lands which [King] David conquered outside of the Land of Canaan, e.g., Aram Naharaim, Aram Tzovah, Achlab,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">These regions are located in contemporary Syria.</i> and the like, even though he was a King of Israel and he was acting with the consent of the High Court,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The wars to conquer these lands are classified as voluntary wars, and a voluntary law requires the consent of the High Court (<i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 5:1).</i> is not considered as the Land of Israel with regard to all matters, nor is it like the Diaspora, i.e., Babylonia and Egypt with regard to all matters. Instead, it was removed from the category of the Diaspora, but did not enter the category of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.<br>Why was its level considered lower than that of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>? Because David conquered them before he conquered all of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Demai</i> 6:11) where he criticizes King David for this approach, quoting the <i>Sifri</i> (<i>Parshat Ekev</i>) that states that he did not act in accordance with the Torah. Since the designation of the land as <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> is through Divine fiat as expressed through the Torah. Accordingly, a conquest that is not in accord with the Torah cannot bring about such a designation (<i>Likkutei Sichot,</i> Vol. V, p. 9).</i> Instead, there were still members of the seven nations there. If, however, he had conquered <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> entirely, in all of its boundaries, and afterwards conquered<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">With the consent of the High Court.</i> other lands,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Even lands outside of the homeland promised to Abraham (Radbaz).</i> his entire conquest would have been equivalent to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> with regard to all matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">And the agricultural laws would have to be observed there.</i> The lands which [King] David conquered are called Syria.
There are dimensions of [the laws that apply to] Syria that resemble [the laws that apply in] <i>Eretz Yisrael</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to those points the Rambam mentions below, among the other examples of this principle are: its air is not considered as a source of impurity (<i>Hilchot Tuma'at Meit</i> 11:6); it is permitted to have a gentile compose a deed of purchase for land there on the Sabbath so that one will acquire the land (<i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 6:11).</i> and there are dimensions of [its laws] that resemble [those which apply] in the Diaspora.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Among the examples of the relevant laws: its earth is considered as a source of ritual impurity (<i>Hilchot Tuma'at Meit, loc. cit.</i>); a servant from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> sold there is granted his freedom (<i>Hilchot Avadim</i> 8:6).</i> A person who purchases landed property [in Syria] is comparable to one who purchases [land] in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> with regard to <i>terumot</i>, tithes, and the Sabbatical year.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 15.</i> All [the laws that apply] in Syria are of Rabbinic origin.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Scriptural Law, it is not part of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.</i>
All of the lands that [the Jews] who ascended from Egypt took possession of were sanctified in the first consecration [of the land]. When they were exiled, that sanctity was nullified.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 6:16, the Rambam states: "It was no longer <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>," i.e., none of the halachic obligations apply there.</i> [The rationale is that] the initial consecration came about because of the conquest. [Hence,] its consecration was effective for the time [it was under their rule], but not for all time.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was established by conquest, it could be nullified by conquest. See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah, loc. cit.</i> The Radbaz explains that when the Jews ascended from Egypt, they did not make a statement, consecrating the land. Therefore its holiness could be nullified.</i> When, by contrast, the descendants of the exiles ascended [from Babylon] and took possession of a portion of the land,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Yevamot</i> 16a states: "Much of the land that was conquered by [the Jews who] ascended from Egypt was not conquered by [the Jews who] ascended from Babylon.</i> they consecrated it a second time.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, since the entire Jewish people did not return together with Ezra, this consecration is only of Rabbinic origin.</i> [This consecration] is perpetuated forever, for that time and for all time.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">And thus at present, Ezra's consecration of the land is still effective.<br>In his gloss to <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah, loc. cit.</i>, the <i>Kessef Mishnah</i> finds these statements difficult to accept because of the following questions:<br>a) On what basis is <i>chazakah</i> considered a more effective means of acquisition than conquest?<br>b) After the initial conquest of Eretz Yisrael, the Jewish people manifested their ownership over it and thus, effected a <i>chazakah</i>. If so, why is Ezra's <i>chazakah</i>, which was not preceded by conquest, more effective than the <i>chazakah</i> which followed the original conquest? Why should the conquest detract from the consecration of the land?<br>To explain: As mentioned above, the Jewish people's ownership of the land began with God's promise of the land to Abraham. The sanctity of the land, by contrast, did not begin until the Jewish people actually took possession of it, upon their reentry into the land after the exodus from Egypt and the journey through the desert. At that time, they were commanded to conquer the land and take it forcefully from the Gentiles (see Numbers 32:29,Deuteronomy 3:21, <i>et al</i>). Since God made the consecration of the land dependent upon its conquest by the Jewish people, it follows that conquest by a Gentile nation can nullify its holiness.<br>In contrast, Ezra was not commanded to reconquer <i>Eretz Yisrael,</i> but to settle it. In this instance, God made the sanctity of the land dependent on the Jewish people manifesting their ownership over it. In other words, the sanctity came from manifesting the true reality: that <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> is a Jewish land. Accordingly, since <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> remains our land, regardless of how many times it has been conquered by Gentiles, the sanctity effected by that manifestation of ownership also continues eternally (<i>Likkutei Sichot</i>, Vol. XV, pp. 102-109).</i><br>[The Sages] left those places that had been settled by [the Jews who] ascended from Egypt, but were not settled by those who ascended from Babylon in their previous [halachic status] and did not exempt them from [the obligations of] <i>terumah</i> and tithes so that the poor could rely upon them in the Sabbatical year.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">And receive the tithe of the poor.</i> Our holy teacher<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbi Yehudah <i>HaNasi</i>.</i> released Beit Shan<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and maintains that Beit Shan was released only from those obligations that are of Rabbinic origin.</i> from those places which [the Jews] who ascended from Babylon did not take hold of. He decided that Ashkelon should be exempted from the tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> relates that Rabbi Yehudah <i>HaNasi</i> released these cities from the obligation of the tithes, because they were not populated by Jews and were not close to the area settled by Jews.</i>
Thus the entire earth is divided into three categories in relation to those mitzvot involving the land: <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, Syria, and the Diaspora. <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> itself is divided into two categories: a) those portions settled by the Jews who ascended from Babylonia, and b) those portions that were settled only by the Jews who ascended from Egypt.<br>The Diaspora is divided into two categories: a) Egypt, Babylon, Ammon, and Moab in which the mitzvot are observed according to the decrees of the sages and the prophets and the other lands in which [the obligations of] the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes are not observed.
What are [the boundaries of] the land settled by the Jews who ascended from Egypt? From Rekem which is in the east of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> until the Mediterranean Sea. From Ashkelon<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Ashkelon - and similarly, the other cities the Rambam refers to - is located near, but not exactly on the same site - as the city presently called by that name. With regard to Ashkelon in particular, there is discussion of the issue by the commentaries, for some sources (see Radbaz and <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>) explain that it was part of the land conquered by the Jews who ascended from Egypt. It was not, however, conquered by the Jews who ascended from Babylonia.</i> which is in the south of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> until Acre which is in the north. As one proceeds from Acre to Kziv,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., proceeding northward.</i> the area to one's right, on the east, can generally be assumed to be of the Diaspora - it is impure as is the lands of the nations<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages decreed that merely touching the earth of the Diaspora is sufficient to impart ritual impurity. See <i>Hilchot Tuma'at Meit</i>, ch. 11.</i> and is exempt from [the mitzvot of] the tithes and the Sabbatical year - unless it is known that it was part of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. The area to one's left, to the west, can generally be assumed to be part of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> - it is pure [from the impurity associated with] the lands of the nations and is obligated with regard to [mitzvot of] the tithes and the Sabbatical year - unless it is known to be part of the Diaspora.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as depicted in the map drawn by the Rambam to accompany the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, the borders of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> ascend northward from Acre to Kziv, but there is a strip of land in the center that did not become part of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.</i> With regard to the entire area that slopes downward from the Samnum Mountains:<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Our text is based on authoritative manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> as cited in the Rav Shabsie Frankel edition. The standard printed text refers to the Umenum Mountains. See also the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Sh'vi'it</i> 6:1).<br>In the map the Rambam drew to depict this matter, the Samnum Mountains are located in the southeastern corner of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, near Ashkelon. Most of the other commentaries understand this term as referring to the Umenum Mountain range in Northern Lebanon.</i> The inner [portion]<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., to the south.</i> is <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, the portion outside the Samnum Mountains is the Diaspora.<br>With regard to the islands in the sea, we consider their [status dependent] on an [imaginary] line extended from the Samnum Mountains until the River of Egypt.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Radbaz, this refers to Wadi el Arish and not the Nile. Most other commentaries, however, identify "the River of Egypt" as the Nile.</i> Those within this line are part of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Those outside this line are part of the Diaspora. This is a depiction of the matter.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the Rambam included the accompanying map so that the matter would be clearly understood.. This reflects his thrust throughout the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>: to provide his readers with an applicable text without any ambiguity.</i>
Which area did the Jews who ascended from Babylonia settle in? [The area] from Kziv inward toward the east. They did not, however, take possession of the area from Kziv outward until Amana which is Samnum until the river, i.e., the River of Egypt.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Thus it appears that the entire coastal range of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> was not settled by the Jews who ascended from Babylon.</i> And they did not take possession of Kziv itself.
What constitutes Syria? From <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and below<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The term "below" in this context is problematic. It does not mean "south," because significant portions of Syria are more northerly than <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Some commentaries understand it as meaning in height, because as <i>Kiddushin</i> 69b states, <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> is higher than other lands.</i> parallel to Aram Naharaim and Aram Tzovah, the entire region of the Euphrates until Babylonia, e.g., Damascus, Achalev, Charan, Minbag, and the like until Shinar<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Shinar itself, however, is part of the Diaspora, as evident from Halachah 1.</i> and Tzahar. These are considered like Syria. Acre, by contrast, is considered part of the Diaspora, like Ashkelon.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned above, there are commentaries who consider Ashkelon to be part of the land conquered by the Jews who ascended from Egypt, but not that conquered by the Jews ascending from Babylonia. Some also place Acre in this category.</i> These are the boundaries of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.
When a gentile purchases land in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, he does not cause it to be absolved from [the observance of] the mitzvot.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah for the practical application of this principle.</i> Instead, its holiness is still intact.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gittin</i> 47a derives this concept from Leviticus 25:23: "The entire land is Mine." Implied is that the land belongs to God and His ownership cannot be absolved through conquest by any earthly power.<br>This ruling was a point of question for the Rambam. As indicated by the notes of Rav Kappach to his translation of the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Pe'ah</i> 4:9; <i>Gittin</i> 4:9, <i>et al</i>), the Rambam changed his mind concerning the issue several times and amended his text of the Commentary to the Mishnah accordingly. At first, he stated the opinion quoted here, then adopted the opposite view, that a gentile's acquisition does cause the agricultural obligations to be absolved, and finally returned to his original position. The <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:3-4) follow the Rambam's ruling here. See also <i>Hilchot Shemitah VeYovel</i> 4:29 where this issue is also discussed.</i> Therefore, if a Jew purchases this land from [the gentile], it is not considered as a private conquest.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, the agriculture laws would not be applicable, as stated in Halachah 2.</i> Instead, he should separate the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes and bring the first fruits. All these [obligations] are Scriptural in origin, as if it was never sold to a gentile. When a gentile acquires land in Syria, his acquisition has the power to absolve [the land] from the obligations of tithes and the Sabbatical year,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The entire obligation to observe the agricultural laws in Syria is Rabbinic in origin (Halachah 4) and our Sages did not enforce their decrees in this instance.</i> as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Halachot 15-19.</i>
[The following laws apply with regard to] produce belonging to a gentile that grew on land that he purchased in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. If all the work involving the produce was completed in the possession of a gentile and the gentile straightened the edges of the grain heap,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., completed the work involved in preparing them. See <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 3:13.</i> they are exempt, because [the verse mentioning the obligation to separate <i>terumah</i>, Deuteronomy 18:4] mentions "your grain," [thus excluding] the grain of a gentile.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">From the interpretation of the term by the Sages (<i>Bechorot</i> 11b), the intent seems to be that not only that the grain must belong to a Jew, but that he must perform the work to complete its preparation for use.</i><br>If a Jew purchases [the produce] after it was harvested, but before the tasks involved with it were completed and they were completed by a Jew, all [of the agricultural obligations apply with regard to the produce] according to Scriptural Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., for the produce to be obligated in the agricultural laws according to Scriptural Law, it must belong to the Jew and the Jew must complete the work involved with preparing it. If only one of these conditions was met, the obligations must be fulfilled only because of Rabbinic decree. See Halachah 13.</i> He must separate the great <i>terumah</i> and give it to a priest and [separate] the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> and sell it to a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>terumat ma'aser</i> is sold to the priest and not given to him for the reason explained shortly afterwards by the Rambam. It cannot be kept by the owner, because he is forbidden to partake of it. Instead, it is consecrated and must be eaten by a priest in a state of ritual purity (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:10).</i> The first tithe belongs to him,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he must separate the first tithe to fulfill the agricultural obligations incumbent upon the produce, but is not obligated to give it to the Levites. There is no difficulty in him keeping the tithes, because, as the Rambam explains, he is not obligated to give them to the Levite and there is no prohibition against him partaking of them, because they are not consecrated at all and any person may partake of them, even when he is not in a state of ritual purity.</i> because he can tell a Levite with regard to the tithes and a priest with regard to the <i>terumat ma'aser</i>: "I am coming in the stead of a person<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The gentile.</i> from which you could not have collected anything."<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Had the gentile completed the tasks associated with preparing the grain, he would not have been under any obligation to make any of these separations. Hence, although the Jew is obligated to separate the various allocations, the obligation is primarily a ritual one and not a financial one. With the exception of the great <i>terumah</i>, he is not obligated to accept any loss and may keep the first tithes for himself.</i><br>Why do we say that he should not give the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> to a priest as [he gives him] the great <i>terumah</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., like the first tithe, the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> must be separated, but it need not be given to the priest.</i> Because with regard to <i>terumat ma'aser,</i> it is written: "When you take the tithes from the children of Israel...."<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">There is, by contrast, no such verse stated with regard to the great <i>terumah</i>. Hence, it is not considered the property of the owner, but instead, must be given - and not sold - to a priest.</i> [Implied is that from] untithed produce that you purchase from a Jew, you should separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i> and give it to a priest, but from untithed produce that you purchase from a gentile, you need not give the priest the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> you separate from it. Instead, you sell it to the priest and take the money.
[The following laws apply if] a gentile sold his produce to a Jew while it was connected to the earth. If [he sold it] before it developed to a stage when he is obligated to separate tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">See below and <i>Hilchot Ma'aserot</i> 2:5 for a definition of this concept.</i> and the work [associated with it] was completed while it was owned by the Jew, [the Jew] is responsible for all of the agricultural obligations and should give the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes to their [appropriate] owners.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the tithes to a Levite and the <i>terumot</i> to a priest. It is as if the produce grew on land belonging to the Jew from the outset.</i> If [the gentile] sold [the produce] after it developed to a stage when he is obligated to separate tithes, he should separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i> and the tithes and give them to their [appropriate] owners according to the [acceptable] reckoning.<br>What is implied? If he purchased grain that had been sown from a gentile after it reached a third of its growth and the work associated with its completion was performed while it was in the possession of a Jew, he should separate the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as explained in the previous halachah and notes, the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes must be separated even if they - or a portion of them - are kept by the owner.</i> He should give two thirds of the tithes to a Levite.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">He may keep one-third of the tithes as his own. The rationale is that since the produce reached one third of its growth in the possession of the gentile, the Jew is not liable to give away the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> and the tithes for that portion of the crop. With regard to that portion, the concepts stated in the previous halachah apply. With regard to the remaining two thirds, it is considered as produce owned by a Jew and is obligated in all agricultural laws.</i> [With regard to the] <i>terumat ma'aser</i>, he should give two thirds to the priest and sell him one third.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in the previous halachah, he must give the great <i>terumah</i> to the priest without receiving any return whatsoever.</i>
[The following laws apply if] a Jew sold his produce to a gentile before it developed to a stage when he is obligated to separate tithes and the work [associated with it] was completed by the gentiles, it is exempt from the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">It is as if the produce grew in the possession of the gentile from the outset in which instance, they are exempt from all agricultural obligations, as stated in Halachah 11.<br>The Ra'avad objects to this ruling based on the principle stated in Halachah 10: "When a gentile purchases land in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, he does not cause it to be absolved from [the observance of] the mitzvot. Instead, its holiness is still intact." The Radbaz explains that this principle applies only when the gentile purchases the land, but not when he purchases merely produce. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:6) quotes the Rambam's view.</i> If [he purchased it] after it developed to a stage when the tithes are obligated to be separated, even if the gentile completed the work associated with it, all of the agricultural obligations are incumbent upon it according to Rabbinic decree.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the produce is later sold to a Jew, the Jew must separate the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes. The rationale is that since the produce grew in a Jew's possession, it is considered as Jewish produce.</i><br>Similarly, if a gentile completed [the work associated with] produce belonging to a Jew,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the interpretation of Rav Yosef Corcus as quoted by the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>. He explains that, based on the exegesis of Deuteronomy 18:4 by <i>Bechorot</i> 11b (see Halachah 11 and notes), as long as the work is completed by a gentile, the fact that the produce belongs to a Jew is not significant.</i> since the work associated with them was completed by a gentile, the obligation for <i>terumot</i> and tithes is incumbent upon them, but it is only Rabbinic in origin.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, the Rambam writes (based on <i>Menachot</i> 67a) that this decree was enacted lest Jewish landowners have gentiles complete the work associated with the crops to avoid having to separate the tithes.</i>
If a gentile sold produce that was still connected to the earth after it reached the stage when the tithes are obligated to be separated and [another] gentile straightened the edges of the grain heap in the possession of a Jew, [the produce] is not obligated in the <i>terumot</i> or the tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even according to Rabbinic Law.</i> Since the produce reached the stage when the tithes are obligated to be separated in the possession of a gentile and a gentile straightened the edges of the grain heap,<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., performs the task which completes the work associated with the produce at which time the obligation to separate the <i>terumot</i> and tithes takes effect.</i> [the fact] the produce belongs to a Jew [is not significant].<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is as if the produce belonged entirely to the gentile and the laws stated in Halachah 11 apply.</i>
When a person purchases a field in Syria, he is obligated by Rabbinic decree to separate <i>terumot</i> and tithes like one who purchases [a field] in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is referring to the wording of <i>Challah</i> 4:11: "One who purchases [land] in Syria is comparable to one who purchases [land] in the suburbs of Jerusalem."</i> according to Scriptural Law, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 4.</i> If, however, a person [merely] purchases produce from a gentile - whether it was harvested or [still] connected to the earth - he is exempt, because [the produce] does not come from his land.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since the entire obligation to separate the <i>terumot</i> and tithes in Syria is Rabbinic in origin, our Sages applied it only when the Jew owned the land itself. Since the obligation to separate tithes when one purchases produce alone is merely Rabbinic in origin even in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, our Sages did not apply it in Syria.</i> [This applies] even if [he purchases the produce] before it develops to a stage when the tithes are obligated to be separated [and] even though a Jew straightened the edges of the grain heap.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., performs the task which completes the work associated with the produce at which time the obligation to separate the <i>terumot</i> and tithes takes effect.</i>
[The following laws apply when a person] purchases land in Syria to which produce is attached. If it reached a stage when the tithes are obligated to be separated while in the possession of a gentile, it is exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is considered as if it was the gentile's produce entirely.</i> [Conversely,] if it did not reach a stage when the tithes are obligated to be separated [in his possession],<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The previous growth of the produce is not significant.</i> [the Jewish purchaser] is obligated to tithe it, since he purchased it together with the land.
When a Jew is working as a sharecropper for a gentile in Syria, his produce is exempt from the tithes, because he does not own the land itself at all. A gentile's purchase of land in Syria causes it to be absolved from [the obligation of] the tithes, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 10.</i> The same laws apply when one rents a field, but stipulates that the payment will be made in produce,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's definition of the term <i>chocher</i> in <i>Hilchot Sechirut</i> 8:1.</i> enters into a sharecropping agreement as a <i>mekabel</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Both the terms <i>aris</i> and <i>mekabel</i> refer to sharecropping agreements. The difference between them is that an <i>aris</i> is working for the owner of the field for a percentage of the crops (see <i>Hilchot Shluchin</i> 8:5), while a <i>mekabel</i> is renting the field from the owner for a percentage of the crops (<i>Hilchot Sechirut</i> 8:2).</i> or rents a field for money from a gentile in Syria, he is absolved [from the obligations of] <i>terumot</i> and tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The motivating principle behind these laws is that renting the field is not equivalent to purchasing it, for the land itself does not become his (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 15a).<br>In <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, by contrast, a person entering any one of these types of agreements would be obligated to separate the tithes (Radbaz).</i>
[The following law applies when] a Jew purchases a field from a gentile in Syria before [the crops] reached a third of their development and sells it to a gentile after the crops reach a third of their development. If a Jew purchases it a second time,<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> who debates whether, to be obligated, the Jew must purchase the field or it is sufficient for him to purchase the produce alone without purchasing the land. It appears that he concludes that if one does not purchase the field, he is exempt as indicated by Halachah 15.</i> he is obligated [to separate] the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that the Jew may not deduct from the tithes the portion of the produce that grew in the gentile's possession. Since it incurred the obligation for tithes while in the Jew's possession and was completed by him, everything else is not of consequence.</i> because [the field] incurred that obligation in the possession of a Jew.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when it reached a third of its development.</i>
When a Jew owns land in Syria and he hired a sharecropper and the sharecropper sent him produce,<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">As payment of the produce due the owner from his field.</i> he is not obligated to separate [the <i>terumot</i> and tithes], for it is possible to say that [the sharecropper] purchased [the produce he gave him] from the marketplace.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we allow for the possibility that the sharecropper sold the produce from the field and then purchased other produce to pay the owner his due. The rationale for this leniency is that since the obligation to tithe in Syria is Rabbinic in origin, as long as we are not definitely certain that it applies, we rule leniently. In <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, he would have to separate the tithes (Radbaz).</i> [This applies] provided this species is available in the marketplace.
There is an obligation [to separate] the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes [from crops grown on land owned in] partnership with a gentile.<br>What is implied? When a Jew and a non-Jew purchased a field in partnership - even if they divided the field while the grain was standing,<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">In such a situation, there is room to say that the principle of <i>bereirah</i> applies. <i>Bereirah</i> means that retroactively, it is considered as if the division that was ultimately made was made at the outset (see <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 7:1). To explain within the context of the present situation: Were a portion of the field to belong to the gentile, there would be no obligation to separate the tithes from the grain that grew in that portion. To apply the principle of <i>bereirah</i> would mean that, after the division of the crops, it is clarified that at the outset, the portion of the field where the crops given to the gentile grew belonged to him. There is a debate among the Sages whether the principle of <i>bereirah</i> can be applied and with regard to halachah, the accepted general principle is that in question of Scriptural Law, it does not apply. Thus in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, where the obligation to tithe is one of Scriptural Law, we do not accept this principle and maintain that the Jew has a share in the grain given to the gentile and hence, it is under obligation to be tithed.</i> and needless to say, if they divided it after it was collected in a grain heap<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, all authorities agree that the principle of <i>bereirah</i> is not applied.</i> - <i>tevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Produce which is forbidden because the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes were not separated from it.</i> and ordinary produce are mixed together in each and every stalk of grain in the gentile's share.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Hence if a Jew purchases that produce, he must separate the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes.</i> [This applies] even if the gentile straightened the edges of the grain heap and thus the obligation is Rabbinic in origin,<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In such an instance, there is room to say that the principle of <i>bereirah</i> should be applied, because the obligation to separate the tithes is Rabbinic in origin. Nevertheless, since the fundamental obligation is Scriptural, we rule stringently even with regard to this dimension which is Rabbinic (Radbaz).</i> as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 13.</i>
When does the above apply? In <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, where [the obligation to] tithe is Scriptural in origin, and according to Scriptural Law, the principle of <i>bereirah</i> does not apply. If, however, he purchased a field in Syria, since [the obligation to] tithe there is Rabbinic in origin, even if they divided a grain heap, the portion of the gentile is exempt entirely.<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">When quoting these laws, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:11) states that in the present age, when the obligation to separate <i>terumot</i> and tithes in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> is Rabbinic in origin (see Halachah 26), the laws applying to produce grown in Syria also apply to produce grown in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.</i>
When produce from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> is taken to the Diaspora, it is exempt from the obligations of <i>challah</i>, the <i>terumot</i>, and the tithes, [for one of the prooftexts requiring the separation of these gifts, Numbers 15:18]<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">And from that verse which speaks of the obligation to separate <i>challah</i>, we learn that one is also obligated to separate the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes.</i> states: "[the land] to which I am bringing you." [Implied is that] these obligations exist there alone. In the Diaspora, one is exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs and maintains that the exemption from the obligation to tithe applies only according to Scriptural Law. According to Rabbinic Law, all authorities agree that one is obligated, for this produce is comparable to that of Ammon and Moav. The Radbaz does not accept this perspective, stating that there is no source which maintains that a Rabbinic obligation exists. The Radbaz does, however, qualify the Rambam's ruling, explaining that it applies only when the work that makes the produce obligated to be tithed is completed in the Diaspora. If this work is completed in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, the obligation to separate the tithes has already been incurred and they must be separated even if the produce was later taken to the Diaspora. For this reason, in most instances, <i>terumah</i> and tithes must be separated from produce that is grown in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> in the present age and later exported to the Diaspora. This, however, applies to fruits only, not vegetables as stated in Chapter 2, Halachah 6.</i> If [the produce] was taken to Syria, one is obligated by Rabbinic decree.<br>Conversely, we are obligated [to separate] <i>challah</i> from produce from the Diaspora that was brought into <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, as [suggested by the phrase] "to which." [Implied is that] one is liable [to make these gifts] there, whether the produce is from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> or the Diaspora. If the obligation [to separate <i>challah</i> or the tithes] was established [because of the actions] of a Jew after the produce entered <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, there is an obligation of Rabbinic origin to separate the tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> questions why the obligation to separate the tithes is only of Rabbinic origin. Since the concept is based on the exegesis of the same Biblical term as mentioned above, if the work that made the produce obligated to be tithed was performed in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, why is the obligation not Scriptural in origin? The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> answers that since the prooftext mentions "the bread of the land," one can conclude that the obligation applies only to produce grown in the Holy Land itself. Alternatively, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests that indeed if the work that makes the produce obligated to be tithed is completed only in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, the obligation is indeed Scriptural in origin. This ruling is quoted by the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:22.</i>
When earth from the Diaspora is brought to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> by ship, if the ship's [bottom] comes in contact with the earth [of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>],<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Challah</i> 2:2).</i> <i>terumah</i> and tithes must [be separated from produce] growing on [the earth in the ship] and the Sabbatical laws must be observed as if [the produce] was growing in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Even if it does not have a hole, since wood is porous, it is as if the earth on the ship is connected to the earth in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.</i>
When a tree is standing in the Diaspora and its foliage leans into <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> or it is standing in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and its foliage leans into the Diaspora, [the determination of its status] depends on its roots.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the trunk is located in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, the tithes must be separated from the produce. If it is located in the Diaspora, they need not.<br>The <i>Rambam LeAm</i> notes that the laws in this halachah must be qualified on the basis of Halachah 22. For if as stated there, everything depends on where the work that makes the produce obligated in the tithes is completed, of what difference does it make where the produce grows. That text explains that if the person separates tithes from produce that grew in the Diaspora before the work that makes the produce obligated in the tithes is completed, even if that work is completed in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, the tithes separated are not significant. For at the time, they were separated, there was no obligation to tithe the produce.</i> If some of its roots were in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and others were in the Diaspora - even if they were separated by glistening stone, [it is considered as if] <i>tevel</i> and ordinary produce are mixed together [throughout the entire produce].
[The following rules apply when] a plant in a flowerpot with a hole has not sprouted roots [outside the flowerpot]. If its roots were in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and its foliage in the Diaspora, [the determination of its status] depends on its foliage.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, if there is a hole in the bottom of a flowerpot, it is considered as if the plant is connected to the earth beneath it. Nevertheless, in this instance, <i>Gittin</i> 22a teaches that we follow the foliage of the plant and not the position of the hole in the flower pot.</i>
In the present era, even in the areas settled by the Jews who ascended from Babylonia, even those [settled] in the era of Ezra,<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Who brought about a consecration of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> that is effective until the present day, as stated in Halachah 5.</i> [the obligation to separate] <i>terumah</i> does not have the status of a Scriptural commandment, merely that of a Rabbinic decree. [The rationale is that] the Scriptural [commandment to separate] <i>terumah</i> applies only in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and only when the entire<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">More precisely, the majority of the Jewish people, for the majority is considered equivalent to the entire whole.</i> Jewish people are located there. [This is derived from the phrase]<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">There is no such phrase in the Torah stated with regard to <i>terumah</i> or the tithes. The commentaries explain that <i>Ketubot</i> 25a makes such a statement with regard to the mitzvah of <i>challah</i> based on Numbers 15:18 and the laws regarding <i>terumah</i> are derived from it. See also a parallel ruling in <i>Hilchot Shemitah VeYovel</i> 10:8.</i> "When you enter...." [Implied is that] the entire [Jewish people] must enter [the land], as they did when they took possession of the land originally<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and maintains that the obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> has the status of a Scriptural commandment in the present age. In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Caro elaborates in support of the Rambam's position and in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:2), he rules accordingly.</i> and as will happen in the future when they take possession of the land a third time.<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In the era of Mashiach. At that time, the observance of all the agricultural laws will return to Scriptural status, as stated in <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 11:1.</i> In contrast, the second time [the people] took possession of the land, in the time of Ezra, only a portion entered.<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, we find several sources (<i>Yoma</i> 9b) where our Sages criticized the majority of the people for remaining in Babylonia.</i> Hence, they were not obligated according to Scriptural Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries point to Ezra 2:62-63 as indicating that in the era of the Second Temple, <i>terumah</i> was still observed with the status of a Scriptural mitzvah. For Ezra and Nechemiah restricted the priests whose lineage was not affirmed from partaking of it. Nevertheless, it can be explained that at that time, they were expecting the majority of the people to return and then, the status of the mitzvah would revert to that of Scriptural origin. Lest confusion arise concerning these priests were this to occur, they enforced these restrictions.</i> Similarly, it appears to me that the same concept applies with regard to the tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 1:7 which points to several dimensions of the equivalence between <i>terumah</i> and the tithes.</i> In the present era, this obligation [as well] has the status of a Rabbinic decree like <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Although in Halachah 1, the Rambam writes that the Scriptural status of <i>terumah</i> and the tithes is not dependent on the Temple, there is no contradiction to this halachah. If the majority of the Jewish people are located in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, these mitzvos have the status of Scriptural Law, even if the Temple is not standing. Conversely, even if the Temple is standing, if the majority of the Jewish people are not located in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> (as was the case in the Second Temple era), the status of these commandments is Rabbinic (Radbaz).</i>

Chapter 2

[We are] obligated [to separate] <i>terumah</i> from food<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">To exclude herbs grown as dyes and the like. This is derived from the fact that the prooftext cited states: "And you shall eat" [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ma'aserot</i> 1:1)].</i> [designated] for human [consumption],<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to animal fodder (<i>Shabbat</i> 68a).</i> that is guarded,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">By its owner, rather than left ownerless (the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> and that grows from the earth.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This excludes mushrooms whose roots do not enter the earth.</i> It is a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 126) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 507) include this commandment among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> to separate the first portion [of such crops] for a priest, as [Deuteronomy 18:4] states: "You shall give him the first portion of your grain, your wine, and your oil." Just as grain, wine, and oil are [agricultural produce that] is food that is designated for humans, grows from the earth, and has an owner - as indicated by the term "your grain,"<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., grain that someone calls his own.</i> - so, too, [we are] obligated to [separate] <i>terumot</i> and tithes from any analogous [agricultural produce].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the Rambam considers grain, wine, and oil as examples, teaching that all similar produce must be tithes. Other commentaries (the Ra'avad, gloss to <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 1:9; Rashi, <i>Berachot</i> 36a) maintain that, according to Scriptural Law, we are obligated to separate <i>terumot</i> and the tithes from only these three types of produce. They maintain that the obligation to separate the tithes from other types of produce is Rabbinic in origin. See also Halachah 6.</i>
[We are] obligated [to separate] <i>terumah</i> and tithes from vetch,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Beans that are used as animal fodder.</i> even though it is not [usually] food for humans, since it is eaten [by humans] in a year of famine. [We are] obligated [to separate] tithes from <i>siyah</i>, hyssop,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This is the common translation for the term <i>aizov</i>. In his notes to the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, <i>Sh'vi'it</i>, ch. 8, note 6, Rav Kappach identifies the Arabic term used by the Rambam as "oregano."</i> and <i>koranit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ma'aserot</i> 3:3), the Rambam identifies these species as types of hyssop that grow wildly in gardens and courtyards.</i> that are sown for human consumption. Similar laws apply to analogous species.<br>If they were sown as animal fodder, even though the person changed his mind and thought to use them for human [consumption] while they were still connected to the ground, they are exempt. For the intent [of the owner] while [the produce] is growing is of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This is a principle applicable in other halachic contexts as well. See <i>Hilchot Tuma'at Ochalin</i> 3:3.</i><br>[The following rules apply if] these herbs grow in a courtyard on their own. If the produce growing in the courtyard is guarded,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., it has a fence or the like around it.</i> [we are] obligated to [separate the tithes], for most probably it is for human consumption. If the produce within is not guarded, it is exempt.
Seeds of garden vegetables that are not eaten, e.g., turnip seed, raddish seed, onion seed, and the like are exempt from <i>terumah</i> and tithes, because they are not used for human consumption. [We are,] by contrast, obligated [to separate] <i>terumah</i> and tithes from caraway seed.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">For it is used for human consumption. The Radbaz maintains that the obligation is Scriptural in origin.</i>
Blossoms of chilba,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">A pungent herb.</i> mustard seed, white beans, capers, and the caper bark<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is taken from the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ma'aserot</i> 4:6).</i> are exempt, because they are not considered as produce.<br>When does the above apply? When they were sown for seed. When, however, they were sown for their produce, there is an obligation [to tithe].<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz maintains that the obligation is Rabbinic in origin.</i> Similarly, there is an obligation [to tithe] caper berries, because they are produce.
When coriander was sown for seed, its vegetable is exempt from <i>terumah</i> and the tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was sown as seed, the vegetables that grow are of no consequence.</i> If it was sown as a vegetable, we must separate <i>terumot</i> and tithes from both the vegetables and the seed.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">For even the seed can be used for human consumption.</i> Similarly, when <i>shevet</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In his notes to the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Pe'ah</i>, ch. 3, note 4), Rav Kappach identifies this as dill.</i> is sown for seed, its vegetable is exempt from <i>terumah</i> and the tithes. If it was sown as a vegetable, we must separate <i>terumot</i> and tithes from both the vegetables and the seed. The seed capsules need not be tithed. If he sowed it for the sake of the seed capsules, he must separate <i>terumot</i> and tithes from the vegetables, the seed, and the seed capsules. Similarly, when cress and wild cress<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See Rav Kappach's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ma'aserot</i> 4:5).</i> were sown as a vegetable, we must separate tithes from both the seed and the vegetables.<br>What is meant by [the statement] we must separate tithes from both the seed and the vegetables? That if he gathered the vegetables to eat them, he must separate <i>terumah</i> and the tithes and [only] then, eat. When [he allows the plant] to dry [and produce] seeds which he gathers, he must make these separations from the seed.
Even though vegetables are used for human consumption, the obligation to tithe them is only Rabbinic in origin.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation to make these separations from other fruits, by contrast, is Scriptural in origin. See Halachah 1 and notes.</i> [The rationale is that] with regard to the tithes, [Deuteronomy 14:22] speaks of "the yield of your planting." Now the term <i>tevuah</i> [translated as "yield"] refers to grain and the like. Vegetables are not included as <i>tevuah</i>.<br>Similarly, it appears to me<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's use of this expression in this instance has raised questions. Generally, he employs this expression to refer to laws that he derives through his powers of deduction without a previous Rabbinic source. In this instance, however, this concept is explicitly stated in the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Challah</i> 4:4) and in the Babylonian Talmud (<i>Zevachim</i> 76a).</i> that this also applies with regard to <i>terumah</i>, for with regard to <i>terumah</i>, it is stated: "your grain, your wine, and your oil." Implied is that the obligation is applied to all species resembling those.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">And vegetables are not comparable to these species.</i> Instead, <i>terumah</i> which is separated from vegetables is a Rabbinic decree like their tithes.
We do not separate <i>terumah</i> and tithes from vegetables in the Diaspora, even in the places where we said that tithes should be separated,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 1, which states that tithes should be separated in Babylon, Ammon and Moab. Nevertheless, since there is no Scriptural obligation to tithe vegetable, our Sages did not impose such an obligation in the Diaspora. Thus even when vegetables from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> are exported to the Diaspora in contemporary times, there is no need to separate <i>terumah</i> and tithes.</i> Similarly, vegetables that come from the Diaspora to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, even though there is earth in their roots,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">And thus they will continue growing in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.</i> they are exempt<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">According to Scriptural Law. Even though there is an obligation on other produce as stated in Chapter 1, Halachah 22, our Sages did not extend this obligation to vegetables.</i> and nothing was decreed concerning them.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">By the Sages.</i><br>When grain or legumes are sown for their vegetables, the person's intent is not considered of consequence because of the prevailing conception of most people. [Hence,] their vegetables are exempt and [we are] obligated [to separate] <i>terumah</i> and tithes from their kernels.
Even though <i>tiltan</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">An herb identified as <i>chilbah</i>, a sharp and pungent herb used in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> today.</i> is not considered as fit for human consumption when it becomes hard,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">And thus there are certain laws (see Chapter 11, Halachah 9; Chapter 12, Halachah 7) that deal with chilbah as if it were not fit for human consumption.</i> since most people eat it at the beginning [of its development],<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">When it is still soft and edible.</i> [we are] obligated [to separate] <i>terumah</i> and tithes from it.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad takes issue with the Rambam, noting that in Halachah 4, he ruled that chilbah blossoms are exempt. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that there is no contradiction, for there is a difference between chilbah and chilbah blossoms.</i>
The following are exempt from <i>terumah</i> and tithes: <i>Leket, shichachah, pe'ah</i>, individual grapes that separate from a cluster, and underdeveloped grape clusters.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Hilchot Matanot Aniyim</i>, all of these are portions of one's crops that must be given to the poor. Since they are ownerless, they are exempt according to Scriptural Law, as indicated by Halachah 1.<br>The Rambam's statements are based on <i>Challah</i> 1:3. That mishnah does not, however, mention individual grapes that separate from a cluster and underdeveloped grape clusters. Nevertheless, since they are also presents given to the poor from our produce, the same laws apply to them.</i> This applies even if [a poor person] collects them in a grainheap.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Although the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Kilayim</i> 6:2) equates a granary with a grainheap, the Babylonian Talmud (<i>Berachot</i> 40b) mentions only a granary (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> If, however, he collects them in a granary in the field, it is established that there is an obligation for tithes upon them and <i>terumah</i> and tithes must be separated from them.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">As evident from the continuation of the Rambam's statements, the obligation is Rabbinic in origin, lest an observer think that the harvesting process will have been completed by the owner of the field without the agricultural obligations being met. See <i>Berachot, loc. cit.</i></i> If, however, he collects them in a granary in a town, they are exempt, for the matter is spoken about and everyone knows that [the produce] is <i>leket, shichachah,</i> and <i>pe'ah</i>.
One is obligated [to separate] <i>terumah</i> and tithes from <i>leket, shichachah,</i> and <i>pe'ah</i> [left] by a gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">A gentile is not required to leave these presents for the poor. Hence, if he does leave them, they are not given that status. Nevertheless, they are not considered as ownerless, but rather as a present given by the gentile to the poor (Radbaz). Hence, the produce is considered as produce from a gentile acquired by a Jew. If the Jew completes the work associated with the produce, <i>terumah</i> and the tithes must be separated from it [Chapter 1, Halachot 10-11; see also the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Pe'ah</i> 4:9)].</i> unless he declared them ownerless. Similarly, if grain and olives did not reach a third of their growth, they are exempt from <i>terumah</i> and the tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Because the produce is not considered significant until it reaches that size (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:28).</i><br>How can one know [whether produce has reached a third of its growth]? When [the kernels] would grow if they were sown,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ma'aserot</i> 1:3).</i> it is known that it has reached a third of its growth. If a person transgressed and separated [<i>terumah</i>] from grain and olives before they reached a third of their growth, [the separated produce] is not <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If a separation was made from produce that was exempt for produce that was liable, a new separation must be made and none of the prohibitions applying to <i>terumah</i> are associated with the produce separated originally.</i>
Similarly, produce that is declared ownerless is exempt from <i>terumah</i> and tithes. [This applies also to produce] declared ownerless by a gentile.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Although declaring property ownerless is in certain matters equivalent to taking a vow (<i>Hilchot Nedarim</i> 2:14) and the laws of vows do not apply to a gentile, when a gentile declares property ownerless, his declaration is effective. This concept is not accepted by all authorities and some even suggest alternate versions of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. Nevertheless, it is borne out by the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Pe'ah, loc. cit.</i>).</i> Nevertheless, if one planted [crops] on a field that was declared ownerless, he is obligated [to separate] <i>terumah</i> and tithes [from the crops].<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">For the crops themselves are not ownerless (Radbaz).</i>
When a person declares standing grain ownerless, takes possession of it, and then transgresses and separates <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">He is considered to have transgressed, because, according to law, there is no obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from such produce. Even though the original owner himself took possession of the field, there is no difference between it and other ownerless crops. See <i>Hilchot Matanot Aniyim</i> 5:27.</i> the laws of <i>terumah</i> apply to the produce separated.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since while the grain is standing, separating a portion as <i>terumah</i> would not be effective, if it was declared ownerless at that time, that declaration does not prevent the separation of <i>terumah</i> afterwards from being effective [Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Ma'aserot</i> 1:1)].</i> If, however, he declared sheaves [of grain] ownerless, takes possession of them, and transgresses and separates <i>terumah</i>, the laws of <i>terumah</i> do not apply to the produce separated.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>terumah</i> separated from sheaves is effective (even though all the work associated with preparing crops is not completed), if the sheaves are declared ownerless, that declaration prevents the separation of <i>terumah</i> from being effective (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> Similarly, whenever a person separates [<i>terumah</i>] from produce from which <i>terumah</i> is not obligated to be separated, the laws of <i>terumah</i> do not apply to the produce separated.<br>Similarly, with regard to tithes, produce which the majority of people do not ordinarily sow in their gardens and fields, but instead, can be assumed to have grown ownerless are exempt from <i>terumah</i> and tithes. [This includes] garlic that makes one cry, onions of Rikpah,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ma'aserot</i> 5:7) where he explains that these species of garlic and onion are so pungent that they cause people to cry. He does, however, allow for the possibility that the Hebrew terms refer to names of places.</i> a pearled Cilcilian bean, Egyptian lentils,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">These types of beans and lentils are of abnormal shape and grow wild (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> and the like.
[The following laws apply if] produce from which we are obligated to separate <i>terumah</i> becomes mixed with produce which is exempt,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">And a separation between the two cannot be made.</i> e.g., olives gathered by the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">From which <i>terumah</i> need not be separated.</i> become mixed with olives reaped [by the owner], underdeveloped grape clusters<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">These types of beans and lentils are of abnormal shape and grow wild (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> become mixed with grapes that were harvested. If the person has other produce,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">From which he is obligated to separate <i>terumah</i>, but from which he did not separate it as of yet.</i> he separates [<i>terumah</i> from it] for the produce upon which the obligation lies according to the appropriate reckoning.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if five pounds of grapes from which there was an obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> become mixed with other grapes, we separate an amount of <i>terumah</i> sufficient for five pounds of grapes from an additional source of grapes. With regard to the measure of <i>terumah</i> appropriate to give, see the following chapter.</i> If this is all the produce the person has, he should separate <i>terumah</i> and <i>terumat ma'aser</i> for the entire mixture,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This is speaking about an instance where wine was made from the grapes and oil from the olives. Hence every drop contains a mixture of produce from which <i>terumah</i> is obligated to be separated and produce upon which there is no obligation.</i> as if there was an obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from the entire amount.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot merely separate the appropriate amount from the mixture, because one may not separate <i>terumah</i> (or tithes) from produce upon which there is no obligation for produce upon which there is an obligation. Since in every drop that he separates, there is a portion upon which there is no obligation, that separation is not effective (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:28). As the Rambam writes in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Challah</i> 3:9), one must be stringent and separate <i>terumah</i> and <i>terumat ma'aser</i> for the entire amount, because one is liable for death at the hand of heaven for partaking of produce from which these allocations were not made properly.<br>In that source, the Rambam adds that the owner must buy the olives or the grapes to be given to the poor from them before he takes possession of the entire mixture.</i> He should separate the first and second tithes for the produce upon which the obligation lies according to the appropriate reckoning.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:29 and the <i>Turei Zahav</i> 331:7 explain that the intent is that one should separate the tithes for the entire amount, but give the Levite only the tithe for the amount upon which the obligation originally lay. In this manner, he will not have separated the tithes from produce upon which there is no obligation and yet will not have given the Levite more than would have had to.</i>
<i>Terumah</i> [must be given] to a priest whether it is in a state of ritual purity or not. Even if all the grain or the wine became impure before [<i>terumah</i>] was separated, he is obligated to separate the <i>terumah</i> that is impure and give it to a priest, as [Numbers 18:8] states: "And behold I have given you the watch over My <i>terumah</i>,"<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In the verse, the noun <i>terumah</i> uses a plural form alluding to two types of <i>terumah</i>: pure <i>terumah</i> and impure <i>terumah</i>. See also <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 6:2.</i> i.e., whether it is pure or impure. The pure [<i>terumah</i>] may be eaten by the priests and they can benefit from the impure [<i>terumah</i>] by burning it.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to partake of it.</i> If it is oil, it can be kindled [as fuel for a lamp]. If it is grain or the like, it can be used as fuel for an oven.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">It may not, however, be given to animals as animal fodder. See <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 90) which states that it is a mitzvah to burn impure <i>terumah</i>. Impure <i>terumah</i> from fruits that will not be useful as fuel must be buried.</i>
Similarly, if <i>terumat ma'aser</i> became impure - or if the tithes became impure, he must separate [<i>terumat ma'aser</i>] in impurity and give it to a priest to benefit from by using it as fuel.
Anyone who separates the great <i>terumah</i> or <i>terumat ma'aser</i> should recite a blessing before separating it, just as one recites a blessing over the observance of all the mitzvot, as we explained in [<i>Hilchot] Berachot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 11:2, 6, 12, a blessing should be recited <i>before</i> the observance of every positive commandment.</i>
<i>Terumah</i> - even impure <i>terumah</i> - should not be taken from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> to the Diaspora.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>terumah</i> is pure, it should not be taken to the Diaspora, because the very earth of the Diaspora is considered as impure by Rabbinic decree (see <i>Hilchot Tuma'at Meit</i>, ch. 11) and the <i>terumah</i> will become impure. Even if the <i>terumah</i> was already impure, it should be burnt in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. See <i>Sh'vi'it</i> 6:5.</i> We should not bring <i>terumah</i> from the Diaspora to <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Lest the priests seek to leave <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> to collect it [Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Sh'vi'it</i> 6:6)].</i> If it was brought [from the Diaspora], it should not be eaten, because it is impure because [of contact with] the earth of the nations.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., by Rabbinic decree.</i> It should not be burnt, lest people say: "We saw <i>terumah</i> that did not become impure being burnt." It should not be returned to the Diaspora, lest people say: "<i>Terumah</i> may be taken [from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>] to the Diaspora." Instead, we leave it until it becomes impure because of a known source of impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., it is forbidden in the Diaspora, because of the doubt that perhaps it came into contact with a corpse. Nevertheless, we do not burn it until we know certainly that it became impure.</i> or until the day before Pesach if it was leaven,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">For all leaven must be burnt at this time.</i> and then it will be burnt.

Chapter 3

There is no minimum requirement for the great <i>terumah</i> according to Scriptural Law, as [implied by Deuteronomy 18:4]: "The first of your grain," i.e., even the slightest amount. Even one kernel of grain fulfills the requirement for the entire grain heap. Idealy, one should separate only according to the measures specified by our Sages. In the present age, when [the <i>terumah</i>] will be burnt because of impurity, a person may separate even the smallest amount as an initial and prefatory measure.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since the produce will ultimately be burnt, there is no purpose in setting aside more than the very minimum. The Radbaz notes that the priests are also given the opportunity to benefit from impure <i>terumah</i>, because they use it as fuel. Why then shouldn't they be given a full measure of impure <i>terumah</i>? He explains that since this is not the primary use of <i>terumah</i>, our Sages did not feel that for the minimal benefit the priest receives from burning the <i>terumah</i>, it was necessary to require the people to separate a larger amount.</i>
What is the measure that our Sages established? A generous measure is one fourtieth [of the crop]. The average [measure] is one fiftieth and a parsimonious measure is one sixtieth.
Idealy, we may separate one sixtieth [of the crop] as <i>terumah</i> from species to which the priests do not pay attention, e.g., carobs<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 1:3, there are certain species of carobs which are generally not eaten by humans. There is no obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from them according to Scriptural Law. Here we are speaking about carobs from which one is obligated to separate <i>terumah</i> according to Scriptural Law. Nevertheless, since they are not considered important by the priests, there is no need to separate more than the minimal amount.</i> and <i>kilisin</i> beans.<br>[Similarly,] we [may separate] one sixtieth [of the crop as <i>terumah</i>] in the following situations: <i>terumah</i> separated from produce grown from <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Chapter 11, Halachot 21 and 27, when produce that is <i>terumah</i> is sown, according to Scriptural Law, the produce which grows is ordinary produce and <i>terumah</i> and tithes must be separated from it. Nevertheless, our Sages were stringent and declared that the entire crop should be considered as <i>terumah</i> and sold to the priests (at a price substantially less than that of ordinary produce). Since the owners are required to consider the entire crop as <i>terumah</i>, our Sages did not require them to separate more than a sixtieth to be given as <i>terumah</i> (<i>Tosefta, Terumot</i>, ch. 5).</i> <i>terumah</i> that is mixed [with <i>tevel</i>],<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tevel</i> is produce from which <i>terumah</i> and tithes were not separated. As indicated by Chapter 13, Halachah 1, if there are more than 100 times the amount of <i>tevel</i> as <i>terumah,</i> it is necessary to remove the amount of <i>terumah</i> that became mixed with the <i>tevel</i> and then separate the <i>terumah</i>. Since the person is already separating the amount of <i>terumah</i> from the mixture, our Sages did not require him to separate a large quantity of <i>terumah</i> from the <i>tevel</i>. As noted in Chapter 13, Halachah 2, more stringent laws apply when <i>terumah</i> becomes mixed with less than 100 times the amount of other produce.</i> or impure <i>terumah</i> that became impure against one's will or inadvertently,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz questions: Why is it not sufficient to separate the slightest amount as <i>terumah</i> as is the practice in the present age (Halachah 1)? He explains that in the present age, all <i>terumah</i> will become ritually impure. Hence, there is no need to give a significant amount at all. In the time of the Talmud, by contrast, significant amounts of <i>terumah</i> that is pure must be separated. If an inconsequential amount of impure <i>terumah</i> would be separated, people might refrain from giving an appropriate amount of pure <i>terumah</i>.</i> <i>terumah</i> taken from consecrated crops,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., crops that were consecrated after the work involving their harvest was completed (at which time the obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> became incumbent upon them), but from which <i>terumah</i> was not actually separated. There is no need to separate <i>terumah</i> from crops that grew in a field belonging to the Temple treasury.</i> <i>terumah</i> [separated in] the Diaspora, <i>terumah</i> from <i>ketzach, kilisin,</i> carobs,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Note, however, <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 1:3.</i> poor quality wild figs, red barley kernels, and the like,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., all of these species are types of produce that are not usually considered valuable.</i> and produce grown in a flower-pot that does not have a hole.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This leniency is granted for the obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from such produce is merely Rabbinic in origin.
