Hence it is sufficient to separate one-sixtieth as an initial measure.</i><br>Similarly, when guardians separate [<i>terumah</i>] from the produce of orphans, they should separate one sixtieth.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Because special consideration is given to the orphans' property.</i>
One should not<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 1:7), Rambam states that separating <i>terumah</i> in this manner is "forbidden." The commentaries question whether his use of different wording in this halachah indicates a change of position or not.</i> separate this <i>terumah</i> with a measure, a scale, or by number, because [the Torah] did not specify a measure for this [allocation]. Instead, one makes an estimation and separates one fiftieth<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is the average measure. Our translation follows authoritative manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard printed text reads "one sixtieth."</i> according to his conception.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Terumah</i> 1:7) derives this from Numbers 18:27: "And your <i>terumah</i> will be considered for you...." <i>Venechshav</i> translated as "considered" shares the root <i>chashav</i> meaning "thought" or "estimate." Implied is that <i>terumah</i> should be given by estimation. Although this verse is speaking about <i>terumat ma'aser</i> which must be separated by exact measure (see Halachah 10), we follow the principle (see Rashi, <i>Pesachim</i> 24a): If a concept explicitly stated with regard to <i>terumat ma'aser</i> cannot be applied to it, we assume that it is referring to the great <i>terumah</i> [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>)].</i> One may, however, separate <i>terumah</i> from [produce that is] measured, weighed, or counted.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one measures the produce and then separates <i>terumah</i> by estimation (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> He should not separate <i>terumah</i> using a basket or a container whose measure is known.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">For that is obviously equivalent to separating with a measure.</i> He may, however, separate <i>terumah</i> by [filling] half or a third of their measure. One should not separate <i>terumah</i> [by using] half [a measure of] a <i>se'ah</i>, because its half is also a measure.
When a person separates a large measure of <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a measure above that stated by the Sages.</i> as long as he leaves a certain portion as ordinary produce,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he did not designate the entire crop as <i>terumah</i>.</i> [the separated portion] is <i>terumah</i>. If, however, he said: "All of this produce is <i>terumah</i>," his statements are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">For Numbers 15:21 speaks of giving "from the first of your kneading." Implied is that one must give "from the first," but not all the first [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 4:5, based on the Jerusalem Talmud)].</i><br>If a person intended to separate one tenth [of his crop] as <i>terumah</i>, but it happened that he actually set aside one sixtieth, [the separated portion] is <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">For he had intended to give this amount of produce - and more - as <i>terumah</i> (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). And when giving <i>terumah</i>, one's deed must be aligned - at least partially - with his intent. Since the <i>terumah</i> is acceptable, there is no need for the person to supplement the amount separated and give the amount he originally intended (Radbaz).</i> If, however, he intended to give the sixtieth as <i>terumah</i>, but it happened that he actually set aside one fiftieth, [the separated portion] is not <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For he had not intended to give this amount as <i>terumah</i> (<i>ibid.</i>).</i>
When a person sets aside <i>terumah</i> and it happened that he gave one twentieth [of his crop],<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., without either desiring to give this amount or having a desire to give a lesser amount. Note the gloss of the Radbaz who suggests that there is a printing error in the text.</i> [the separated portion] is <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that he gave a larger measure than usual as <i>terumah</i> does not disqualify his gift.</i> If he set aside <i>terumah</i> and it happened that he gave one sixtieth [of his crop] and hence, he added more produce for the sake of <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">So that he would not be giving a parsimonious measure.</i> the tithes must be separated from that additional amount.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Although normally, there is no obligation to separate tithes from <i>terumah</i>, since the portion he separated originally fulfilled his obligation, it immediately became necessary to separate tithes from the remainder of the produce.</i> The priest must separate them and only then may he partake of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">He must observe all the obligations required when partaking of <i>terumah</i>.</i><br>If a person set aside <i>terumah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">With the intent of giving the measure required by our Sages.</i> and it happened that he gave one sixty-first [of his crop], [the separated portion] is <i>terumah</i>, but he must set aside a second portion to complete the measure that he originally intended to give. This additional measure may be set aside with a measure, a scale, or by number.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Although initially, one should not give a measured amount of <i>terumah</i> as stated in Halachah 4, in this instance, he is required to give a measured amount so that he will give the amount that he originally intended.</i> He should set aside [the <i>terumah</i>] only from the produce that is encompassed [in the same area] as is true with regard to the initial separation [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">As is required by Halachah 17, as an initial and preferable measure.</i>
When a person separates a portion of the <i>terumah</i> [that he originally intended to separate], that portion does not have the status of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the situation described in the previous halachah, in this instance, the person originally intended to separate more. Hence, what he separated is not considered as <i>terumah</i>.</i> Nevertheless, the <i>terumah</i> from that portion<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since the separation was not effective, that portion is considered as <i>tevel</i> and <i>terumah</i> must be separated from it. Since it was set aside as a separate portion, the <i>terumah</i> should be separated from it and not from other produce. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 4:1).<br>The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's interpretation of that mishnah and maintains that the separated produce is in fact <i>terumah</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> justifies the Rambam's interpretation.</i> should be separated from it and not from other produce.
When a person separates a portion of <i>terumah</i> from one grain heap and a portion of <i>terumah</i> from a second grain heap of the same species, he should not separate <i>terumah</i> from one grain heap for the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For as mentioned above and in Halachah 17, preferably, one should separate <i>terumah</i> from the grain for which it is being separated and not from other grain.</i><br>[The following rules apply when a person] says: "The <i>terumah</i> for this grain heap is in its midst." If he specified a place - on the north side or the south - he has designated <i>terumah</i> and is obligated to separate <i>terumah</i> from that [portion of the grain heap]. If he did not specify a place, his statements are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">And he may separate <i>terumah</i> from other produce. The rationale is that <i>terumah</i> is called "the first." Implied is that it must be set aside from produce that is not "first" and in this instance, that has not been done (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:45).</i>
If [a person separating <i>terumah</i>] said: "The <i>terumah</i> for this grain heap is this<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he designated a certain portion of the first grain heap as <i>terumah</i>.</i> and the <i>terumah</i> for this grain heap is like that one,"<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the <i>terumah</i> for the second grain heap should be separated in a way entirely parallel to the separation from the first. It should be taken from an equivalent place and have the measure. Our translation is based on the version of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> possessed by the Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and their interpretations of that text. There are other slightly different versions of the text.</i> at the place where the <i>terumah</i> for the first grain heap ended, the <i>terumah</i> for the second grain heap also ended.
<i>Terumat Ma'aser</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 12, after a tenth of the produce is given to the Levites, they must separate a tenth of that tenth and give it to the priests as <i>terumat ma'aser</i>.</i> may not be separated by estimation.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in contrast to "the great <i>terumah</i>," as mentioned in Halachah 4.<br>As stated in the notes to that halachah, a verse ostensibly referring to <i>terumat ma'aser</i> (Numbers 18:27  states: "And your <i>terumah</i> will be considered for you...." <i>Venechshav</i> translated as "considered" shares the root <i>chashav</i> meaning "thought" or "estimate." Implied is that <i>terumah</i> should be given by estimation. Nevertheless, according to the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Terumah</i> 1:7), that verse refers to the great <i>terumah</i> and not to <i>terumat ma'aser</i> (Radbaz).</i> [Instead,] one must be precise in its measure, even in the present age,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">When it will be destroyed because of impurity.</i> because its measure is explicitly stated in the Torah.
[Produce] that is usually measured in volume, should be measured in volume. [Produce] that is weighed should be weighed and if it can [easily] be counted, it should be counted. If it could [easily] be counted, weighed, or measured in volume, it is praiseworthy to count it,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., separating the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> after counting it is praiseworthy [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 4:6)].</i> more praiseworthy to measure its volume, and most praiseworthy to weigh it.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">For in this way, the measurement is most precise.</i>
The mitzvah [to separate] <i>terumat ma'aser</i> is for a Levite to separate it from the tithe [given] him, as [Numbers 18:26] states: "When you take the tithe from the children of Israel...."<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 129) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 396) include this commandment among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> An Israelite may separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i> and give it to a priest<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the Torah's requirement is for the Israelite to give the tithes to the Levite and for the Levite to separate one tenth of the tithes as <i>terumat ma'aser</i>. Nevertheless, if the Israelite desires to fulfill this mitzvah, he has the option. It is not considered as if he is usurping the Levite's right.<br>The Radbaz notes that the source for this concept (<i>Gittin</i> 30b) also implies that <i>terumat ma'aser</i> can be separated by estimation and finds it somewhat problematic to derive one concept from that source, but not the second. Nevertheless, he concludes that this is in fact the Talmud's conclusion.</i> and then give the tithes to a Levite after separating the <i>terumat [ma'aser]</i> from it, i.e., a tenth of a tenth.
When an Israelite separates the first tithe while the crop is still stalks [of grain] and gives it to the Levite before it was threshed and before he separated the great <i>terumah</i>, the Levite is not required to separate the great <i>terumah</i> from it after he threshes it, only <i>terumat ma'aser</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Berachot</i> 47a derives this concept from the exegesis of Numbers 18:26 which speaks of taking "a tenth from the tithe." Implied is that only a tenth is separated from the tithe and nothing more.</i><br>If, however, the Israelite threshed [the crop]<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">And completed all the other tasks to make it obligated for tithing.</i> and separated a tithe of the grain and gave it to the Levite before he separated the great <i>terumah</i>, the Levite is obligated to separate the great <i>terumah</i> and <i>terumat ma'aser</i>, since [the crop] has already been [separated into kernels of] grain, the obligation [to separate] <i>terumah</i> has fallen upon it, as [implied by the phrase]: "The first of your grain."<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., once produce has been reduced to kernels of grain, the obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> takes effect. Hence, the Levite must separate <i>terumah</i> from the tithe as well as <i>terumat ma'aser</i>.</i>
When a Levite took grain while it was in stalks, he should not give the <i>terumah</i> to the priest in stalks. Instead, we penalize him [and require him] to thresh [the crop], winnow it, and give the priest a tenth of a tenth of the kernels of the grain.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he is penalized by being required to perform the work necessary to process the stalks of grain until they are reduced to kernels.</i> He is not required to give him a tenth of the straw or the chaff.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">For the obligation to tithe is incumbent only on the food and not on the other parts of the plant.</i> If he set aside <i>terumat ma'aser</i> while the crop was in stalks as it was given to him, he must crush it and give the priest the kernels and the straw.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He is required to perform the tasks necessary to process the grain for the reasons mentioned above. Nevertheless, he must give the priest the chaff because he designated it as <i>terumah</i> while it was in its stalks. That sacred designation does not depart from the chaff when the kernels are separated from it.</i><br>Why did they penalize him and make him crush it? Because he accepted the tithe while it was in the stalks and thus precluded the separation of the great <i>terumah</i>.
When an Israelite tells a Levite: "My father told me: 'I have <i>ma'aser</i> for you in my possession,' we are not concerned about [the possibility of] it containing <i>terumat ma'aser</i>. [We are confident] that his father separated the <i>terumat ma'aser</i>. Therefore he commanded him that the [entire] tithe should be given to the Levite.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., his statements indicate that his father desired that the entire tithe be given to the Levite. Hence, we assume that he already separated the <i>terumat ma'aser</i>.</i><br>If [the Israelite] told [the Levite]: "My father told me: 'I have a <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The mention of a measure indicates that he expects the Levite to separate the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> required for this measure.<br>The Ra'avad challenges the Rambam's ruling, noting that it is the opposite of the standard text of the Talmud (<i>Gittin</i> 30b). The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> state that the version of the Talmud possessed by the Rambam differed from the standard version and indeed, possesses an advantage over it.</i> of <i>ma'aser</i> for you in my possession,' we are concerned about [the possibility of] it containing <i>terumat ma'aser</i>.
<i>Terumat ma'aser</i> that is an eighth of an eighth [of a <i>log</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">This measure, a <i>kortov</i>, is slightly less than 5 cc. according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i> and approximately 9.5 cc. according to <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> should be brought to a priest. For less than that, [we do not trouble him] to bring it to a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Because such a small amount is of little value.<br>On the basis of the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Terumot</i> 11:4), the Ra'avad questions the Rambam's ruling, maintaining that even the slightest amount of pure grain should also be brought to a priest if there was a definite obligation to tithe. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> acknowledge the validity of Ra'avad's questions, but offer possible resolutions for the Rambam's approach.</i> Instead, he may throw it into the fire and burn it.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">He must, however, separate it. Otherwise, it is forbidden to benefit from the tithes.</i> With regard to wine and oil, by contrast, even the smallest amount [of <i>terumat ma'aser</i>] should be brought to a priest<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For even a small amount of these substances is of value for a priest (Radbaz).</i> provided it is definitely <i>terumat ma'aser</i> and ritually pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">If it was not ritually pure, it would be of little benefit to the priest, for he could use it only as fuel. Hence we do not require that it be brought to him.</i> If it was ritually impure or if it was <i>demai</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Produce from which we are uncertain whether the tithes had been separated and hence, require that the tithes be separated again. Since the tithes are being separated merely as a safeguard and thus it is possible that there is no Scriptural obligation from this crop, we do not trouble him to bring a priest a very small amount.</i> if it does not contain [the above] measure, he is not required to trouble himself with it. Instead, he should burn it.
We should separate the great <i>terumah</i> only from [produce] in the same location.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is taken from the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Challah</i> 1:9). Note the contrast to <i>terumat ma'aser</i> as stated in Halachah 20.</i> What is implied? A person had 50 <i>se'ah</i> in one room<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">We have chosen this translation for <i>bayit</i> based on the gloss of the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> to Halachah 18 where he explains that when one grain heap is in a room and another in the loft of the same building, they are considered as being in the same place. This interpretation enables us to understand an otherwise difficult passage in the <i>Tosefta, Terumot</i> 3:9.</i> and 50 <i>se'ah</i> in another,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Everything that is in the same room is considered as being in the same location even if the two piles of produce are not touching each other (Radbaz; see the following halachah).</i> he should not separate two <i>se'ah</i> from one room for the entire 100 <i>se'ah</i>, for he would be separating produce from one location for produce from another.<br>[After the fact,] if one separated <i>terumah</i> from one location for produce in another, the separation is effective,<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:49 explains the rationale for the above restriction and why it is not binding after the fact. As mentioned above, according to Rabbinic Law, one must separate between one fortieth and one sixtieth of the crop as <i>terumah</i> and that separation must be made by estimation. Hence, in order to make an appropriate estimation, one must be in the same place as the produce. Nevertheless, since according to Scriptural Law, one fulfills his obligation with even one grain, after the fact, such a separation is not disqualified.</i> 
 provided the produce separated [as <i>terumah</i>] is protected. If, however, a person possessed jugs of oil and/or wine and saw that they were breaking, should he say: "These are <i>terumah</i> for the produce in my home,"<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">And thus try to receive something of value for the wine and/or oil that will be spilled.</i> his words are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since the jugs are breaking, we considered them as having been broken already and the produce as having been spilled. When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:25) states that in the present age, when <i>terumah</i> is destroyed regardless because of its impurity, even when the jugs are breaking, the separation is effective.</i>
When produce is scattered in a room or there are two granaries in the same room,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is taken from the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Challah</i> 1:9). Note the contrast to <i>terumat ma'aser</i> as stated in Halachah 20.</i> one may separate <i>terumah</i> from one for the entire amount.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are in the same room, they are considered as being in the same place.</i> When sacks of grain, spheres of dried figs, or jugs of figs are in the same place, one may separate from one for the entire amount. With regard to jugs of wine, until one has sealed their lids closed, he may separate <i>terumah</i> from one for the entire amount.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">It is considered as if they were all mixed together [see the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ma'aser Sheni</i> 3:12)].</i> After they have been sealed, he must separate <i>terumah</i> from each one individually.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Each closed jug is considered as a separate entity, even though they are all in the same room.</i>
When a person was gathering bunches of vegetables and leaving them in [his] garden, he may separate <i>terumah</i> from one for the entire amount.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">For the entire garden is considered as a single entity.</i> If he brings another type of produce between them, he must separate <i>terumah</i> from each one individually. If he brought several species together in a container, e.g., cabbage on top, cabbage on bottom, and another species in between, he should not separate <i>terumah</i> from the top head for the bottom head.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are other species between them, they are not considered as of the same group.</i><br>If he collected five piles in one granary, he may separate <i>terumah</i> from one for the entire amount<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that they are in the same granary causes them to be considered as a single entity.</i> provided the fundamental collection is intact.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., provided he has not begun removing produce from the granary.</i> If the fundamental collection is not intact, he must separate <i>terumah</i> from each one individually.
<i>Terumat ma'aser</i> may be separated even if [the produce] is not in the same location. [This is implied by Numbers 18:28]: Separate <i>terumah</i> "from all of your tithes."<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is not quoting the verse as it appears in the Torah.</i> [Implied is that] even if there is one [collection of] tithes in one city and another [collection of] tithes in another city, one may separate <i>terumat [ma'aser]</i> once for all of them. Torah scholars separate even <i>terumat ma'aser</i> only from [produce] in one location.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:48 describes this as the most choice way of observing the mitzvah. The Radbaz emphasizes that this is a stringency that Torah scholars have accepted upon themselves and, according to the Rambam, is not even a Rabbinic obligation. The Ra'avad explains that an Israelite need not separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i> from produce that is in one place, but a Levite must. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> does not accept that position and quotes sources in support of the Rambam's view.</i>
When a Levite possessed [produce from] the first tithe from which <i>terumat ma'aser</i> was not separated and he left it so that he could continuously separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i> [for other crops] from it while it is still <i>tevel</i>, his actions are viable. [This is derived from Numbers 18:24]: "For the tithes of the children of Israel [from] which they will separate <i>terumah</i>...." Implied is that [the tithes] can be transformed into <i>terumat [ma'aser]</i> in their entirety.
If one separated <i>terumat ma'aser</i> first and then left [the produce] so that one could continually separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i> for other produce from it until it becomes <i>terumat ma'aser</i> in its entirety, at which he would give it to a priest, his actions are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">And it is as if <i>terumat ma'aser</i> has never been separated for the produce for which he intended that it serve that purpose.</i> [This is derived from <i>ibid.</i>:29]: "The sacred portion from it." Implied is that when its sacred portion is in it,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., <i>terumat ma'aser</i> has not yet been separated.</i> it can be made <i>terumat [ma'aser]</i> for other [crops]. If its sacred portion is no longer in it, it cannot be made <i>terumat [ma'aser]</i> for other [crops]. Similarly, when a person sets aside crops to separate the great <i>terumah</i> from them, they must be <i>tevel</i> with regard to <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., <i>terumah</i> has never been separated from them. See Chapter 5, Halachah 12.</i> If a person sets aside crops to separate the tithes from them, they must be <i>tevel</i> with regard to the tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the tithes have never been separated from them. See <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 1:7.</i>
When <i>terumah</i> and the tithes are separated, we separate them in proper sequence.<br>What is implied? Before everything, one separates <i>bikkurim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The first fruits.</i> Afterwards, [he separates] the great <i>terumah</i>, the first tithe, then the second tithe or the tithe for the poor.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., either one or the other. For in the year the second tithe is separated, the tithe for the poor is not separated.</i> If a person separated the second [tithe] before the first, or the tithes before <i>terumah</i>, or <i>terumah</i> before <i>bikkurim</i>, his actions are effective despite the fact that he has transgressed a negative commandment.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 154) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 72) include this commandment among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i><br>What is the source which teaches that a negative commandment is involved? [Exodus 22:28] states: "Do not delay your fullness offering or your priestly heave-offering."<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">"Your fullness offering" refers to <i>bikkurim</i> for they are separated when the produce becomes full. "Your priestly heave-offering" refers to <i>terumah</i>. Thus the <i>bikkurim</i> precede <i>terumah</i>. See also <i>Terumot</i> 3:6-7.</i> [Implied is a command] not to delay [the separation of produce] that should come first. Lashes are not given [as punishment for the violation] of this prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Temurah</i> 4b explains that this prohibition is considered as correctable by the fulfillment of a positive commandment, for one may make separations in the proper order. See the gloss of Radbaz.</i>
When a person desires to separate the great <i>terumah</i> and <i>terumat ma'aser</i> at the same time, he should separate 1/33.33 of his crop.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., three hundredths of his crop. An average donation of <i>terumah</i> is one fifitieth, two hundredths. And <i>terumat ma'aser</i> is one tenth of the first tithe, another hundredth. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Demai</i> 5:2).</i> He should say: "One hundredth of [the produce] that is here - i.e., on the side of the produce which I set aside - is ordinary produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the portion that will be separated as <i>terumat ma'aser</i>. Initially, it is to remain as ordinary produce, so that the prohibition against making the separations in improper sequence is not violated. See Ra'avad.</i> The remainder of what I separated is <i>terumah</i> for the entire crop. The tithes which are necessary to be separated for these hundred portions<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., including that which was separated as <i>terumah</i>.</i> of ordinary produce are at the side of [the produce] that I separated. The remainder of what I separated aside from the <i>terumah</i> is <i>terumat ma'aser</i> on the entire amount."<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">In this way, he will have separated <i>terumah</i>, the tithes, and <i>terumat ma'aser</i> in proper sequence.</i>

Chapter 4

A person may appoint an agent to separate <i>terumah</i> and the tithes for him, as [Numbers 18:28] states: "So shall you separate, also you." [The wording implies] the inclusion of an agent.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">By saying "also you," the verse implies that there is someone other than you - an agent - that may also separate <i>terumah</i> [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumah</i> 1:1)].</i> A gentile may not be appointed as an agent, because [the phrase] "also you" [implies an equation between you and your agent]. Just as you are a member of the covenant, your agent must be a member of the covenant.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle applying not only with regard to <i>terumah</i>. A gentile cannot serve as an agent for a Jew in any matter (<i>Kiddushin</i> 41b).</i>
There are five [types of individuals] who should not separate <i>terumah</i> and [even] if they do, [the produce] they separated is not considered as <i>terumah</i>: A deaf-mute,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam explains in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumah</i> 1:2) although the term <i>cheresh</i> refers to someone who cannot hear, in a halachic context, the intent is someone who also cannot speak. He is referred to as a <i>cheresh</i> because it is his inability to hear that prevents him from learning how to speak.</i> a mentally or emotionally unstable person, a minor,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">These three individuals are considered as incapable of controlling their affairs. Hence they are not obligated in the observance of the Torah and its mitzvot. Just as they are not responsible for themselves, they cannot discharge the responsibilities of others.</i> a gentile who separated <i>terumah</i> from produce belonging to a Jew, even with his permission,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For as stated above, a gentile cannot act as an agent for a Jew.</i> and a person who separates <i>terumah</i> from produce that does not belong to him without the owner's permission.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If he is granted the owner's permission, he is considered as his agent and the separation is effective as stated above.</i> If, however, a person separates <i>terumah</i> from his own produce for the produce of others, the produce is <i>terumah</i> and [the colleague's] produce has been prepared for use. The satisfaction [of allocating the <i>terumah</i>] belongs [to the person who separated it and he may] give to whichever priest he desires.
[The following rules apply when a person] separates <i>terumah</i> [from a colleague's produce] without permission or descends into his colleague's field and gathered produce without permission so that he could take them [for himself], but separate some as <i>terumah</i>. When the owner comes and says: "You should have taken better ones,"<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on Rashi's commentary (<i>Bava Metzia</i> 22a).</i> if there are better ones than those separated as <i>terumah,</i> the separation is effective,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">According to this approach, the statement "You should have taken better ones" is understood simply. The owner in truth desired that the person give the priest the best produce possible as <i>terumah</i>.</i> because [the owner] did not object.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Thus we consider it as if the person acted with the owner's consent and thus functioned as his agent. Since a mitzvah is involved and ultimately, he consented, we assume that this was his intent from the outset (Radbaz, <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). Also, implied is that the owner was willing to allow the person to take a certain amount of the produce as his own. Note the <i>Turei Zahav</i> 331:15 who maintains that the Rambam's ruling runs contrary to the simple meaning of the passage in <i>Bava Metzia, loc. cit.</i>, and rejects the resolutions offered by the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>. Note, also, the contrast to <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 5:8.</i> If there are not better ones, his separation is not effective, for his statements were made as an objection.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we assume that he was speaking facetiously.</i> If the owner came and gathered produce and added it to the quantity separated,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">We interpret that as an expression of consent to the other person's actions.</i> whether he possesses better produce or not, his separation is effective.
There are five<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumah</i> 1:4 also mentions a person who had a seminal emission. He is prohibited, because he is not allowed to recite a blessing until he immerses himself in a <i>mikveh</i>. The Rambam does not mention such a person here, because - as stated in <i>Hilchot K'riat Shema</i> 4:8; <i>Hilchot Tefilah</i> 4:4-6 - Ezra's decree restricting a person who had a seminal emission from prayer and Torah study was never fully accepted and ultimately, nullified by the Rabbis. In the present age, such a person should recite blessings even though he has not yet immersed.</i> who should not separate <i>terumah</i>, but if they do, their separation is effective:<br>a person who is deaf, but not mute, because he cannot hear the blessing,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This category is not mentioned in the mishnah, <i>loc. cit.</i> The Rambam mentions it, based on the principle (see <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 1:7) that a person who recites a blessing should recite it in a manner that enables him to hear it.</i> a mute who can hear, but not speak and a person who is naked, because they cannot recite the blessing,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">A person who cannot speak is physically incapable of reciting the blessing. A person who is naked is forbidden to recite the blessing, because his personal state is not considered as appropriate for the recitation of a blessing (see <i>Shabbat</i> 23b).</i> and a person who is drunk<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This is speaking about a person who is slightly intoxicated, but still able to function. If a person is intoxicated to the extent that is not at all in command of his mental faculties, he is considered as one who is intellectually incapable and his separation is of no consequence whatsoever. See <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 29:18.</i> and a blind person, because they cannot make distinctions and separate the most attractive portion [as <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">And as an initial and preferable measure, the finest portion of the produce should be separated as <i>terumah</i> (see Chapter 5, Halachah 1). When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:32) states that in the present age, when the <i>terumah</i> is ultimately destroyed, we are not precise in separating the finest portion of the crops as <i>terumah</i>. Hence, it is also acceptable if individuals in the latter two categories also separate <i>terumah</i>.</i>
When a minor reaches the age when his vows may be of consequence<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a male minor who is twelve years old and a female minor who is eleven years old, provided they know the significance of vows, or a male of thirteen and a female of twelve who have not manifested signs of physical maturity, .</i> - even though he has not manifested signs of physical maturity and he has not attained majority - separates <i>terumah</i>, his separation is of consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In one of his responsa, however, the Rambam writes that as an initial preference, it is not desirable for a minor to separate <i>terumah</i> until he manifests signs of physical maturity.</i> [This applies] even with regard to <i>terumah</i> mandated by Scriptural Law. [The rationale is that] their vows and their consecration [of property] is valid according to Scriptural Law, as explained in <i>[Hilchot] Nedarim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Nedarim</i> 11:1-5.</i>
When a person tells his agent: "Go and separate <i>terumah</i> for me" and he goes to separate <i>terumah</i>, but [the principal] does not know whether he [actually] separated <i>terumah</i> or not, [even] when he discovers that <i>terumah</i> was separated from the granary, we do not assume that [he] separated <i>terumah</i>. For with regard to prohibitions, we apply the principle:<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Eruvin</i> 31b; <i>Gittin</i> 64a,b <i>et al</i>.</i> "An agent can be assumed to have carried out his mission," only when that leads to a stringency, not when it leads to a leniency.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we are uncertain whether we can rely on the fact that an agent will carry out his mission. Hence, if his doing so would lead to a prohibition, we rule stringently and assume that he carried out his mission (see <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 9:6). If, however, his doing so would lead to a leniency, we do not make such an assumption.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:34) quotes the Rambam's ruling. The <i>Tur</i> and the Rama, however, rule that in such a situation, we can assume that the agent carried out his mission, for failing to do so would create a spiritual stumbling block for the principal.</i> [Instead,] we suspect that another person separated the <i>terumah</i> without his permission.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, the separation of <i>terumah</i> would not be effective, as stated in Halachah 2.</i>
When a person tells his agent: "Go and separate <i>terumah</i>," he should separate according to the temperament of the owner. If he knows that he is parsimonious, he should separate one sixtieth. If he was generous, he should separate one fortieth. If he does not know his temperament, he should make the average separation, one fiftieth. If he intended to make the average separation, but it turned out that he separated one fortieth or one sixtieth, his separation is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 4:4), the Rambam writes that if the agent gave more or less than the average measure unintentionally, his separation is effective. In contrast to the situation described in Chapter 5, Halachah 5, here the agent is acting with the principal's permission. Hence, even if he deviated from the amount he could be expected to give, his act is of consequence.</i> If he intended to add to the average separation, and he separated even one forty-ninth,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on authoritative manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard published text has a slightly different version.</i> his separation is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz interprets this as meaning that the agent knew the amount the principal would prefer giving and intentionally gave more. Since he intentionally set aside a measure that differs from the principal's wishes, he is considered as acting without permission.</i>
There is an obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> and tithes from produce belonging to partners, for [Numbers 18:28] speaks of "your tithes," [using a plural term,] implying even from two people.<br>Partners do not have to receive permission from each other. Instead, whenever anyone separates <i>terumah</i>, the separation is effective. [The following rules apply when] one of them separated <i>terumah</i> and then the other came and separated <i>terumah</i>, because he did not know that his colleague had separated it. If they relied<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the glosses of the Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>. Other definitions have been offered in different sources.</i> on each other, the <i>terumah</i> separated by the second is not of consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Because the mitzvah was completed by the <i>terumah</i> separated by the first. Had the second known that the first had made the separation, he would not have separated <i>terumah</i>. Thus the separation of the second was made in error.</i> If they did not rely on each other, but the first one separated an appropriate amount, the separation of the second one is of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Had the second known that the first separated the appropriate amount, he would not have separated the <i>terumah</i>. Hence, here also, we say that the separation of the second was made in error.</i> If the first did not separate the appropriate amount, the separation of the second one is also of consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">And it completes the requirement made by our Sages (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).<br>When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:35) emphasizes that in the present age, when it is sufficient to separate even the slightest amount as <i>terumah</i>, the first person's separation is always sufficient and the second's separation is never of consequence.</i>
[The following rules apply when a person] tells his agent, his attendant,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 3:4).</i> his servant, or his maid-servant to separate <i>terumah</i> and they went to separate the <i>terumah</i>, but he nullified their agency before they made the separation. If the agent did not deviate [from the principal's instructions], his separation is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the principal nullified the agency. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit</i>), the Rambam explains the rationale for his ruling. The nullification of the agency was brought about through speech and speech does not have the power to effect such a change.<br>The Ra'avad and the <i>Tur</i> question the Rambam's decision, for seemingly, just as the appointment of the agent was made through speech, the nullification of his agency can also be made through speech. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> note that in fact, the Rambam himself accepts this concept in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 9:20 with regard to appointing an agent to consecrate a woman as one's wife. Both of these commentaries quote possible rationales to distinguish between the separation of <i>terumah</i> and the consecration of a woman.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:36) quotes the Rambam's ruling, while the Rama cites that of the Ra'avad and the <i>Tur</i>.</i> If he did deviate, e.g., the principal told him: "Separate from the northern side," and he separated from the southern side, since he nullified the agency previously, the separation is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Here too, the <i>Tur</i> questions the Rambam's wording. Seemingly, since the agent deviated from the principal's instructions, the agency is nullified even if the principal did not state that he was nullifying it. The Radbaz explains that a deviation as slight as the side of the produce from which <i>terumah</i> should be separated would not be sufficient to nullify the agency unless the principal had already expressed his willingness to do so.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a sharecropper separates <i>terumah</i> and the owner [of the land] objects. If he objects before he completes the separation of the <i>terumah</i>, the separation is not of consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz notes that, in all instances, he is allowed to separate the tithes for his own portion of the crops.</i> If he objected after the separation, the separation is of consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:67 states that his separation is effective, because we can assume that he appointed him as an agent.</i><br>Guardians may separate <i>terumah</i> from the property of orphans.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> refers to the Rambam's ruling, <i>Hilchot Nachalot</i> 11:9:

Guardians should separate <i>terumah</i> and the tithes from the [crops of] orphans so that they can provide them with food. For we may not feed orphans forbidden substances. They may not, however, tithe or separate <i>terumah</i> from produce so that it will be ready for use [by others]. Instead, they should sell it as <i>tevel</i>. Those who desire to make it ready for use will do so.
</i>
The separation of <i>terumah</i> by a thief, a robber, and a man of force<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">One who compelled a person to give him or sell him produce.</i> is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">From a comparison to the following clause, we assume that this clause is speaking about a situation where the owners already despaired of receiving their produce again. Since the separation of <i>terumah</i> changed the status of the crops, the thief or robber acquires them because of the owner's desperation and their change of status. Since the thief is now the owner, his separation is effective.</i> If the owner is pursuing them,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">With the intent of retaking his produce.</i> the separation is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since the owner has not given up hope for the recovery of the produce, it has not left his possession. Thus the thief is not separating <i>terumah</i> from produce that belongs to him.</i>
A child, a worker, a servant, and a wife should separate <i>terumah</i> for [produce] that they are eating, but not for the entire [crop].<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since this produce was given to them to eat, we assume that license was also given for them to separate <i>terumah</i> from it.</i> For a person should not separate <i>terumah</i> from [produce] that does not belong to him.<br>When a son eats together with his father<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is eating together with his father - in contrast to the child mentioned in the previous halachah - we assume that his father gives him license to separate the <i>terumah</i>.</i> and a woman [makes] dough,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since she is given the responsibility of preparing the dough, she is also given the license to separate <i>terumah</i> from it.</i> they may separate <i>terumah</i>, because they have license to do so.
Workers do not have license to separate <i>terumah</i> without the consent of the owner with the exception of those who tread grapes in the vat. [They are given this license because] if they desired to make the wine impure, they could do so immediately. Since the wine was thus given over to their domain and they were entrusted with it, they are considered as agents. If they separate <i>terumah</i>, the separation is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Since we are all impure in the present age, this law no longer applies and it is not cited by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:42) together with the first part of the halachah.</i>
When a worker is told by the owner: "Bring my granary in and separate <i>terumah</i>," but he separates and then brings the granary in, his separation is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Although he has deviated from the principal's words, we still consider him as an agent. The rationale is that the principal did not make an explicit statement that he was particular that the worker take these steps in this order. Indeed, the common practice is to separate <i>terumah</i> before one brings in the granary. This represents the Rambam's understanding of the <i>Tosefta, Terumot</i> 1:9 (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
When a gentile separates <i>terumah</i> from his own produce, according to Scriptural Law, the separation is not effective, because he is not obligated to do so.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 11. This ruling could be understood as a reversal of the Rambam's position in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 3:9). There he writes that although the gentiles are not obligated to fulfill the mitzvot, if they do, their deeds are effective and they receive a reward. (See <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 10:10).</i> [Our Rabbis] decreed that his separation should be effective, because of the wealthy,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 13.</i> [i.e., they were fearful] lest the money belong to a Jew and he say that it belongs to a gentile to make it exempt.<br>We cross-examine the gentile who separates <i>terumah</i>. If he says: "I separated it so that it should be like a Jew's," we give it to a priest. If not, it should be entombed, for perhaps his intent was [to dedicate it] to heaven.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">And hence it is considered like consecrated property and we are forbidden to benefit from it.</i><br>When does the above apply? In <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Our Sages did not, however, issue a decree if a gentile separates <i>terumah</i> in the Diaspora.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbinic decrees are considered as safeguards and generally, we do not make a safeguard for a safeguard. Hence, since the need to separate <i>terumah</i> in the Diaspora is only a Rabbinic decree, we do not add an additional Rabbinic decree and consider the gentile's separation effective (Radbaz).</i> We tell him that he is not obligated to do this and the produce is not <i>terumah</i> at all.
When a person [makes a separation and] intends to say <i>terumah</i>, but instead says tithes, or intends to say tithes, but instead says <i>terumah</i>, his statement is of no consequence unless his mouth and his heart are in accord.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For the separation of <i>terumah</i> is equivalent to a vow and when making a vow, one's intent and one's statements must be in accord (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:75).</i><br>If one separates <i>terumah</i> in his mind without uttering anything verbally, the separation is effective, as [implied by Numbers 18:27]: "And your <i>terumah</i> will be considered<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Venechshav</i> translated as "considered" shares the root <i>chashav</i> meaning "thought," thus leading to the interpretation (<i>Beitzah</i> 13b) that <i>terumah</i> can be separated through thought alone. See also the notes to Chapter 3, Halachah 4.</i> for you as the <i>terumah</i> of the granary." Through thought alone, it becomes <i>terumah</i>.
[The following rules apply when a person] separates <i>terumah</i> with a stipulation. If the stipulation is fulfilled, the separation is effective. If not, it is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This principle is derived from the law stated in the following halachah (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i><br>When a person separates <i>terumah</i> and/or the tithes and then regrets [his act], he may approach a sage and ask for its repeal as other vows are repealed.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nedarim</i> 4:5.</i> The produce then reverts to being ordinary produce as it was before<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Nedarim</i> 59a, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that this halachah applies only when the <i>terumah</i> has not yet been given to a priest or the tithes to a Levite. If these portions have already been given, there is no way the separations can be retracted.</i> until he makes a separation a second time, [setting aside] either the same produce he separated initially or other produce.
[The following rules apply when a person] separates <i>terumah</i> from a cistern of wine, saying: <sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he lets down a utensil into the cistern, allows it to fill, and makes the stipulation cited. Making the stipulation is desirable so that if the <i>terumah</i> falls back into the cistern - because the container breaks or because the wine spills from it - it does not cause the remaining wine to become <i>meduma</i> (mixed with <i>terumah</i>). As indicated by the following halachah, it is preferable to do this - instead of separating the <i>terumah</i> after the container has been lifted from the cistern, so that the <i>terumah</i> and the produce from which it is being separated will be in the same location (see Chapter 3, Halachah 17).</i> "Behold [the contents of] this [container] are <i>terumah</i> on the condition that it ascends intact [from the cistern]," [for the condition to have been met, the container] must ascend intact from being broken or spilt. [The stipulation does not cover the wine] becoming impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is not a frequent occurrence, we do not assume the person had it in mind when he made the stipulation.</i> If [the container] is broken [and the wine] spills back into the cistern, it does not cause the mixture to become <i>meduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>meduma</i> refers to a mixture of <i>terumah</i> and ordinary produce, as explained in Chapter 13, Halachah 2. In this instance, although the wine in the container flows back into the cistern after it was designated as <i>terumah</i>, since that designation was made conditionally and the condition was not fulfilled, the wine separated is not considered as <i>terumah</i>. Hence the mixture is not <i>meduma</i>.</i> If [he lifted the container from the cistern and] put it in a place where if it breaks or rolls, it will not reach the cistern, [should the wine later spill into the cistern], it causes the mixture to become <i>meduma</i>, because the stipulation was fulfilled.
When does the above apply? With regard to the great <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">For, as mentioned above, the <i>terumah</i> and the produce from which it is being separated should be in the same location.</i> With regard to <i>terumat ma'aser</i>, by contrast, [different rules apply] because it is permitted to make this separation from produce that is not in the same place.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 3, Halachah 20.</i> [Hence as soon as the container] ascends [from the cistern], his stipulation is considered to have been fulfilled and [the wine separated] is <i>terumat ma'aser</i>, even though [afterwards, the container] breaks or [the wine] spills.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale for the Rambam's ruling can be explained as follows: <i>Terumat ma'aser</i> can be separated when the produce is not in the same place as the produce for which it is being separated. Hence, were the person making the separation to be concerned that the produce separated as <i>terumat ma'aser</i> might fall into the cistern, he should have made the separation outside the cistern. The fact that he did not do so, nor make a specific statement acknowledging this possibility in his stipulation shows that this was not the intent motivating his stipulation. Hence, as soon as the container ascends from the cistern, his condition is met, even if the wine spills back in afterwards.</i> Needless to say, this applies if [the <i>terumat ma'aser</i>] becomes impure.
When a person says: "The produce in the upper portion [of this container] is <i>terumah</i> and that in its lower portion is ordinary produce" or "The produce in the upper portion [of this container] is ordinary produce and that in its lower portion is <i>terumah</i>," his statements are effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even though physically, the produce was not separated, since a separation was made in the mind of the owner, it is sufficient and it is permitted to eat from the portion of the produce that is designated as "ordinary."</i> For the matter is dependent on the thought of the person making the separation.
When a person separates <i>terumah</i> from [grain in] a granary, he must have the intent in his heart that [the produce separated] will be <i>terumah</i> for this grain heap, the stalks of wheat that were cut [and not threshed], [the grain] on the side of the grain heap, and [the kernels] in the straw.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ma'aserot</i> 5:4), the Rambam writes that the intent is that he should separate <i>terumah</i> for the entire crop, that which he sees and that which he does not see.</i> When a person separates <i>terumah</i> from a vat [of wine], he must have the intent in his heart that he is separating <i>terumah</i> for [the wine absorbed] in the kernels and the peels.<br>When a person separates <i>terumah</i> from a cistern of wine,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:50) speaks of a vat of oil. There are some who maintain that the wording in the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> should also read in that manner. Nevertheless, the authoritative manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> read as above.</i> he must have the intent in his heart that he is separating <i>terumah</i> for [the wine absorbed] in the peat.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the dregs. In truth, the term <i>gefet</i> is used more frequently in connection with oil than with wine.</i> [in all the above instances,] if he separates <i>terumah</i> without having a specific intent, all of the produce is included. For it is a condition of the court that [when one separates <i>terumah</i>, it] includes everything.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, since we assume that every Jew desires to perform the mitzvot in keeping with our Sages' desires, it is as if the person separating the <i>terumah</i> had this intent in mind. Preferably, however, a person should consciously have such thoughts when separating <i>terumah</i>.</i> When a person separates <i>terumah</i> from a basket of figs and other figs are found at the side of the basket, <i>terumah</i> need not be separated from them, because in his heart, a person has the intent to separate <i>terumah</i> for all the produce.

Chapter 5

We should separate <i>terumah</i> only from the most choice [produce], as [implied by Numbers 18:30]: "When you separate its most choice portion from it."<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chelbo</i>, translated as "most choice portion," literally means "fat."<br>When quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:52) states that in the present age, when <i>terumah</i> will be destroyed regardless, there is no need to separate the most choice portions as <i>terumah</i>.<br>See also the Ramban's <i>Hosafot</i> to <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Negative Mitzvah 7) which considers the adjuration against separating lower quality produce as <i>terumah</i> as one of the Torah's 613 prohibitions.</i> If, however, there is no priest accessible,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the <i>terumah</i> will not be able to be given to the priest immediately.</i> one should separate <i>terumah</i> from produce that will last<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since there is no priest immediately available, it is preferable to separate produce that will not spoil, so that ultimately, it can be given to a priest.</i> even though there is more choice produce but it will not last.<br>What is implied? A person should separate fresh figs as <i>terumah</i> for dried figs.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For fresh figs are tastier and considered as a higher grade produce.</i> In a place where there are no priests, he should separate dried figs for fresh figs.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since there is no priest immediately available, it is preferable to separate produce that will not spoil, so that ultimately, it can be given to a priest.</i> If he is accustomed to dry fresh figs, he may separate fresh figs as <i>terumah</i> for dried figs even in a place where there is no priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">For he will dry the figs himself and prevent them from spoiling before they are given to the priest.</i> In a place where there is a priest, by contrast, dried figs are never separated as <i>terumah</i> for fresh figs, even in a place where it is customary to dry fresh figs.
One may separate an entire onion as <i>terumah</i> even if it is small,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A whole onion is considered more important and choicer than a portion of an onion [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 2:5)].</i> but not half an onion, even if it is large in all places.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in places where a priest is present or those where he is not present, for a whole onion will keep longer than a cut one (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> We do not separate one type of produce as <i>terumah</i> for another type of produce, as [implied by Numbers 18:30]: "As grain from the granary and as wine from the vat."<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that the <i>terumah</i> and the produce for which it is being separated must be of the same species.</i> If one made such a separation, it is not considered <i>terumah</i>.
Zucchini and cucumbers are considered as one species.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">They are also considered as one species with regard to the prohibition against <i>kilayim</i> (<i>Hilchot Kilayim</i> 3:3).</i> All of the types of wheat are one species. All of the types of figs,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., light figs and dark figs.</i> dried figs, and blocks of figs are one species. [In the above instances,] one may separate <i>terumah</i> from one type for the other. [By contrast,] all types of produce which are considered as a mixture of species may not be separated as <i>terumah</i> for each other, even when one separates the choicest produce as <i>terumah</i> for produce of lesser quality.<br>What is implied? A person has 50 <i>se'ah</i> of wheat and 50 <i>se'ah</i> of barley in one building. If he separates two <i>se'ah</i> of wheat [as <i>terumah</i>] for the entire amount, his separation is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Because wheat and barley are different species. Moreover, it is considered as if <i>terumah</i> has not been separated even from the wheat alone. The rationale is that separating <i>terumah</i> is considered equivalent to taking a vow and if a portion of a vow has been nullified, the entire vow is nullified.</i> When types of produce are not considered as a mixture of species, one may separate the better type as <i>terumah</i> for the lesser type, but not the lesser type as <i>terumah</i> for the better type. If one did separate [the lesser type of produce as <i>terumah</i> for the better type, after the fact,] the separation is effective with one exception. <i>Zunin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">A coarse species of wild wheat.</i>may not be separated as <i>terumah</i> for wheat, because it is not used as food for humans.
We do not separate <i>terumah</i> from produce for which all the work [in preparing it] was completed<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">At which point the obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> and the tithes falls upon it (see Chapter 1, Halachah 11, and notes).</i> for produce for which the work [in preparing it] is incomplete, from produce for which the work [in preparing it] is incomplete for [other] produce for which the work [in preparing it] is incomplete, nor from produce for which the work [in preparing it] is incomplete for produce for which all the work [in preparing it] was completed.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries note that the order of the clauses employed by the Rambam deviates slightly from the order of his source, <i>Terumot</i> 1:10.</i> [This is also derived from the above prooftext:] "As grain from the granary and as wine from the vat."<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam and previous Rabbinic sources cite several prooftexts for this law. Nevertheless, the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:80 maintains that the law is a Rabbinic decree and the prooftexts are merely <i>asmachteot</i>, allusions cited for support. For that reason, after the fact, the separation is effective.</i> [Implied is that <i>terumah</i> should be separated from produce] for which [all work] was completed for similar produce. If, however, one separated <i>terumah</i> [in any of the above instances], the separation is effective [after the fact].<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 18-20 for more particulars concerning this issue.</i>
When should <i>terumah</i> be separated from a granary? When the kernels are separated.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">When the grain has been threshed and winnowed and then the kernels separated from the chaff. Different commentaries suggest a slight change in the Rambam's wording which would cause the produce to be considered as complete at an earlier stage.</i> If a person separated part [of the grain], he may designate the kernels he separated as <i>terumah</i> for the produce that was not separated. When one brings stalks of grain into his home to eat husked grain, he should separate <i>terumah</i> while the grain is in the stalks.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Because they will not be processed any further.</i>
When should <i>terumah</i> be separated from a vat? When [the treaders] have walked horizontally and vertically over the grapes.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For then, the wine has been separated from the grapes.</i><br>When should one separate <i>terumah</i> from olives? When [the beam of the press] is placed upon them.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">For then, the oil has been separated from the olives.</i>
We should not separate <i>terumah</i> from ritually pure produce for produce that is not ritually pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Terumot</i> 2:1) derives this concept from the prooftext cited above: "As grain from the granary and as wine from the vat." It is impossible that a vat will be partially pure and partially impure, so, too, <i>terumah</i> and the produce for which it is being separated must be either pure or impure."</i> If, however, one separated <i>terumah</i> [in this manner], the separation is effective [after the fact].<br>It is a halachah conveyed to Moses at Sinai that when a portion of a cake of dried figs has become impure, we may separate <i>terumah</i> from the pure portion of it for the impure portion of it as an initial preference.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 2:1), the Rambam explains the rationale for this law. Since the cake of figs is made from several discrete elements, it is not considered as a single entity with regard to the laws of ritual impurity. Hence, the fact that one portion has been touched by a source of ritual impurity does not cause another portion to become impure. Nevertheless, since it is one body of produce, <i>terumah</i> can be separated from one portion for the other.</i> This applies not only to a cake of dried figs which appears as one mass, but also to a bundle of vegetables and even a mound of wheat.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In these instances as well, since the whole is made up of discrete entities, the fact that they are connected or somewhat associated is not of consequence (<i>ibid.</i>; see also <i>Hilchot Tuma'at Ochalin</i> 6:14).</i> If a portion of it became impure, one may separate <i>terumah</i> from the pure portion for the impure portion.<br>If, however, there were two cakes of dried figs, two bundles of vegetables, or two mounds of wheat, one impure and one pure at its side, as an initial preference, one should not separate <i>terumah</i> from the produce that is pure for the produce that is impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For the two are not considered as being "in the same place" (<i>min hamukaf</i>). After the fact, however, the separation is acceptable.</i> One may, however, separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i> from produce that is pure for produce that is impure as an initial preference. [This is derived from Numbers 18:29:] "its sacred portion from it." [Implied is that] one should take from the sacred portion in it.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The verse indicates that when one is separating <i>terumat ma'aser</i>, it is possible that there be a "sacred." i.e., pure, portion, and a portion that is not "sacred," i.e., impure, and the pure should be separated for the impure. As stated at the conclusion of ch. 3, the produce separated as <i>terumat ma'aser</i> need not be located together with the produce for which it is being separated.</i>
We may not separate produce that is ritually impure as <i>terumah</i> for produce that is ritually pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that this is a Rabbinic decree instituted so that the priest will not suffer a loss.</i> If one made such a separation inadvertently, the portion separated is <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Scriptural Law, the separation is effective. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 2:2), the Rambam explains that this is speaking about a situation where initially, at the time the obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> took effect, the produce separated as <i>terumah</i> was pure. If, however, it became impure before the work associated with its preparation was completed, even if it was inadvertently separated as <i>terumah</i>, the separation is not effective.</i> If one made the separation intentionally, he did not fulfill the obligation for the remainder [of the produce], but [the produce] separated is <i>terumah</i>. He must separate <i>terumah</i> a second time.<br>When does the above apply? When he did not know of the impurity. If, however, he knew of the impurity, but erred in that he thought it was permitted to separate produce that is ritually impure as <i>terumah</i> for produce that is ritually pure, he is considered as if he acted intentionally.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">And he must make another separation.</i> Similar laws apply with regard to <i>terumat ma'aser</i>.
We may not separate produce that is still attached to the earth as <i>terumah</i> for produce that has already been detached.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Sifri</i> derives this from the exegesis of Numbers 18:26: "And you shall separate from it...." Since the produce is still attached to the earth, it is not considered as the same type as the produce which is detached.</i> Nor may produce that has already been detached be separated as <i>terumah</i> for produce that is still attached to the earth.<br>What is implied? A person had produce that was detached and said: "This produce will be <i>terumah</i> for this produce which is attached to the earth" - and even if he said "...when [the attached produce] is detached,"<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">To reconcile the apparent contradiction between the Rambam's ruling here and that in the following clause, the commentaries explain that here we are speaking of attached produce that does not belong to the person who wishes to separate <i>terumah</i>. Ordinarily, if a person separated <i>terumah</i> from produce belonging to him for produce that does not belong to him, his separation is valid, as stated in Chapter 4, Halachah 2. In this instance, however, since the attached produce does not belong to him and he has no control of when it will be detached, he does not have the potential to designate his own produce as <i>terumah</i> for it.</i> his words are of no consequence. [This ruling also applies] if he possessed two rows [of produce] and said: "The produce of this row which is detached will be <i>terumah</i> for this row which is attached," or "The produce of this row which is attached will be <i>terumah</i> for this row which is detached."<br>[Diferent rules apply should] he say: "The produce of this row which is detached will be <i>terumah</i> for this row when it will be detached," if [the produce] is [later] detached - since it is within his potential to detach it<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This is his own produce which he is allowed to detach.</i> - when they are both detached, his words are binding, provided both [types of produce] had attained at least a third of their standard size<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For until produce reaches this size, there is no obligation to separate <i>terumah</i>, as stated in Chapter 2, Halachah 10. Therefore, even if one separated <i>terumah</i> for it, the separation is not effective.</i> at the time he made his statements.
We may not separate fresh produce as <i>terumah</i> for dried produce, nor dried produce as <i>terumah</i> for fresh produce. If, however, one made such a separation, it is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:83 states that this is a Rabbinic decree, enacted as a safeguard so that one will not separate produce from one type over another. Hence, after the fact, the separation is effective.</i><br>What is implied? One reaped a type of vegetable one day and then reaped the same [type of vegetable] the following day. One may not separate one as <i>terumah</i> for the other unless this vegetable remains fresh for two days. Similarly, if a vegetable usually remains fresh for three days, e.g., cucumbers, all [of that type of vegetable] that are harvested for three days may be joined together and <i>terumah</i> may be separated from one for the other. When a type of vegetable remains fresh for only one day and one harvested some in the morning and some in the evening, one may separate one as <i>terumah</i> for the other.
One may not separate produce from the present year as <i>terumah</i> for produce of the previous year,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">To clarify the point under discussion in this halachah: As indicated by the concluding phrase of the halachah, "produce from the previous year" does not necessarily mean produce that is a year old. Instead, every Rosh HaShanah, the year of the agricultural cycle changes with regard to vegetables (<i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 14b). As the Rambam proceeds to explain, a Scriptural decree causes such produce to be considered as a discrete entity, distinct from the same type of produce harvested in the present year.</i> nor may one separate produce from the previous year as <i>terumah</i> for produce of the present year. If one made such a separation, it is not effective,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Even after the fact.</i> as [indicated by Deuteronomy 14:22]: "From year to year."<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In its entirety, the verse reads: "Tithe all the crops of your sowing produced by the field, year by year." Implied is an exclusion: that the produce of each year is a separate entity.
