Although the verse refers to the tithes, the same principles also apply to <i>terumah</i>.<br>In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 1:5), the Rambam cites a different prooftext for the derivation of this principle.</i>Thus if one harvested a vegetable on the day preceding Rosh HaShanah before sunset and harvested another after sunset, one may not separate <i>terumah</i> from one for the other. For one is considered "old" and the other, "new."<br>Similarly, if one harvested an esrog<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">In that era, <i>esrogim</i> were eaten as fruit, not only used for the mitzvah on Sukkot.</i> on the day before the fifteenth of Shvat on the evening before sunset and harvested another one after sunset [that day], one may not separate <i>terumah</i> from one for the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">An <i>esrog</i> is singled out, because unlike other fruit, the time when it is picked - and not the time it reaches the stage of development when we are required to separate the tithes - determines the year of the agricultural cycle we follow. See <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 1:1-4.</i> [The rationale is that] the first of Tishrei is the Rosh HaShanah for tithing grain, legumes, and vegetables and the fifteenth of Shvat is Rosh HaShanah for tithing [of the produce] of trees.
We may not separate produce from <i>Eretz [Yisrael]</i> as <i>terumah</i> for produce of the Diaspora, nor may produce of the Diaspora be separated for produce from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 1:5), the Rambam explains that this concept is derived from Leviticus 27:30: "All the tithes of the land from the plantings of the land." Implied is that both the tithes and the produce must be from "the land," from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Although the verse speaks of the tithes, the principle derived is also applied to <i>terumah</i>.</i> nor may produce of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> be separated for produce from Syria, and nor may produce of Syria be separated for produce from <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Scriptural Law, Syria is also part of the Diaspora.</i><br>Similarly, [we may not separate <i>terumah</i>] from produce for which <i>terumah</i> need not be separated, e.g., <i>leket, shichachah</i>, and <i>pe'ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 9.</i> or from produce from which <i>terumah</i> was already separated for produce for which <i>terumah</i> must be separated. Nor may we separate <i>terumah</i> from produce for which <i>terumah</i> must be separated for produce for which there is no obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, the fundamental point the Rambam is emphasizing is the following clause: that even if the <i>terumah</i> was separated, the separation is not of consequence.</i> [Even] if the <i>terumah</i> was separated, the separation is not of consequence.
When a person separates <i>terumah</i> [for other produce] from produce that was designated as the first tithe, but from which its <i>terumah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., <i>terumah ma'aser</i>, the <i>terumah</i> that must be separated from the tithes.</i> had not been separated<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 1:5, Rav Kapach's edition), the Rambam explains that this is referring to a situation in which a Levite took produce that he had been given as tithes by an Israelite, but from which he had not separated <i>terumat ma'aser</i> and desired to use it to separate <i>terumah</i> for produce from his own field from which he is obligated to separate <i>terumah</i>. This is unacceptable, because there is no longer any obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from the produce given as tithes, even though there is an obligation to separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i> from it.<br>This explanation is somewhat unique. Most commentaries interpret the mishnah as referring to a situation where the person tithed his crop, but did not separate <i>terumah</i> from it. Nor did he separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i>. Afterwards, he desired to separate <i>terumah</i> for other produce from the tithes. The Rambam's interpretation avoids several difficulties that arise according to the other approach.</i> or from produce which was designated as the second tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Produce separated as the second tithe must be eaten in Jerusalem in a state of ritual purity. It is not considered as the person's private property.</i> or that had been consecrated,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is consecrated, there is no obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> or the tithes from it.</i> but which had not been redeemed, the separation is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In all these instances, the produce that is being separated is holy and is not the owner's individual property. (For even the tithes which the Levites own have a dimension of holiness to them, because of the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> that has not been separated.) The Ra'avad raises objections to the Rambam's ruling. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> respond to them. If, however, they would have followed the Rambam's explanation in his Commentary to the Mishnah as explained above, the difficulties would not arise.</i>
We may not separate <i>terumah</i> from produce for which we are required to separate <i>terumah</i> according to Scriptural Law for produce for which we are required to separate <i>terumah</i> [only] according to Rabbinic Law,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">According to Scriptural Law, there is no obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from this produce. Hence, it is as if one is separating <i>terumah</i> from produce obligated in <i>terumah</i> for produce for which there is no obligation. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that this general principle is derived from the law quoted in the following halachah.</i> nor from produce for which we are required to separate <i>terumah</i> [only] according to Rabbinic Law for produce for which we are required to separate <i>terumah</i> according to Scriptural Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">For this is as if one is separating <i>terumah</i> from produce for which there is no obligation to separate <i>terumah</i>, for produce for which such an obligation exists.</i> If one separated <i>terumah</i> [in the above instance], the produce separated is considered as <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This is a Rabbinic stringency. According to Scriptural Law, the separation is of no consequence at all.</i> but he must separate <i>terumah</i> again [for the obligation incumbent on this produce to be fulfilled].<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, this applies only to the second clause. The laws regarding the situation described in the first clause are explained in Halachah 16.<br>In the situation described in the second clause, the priest is allowed to partake of the produce, for although according to Scriptural Law, the separation is insignificant, there is no difficulty in having the priest partake of the produce for there is no obligation incumbent upon it.</i>
A flowerpot with a hole is considered as [connected] to the earth.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Because the nurture from the power of growth in the earth is drawn to the earth in the flowerpot through the hole. This concept is relevant not only in this particular context, but in many others.</i> How large must the hole be [for this law to apply]? Large enough for a small root to pass through it.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">For then it can be considered to have derived nurture from the earth through its root. Hence, we are obligated to separate <i>terumah</i> from produce which grows within such a flowerpot according to Scriptural Law [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Demai</i> 5:10)].</i> This is smaller than an olive.<br>[The following rules apply when a person] planted grain in a flowerpot that did not have a hole and it reached a third of its maturity.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">If produce grows in a flowerpot without a hole, there is no obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from it according to Scriptural Law (<i>ibid.</i>). Here a question arises, because the flowerpot was perforated afterwards. The Rambam clarifies that as long as the produce reaches a third of its growth, the point at which we are obligated to separate <i>terumah</i>, under conditions in which we are not obligated to separate <i>terumah</i>, an obligation is not incurred even though afterwards, those conditions change. See Halachah 9; see also Chapter 1, Halachot 11-13.</i> Afterwards, he perforated [the flowerpot]<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">And thus enabled the produce to derive nurture from the earth.</i> and the grain completed [its growth] while [the flowerpot] was perforated. It is, [nevertheless,] considered as if it grew in [a flowerpot] without a hole. [This ruling changes] only when [the flowerpot] was perforated before [the grain] reached a third of its maturity.
When a person separates <i>terumah</i> from produce which grows in the earth for produce that grows in a perforated flowerpot or from produce which grows in a perforated flowerpot for produce which grows in the earth, the separation is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">For in both instances, we are obligated to separate <i>terumah</i> from both sets of produce according to Scriptural Law.</i><br>If he separates <i>terumah</i> from [produce growing] in a [flowerpot] that was not perforated for [produce growing] in a perforated [flowerpot], the produce separated is considered as <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">This is a Rabbinic decree, as stated in Halachah 14 and notes.</i> but he must separate <i>terumah</i> again.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In order to fulfill the obligation of <i>terumah</i> as stated in Halachah 14.</i><br>If he separates <i>terumah</i> from [produce growing] in a perforated [flowerpot] for [produce growing] in a [flowerpot] that was not perforated, the produce separated is considered as <i>terumah</i>. [Nevertheless, the priest to whom it was given] should not partake of it until [the owner] separates <i>terumah</i> and tithes for [the portion separated] from other produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The setting aside of the produce as <i>terumah</i> is not effective according to Scriptural Law. Hence, according to Scriptural Law, not only is this produce not <i>terumah</i>, it remains <i>tevel</i>, produce from which <i>terumah</i> and the tithes were not separated. Although our Sages ruled stringently and determined that it must be considered as <i>terumah</i>, they did not change its status as <i>tevel</i>. Hence, for the priest to partake of it, it is necessary that he separate <i>terumah</i> and tithes for it from other produce [see the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Demai</i> 5:10)].<br>The <i>terumah</i> and the tithes for this produce must be separated from other produce, lest one think that what remains is ordinary produce and can be eaten be a person other than a priest (<i>Tosafot, Yevamot</i> 89b).</i>
When a person separates <i>terumat [ma'aser]</i> from produce that is <i>demai</i><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>demai</i> refers to produce concerning which we are uncertain whether the tithes were separated from it or not. According to Rabbinic Law, we are required to separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i>, <i>terumah</i> which the Levites are required to separate from the tithes from such produce (see <i>Hilchot Ma'aserot</i>, Chapter 9).</i> for other produce that is <i>demai</i> or from produce that is <i>demai</i> for produce from which we are certain that the tithes were not separated, the produce separated is considered as <i>terumat [ma'aser]</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">By Rabbinic decree. According to Scriptural Law, the produce separated is not <i>terumat ma'aser</i>, because it is possible that previously tithes and <i>terumat ma'aser</i> had been separated from the produce that was <i>demai</i>. Were that to be the case, one would be separating <i>terumat ma'aser</i> from produce for which one is obligated to make a separation from produce for which no such obligation exists. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> [Nevertheless,] he should separate <i>terumah</i> again for each type of produce individually.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is possible that the separation of the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> is not effective, the owner must make a second separation.<br>In this instance, the priest is allowed to partake of the produce given him. The rationale is that in the second instance, when one separates <i>terumat ma'aser</i> from produce that is <i>demai</i> for produce from which we are certain that the tithes were not separated, the priest is certainly allowed to partake of the produce given him. If <i>terumat ma'aser</i> had not been separated from such produce previously, it is being made at present. And if it had been made previously, according to Scriptural Law, the produce to be given the priest is ordinary produce that may be consumed.<br>If the separation was made from produce that is <i>demai</i> for other produce that is <i>demai</i>, there is a difficulty, for it is possible that a separation had previously been made for the produce for which the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> is being separated, but not from the produce from which the separation is being made at present. Since such a separation is invalid according to Scriptural Law, in such an instance, the priest is being given produce from which tithes and <i>terumat ma'aser</i> have not been separated. Nevertheless, since the majority of the people do in fact separate tithes and the prohibition of <i>demai</i> is just a stringency, in this instance, our Sages were lenient (Rabbi Akiva Eiger).</i><br>When he separates <i>terumah</i> from produce from which we are certain that the tithes were not separated for produce that is <i>demai</i>, the produce separated is considered as <i>terumat [ma'aser]</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This is a Rabbinic stringency as mentioned above.</i> [Nevertheless, the priest to whom it was given] should not partake of it until [the owner] separates <i>terumah</i> and tithes for [the portion separated].<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">For as in the previous halachah, if the original separation of <i>terumat ma'aser</i> is not effective - as would be the case if a separation had been made from the produce classified as <i>demai</i> - there is an obligation to separate <i>terumat ma'aser</i> from the produce being separated as <i>terumat ma'aser</i>. Hence, until that separation is made, the priest is forbidden to partake of the produce given him.<br>There is, however, a difference between this halachah and the previous one. In the previous instance, the separation had to be made from other produce, while in this instance, the separation can be made from the produce given to the priest. In the previous instance, care had to be taken because of the impression that might be created, i.e., an onlooker might not realize that there is a Rabbinic obligation to separate <i>terumot</i> and tithes from produce growing in a flowerpot that is not perforated. No such difficulty exists in this instance, because the obligations incumbent on <i>demai</i> are well known. See the gloss of Ra'avad to <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 13:19.</i>
We may not separate stalks of grain as <i>terumah</i> for kernels of grain, olives [as <i>terumah</i>] for oil, or grapes [as <i>terumah</i>] for wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if one separates a measure of produce sufficient to produce the said quantity of wheat, wine, or oil.</i> If one made such a separation, it is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that, after the fact, if a priest agrees to accept such produce, the separation is acceptable, for there are times when one will consider that preferable.</i> This is a decree [enacted] lest one cause the priest to undertake the difficulty of treading [on the grapes] or pressing [the olives].<br>One may, however, separate oil as <i>terumah</i> for olives that are being pickled<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">But have not been squeezed yet.</i> or wine as <i>terumah</i> for grapes that are being dried as raisins. What does this resemble? To separating <i>terumah</i> from two species that are not considered <i>kilayim</i>, separating from the good for the bad.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This is permissible as stated in Halachah 3.</i><br>Similarly, one may separate <i>terumah</i> from olives from which oil will be squeezed<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 2:6), the Rambam explains that the Hebrew terms refers to olives that contain much oil and their oil is of high quality.</i> for olives that will be pickled,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., these olives have little oil. Hence, they are set aside to be used for pickling. Thus making such a separation would be considered as separating <i>terumah</i> from higher quality produce for lower quality produce which is permitted as stated above.</i> but not from olives that will be pickled for olives from which oil will be squeezed.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">For this is considered as separating <i>terumah</i> from lower quality produce for higher quality produce which is forbidden as an initial preference, as stated in Halachah 3.</i> [One may separate <i>terumah</i>] from wine that has not been boiled for wine that has been boiled,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Boiling detracts from wine's flavor. Hence, one is separating <i>terumah</i> from higher quality produce for lower quality produce.</i> but not from [wine] that has been boiled for [wine] that has not been boiled. [One may separate <i>terumah</i>] from [wine that is] clear<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., does not have dregs suspended in it.</i> for [wine that is] not clear, but not from [wine that is] not clear for wine [that is] clear.<br>[One may separate <i>terumah</i>] from a specific number of fresh figs for a specific number of dried figs<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">When a priest is present and he is being given the figs, fresh figs are obviously considered as more desirable than dried figs.</i> and from a measure of dried figs for a measure of fresh figs,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">For the measure of dried figs will be more compact than that of fresh figs. Thus the priest will be receiving a greater quantity of fruit.</i> but not from a measure of fresh figs for a measure of dried figs, nor for a specific number of dried figs for a specific number of fresh figs. [The rationale for all the above is that] one should always separate <i>terumah</i> in a generous manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., giving and produce of a higher quality or giving more ample portions.</i><br>One may separate <i>terumah</i> from kernels of wheat for bread,<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Kernels of wheat are considered as having an advantage over grain, because a) one can use them for whatever purposes one desires, and b) they store longer than grain.</i> but not from bread for kernels of wheat according to the appropriate calculations.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">i.e., how many kernels of wheat it would take to produce this quantity of bread.</i> In all the above situations, if one separated <i>terumah</i> [when it was stated that one should not], the separation is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 3. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>:63) states that in the present era when the produce separated as <i>terumah</i> is discarded, there is no need to show these precautions.</i>
We do not separate <i>terumah</i> from oil for olives that are being pressed, nor [do we separate <i>terumah</i>] from wine for grapes that are being tread, for this resembles separating <i>terumah</i> from produce for which all work has been completed for produce for which the work has not been completed.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 4 above. In the situation described in that halachah, the person is not required to make a second separation, because our Sages felt that people understood that <i>terumah</i> should not be separated in such an instance and did not see the need for an additional safeguard. In this situation, since work with that produce has already begun, they felt that a safeguard was necessary (<i>Rambam LeAm</i>). Other commentaries offer different rationales for the difference in the rulings.</i> If he did separate <i>terumah</i> [in such a situation], the produce separated is considered as <i>terumah</i>, but he must separate <i>terumah</i> from the olives and the grapes from them themselves.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after he completed pressing the olives or treading the grapes, he should separate <i>terumah</i> a second time for that oil or wine [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 1:8)].</i><br>[When mixed with other produce,] the first creates a situation of <i>dimua</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">A mixture of <i>terumah</i> and ordinary produce, as mentioned in Chapter 13, Halachah 2.</i> alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if it is not mixed with the produce separated a second time in contrast to the situation described in Halachah 22. The rationale is that, in this instance, after the fact, the first separation is effective, as stated in Halachah 4 above.</i> One who eats it is liable for it, as one is liable [for partaking] of other <i>terumah</i> that is clearly defined as such.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 6, Halachah 6.</i> This does not apply with regard to the second <i>terumah</i> [separated].<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">For it is <i>terumah</i> only as a result of Rabbinic decree.</i>
When a person separated oil as <i>terumah</i> for olives that he intended to eat or he separated olives for olives when he intended to eat the entire quantity and then changed his mind and decided to press the olives [for oil] and indeed pressed them after separating <i>terumah</i> for them, he is not required to separate <i>terumah</i> again.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">For at the time the <i>terumah</i> was separated, the work associated with this produce was completed according to his intent at that time [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 1:9)]. Although he changed his mind, we do not penalize him for making such a decision (Radbaz).</i> [This law also applies if] one separated wine for grapes that he intended to eat or grapes for grapes when he intended to eat the entire quantity and then changed his mind and decided to tread the grapes for wine and indeed had them tread upon after separating <i>terumah</i> for them.
We do not separate vinegar as <i>terumah</i> for wine,<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">For this is equivalent to separating produce of inferior quality for produce of superior quality which should not be done at the outset, as stated in Halachah 3. Nevertheless, if one made such a separation, after the fact, the separation s effective.</i> but we may separate wine as <i>terumah</i> for vinegar, for wine and vinegar are one type of produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">For when wine sours, it becomes vinegar.</i> If one had the intent of separating wine for wine and it was discovered that what he separated was vinegar, the separation is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">For his separation was made in error and the error lowers the quality of the <i>terumah</i> (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> If one had the intent of separating vinegar for vinegar and it was discovered that what he separated was wine, the separation is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">For in this instance, his error improves the quality of the <i>terumah</i> (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>; <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 331:95).</i>
[The following rules apply when a person] separates a barrel of wine as <i>terumah</i> for wine and it is discovered to be vinegar. If it was known to be vinegar before it was separated, the separation is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">As in the previous halachah, this is speaking about an instance where the person had the intent of separating wine as <i>terumah</i>. If, however, he intended to separate vinegar, the separation is effective after the fact (Radbaz).</i> If it became vinegar after it was designated as <i>terumah</i>, the separation is effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">For at the time of the separation, wine was separated as the person intended. The fact that it soured afterwards is not his responsibility.</i> If there is a doubt, the separation is effective,<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the doubt involved. Perhaps it had not become sour at the time it was separated and the first separation was effective.</i> but he should make a second separation.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">For perhaps it was sour before the first separation was made and thus the separation was not effective.</i> The same laws apply when one separates squash as <i>terumah</i> and it is discovered to be bitter or one separates a watermelon as <i>terumah</i> and it has become spoiled<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., its contents are not fit for consumption [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 3:1)].</i> or perforated.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, we fear that the holes were made by a poisonous snake which deposited its venom in the watermelon.</i> If he separated a barrel of wine as <i>terumah</i> and it was discovered to have been left open in which instance, it is forbidden to drink it,<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the danger involved. We fear that perhaps a poisonous snake or the like deposited venom in it (<i>Hilchot Rotzeach UShemirat Nefesh</i> 12:2).</i> it is <i>terumah</i>, but he should make a second separation.<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">This appears to be speaking about a situation in which he is unsure when the snake drank from the wine before the <i>terumah</i> was separated or not.</i><br>[In all these situations, when mixed with other produce,] neither of the two [portions separated as <i>terumah</i>] creates a situation of <i>dimua</i> alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since we are unsure which of the separations is effective, neither alone is considered as <i>terumah</i>.</i> Nor is a person who eats only one of the two obligated to pay a fifth.<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">As is required when one partakes of <i>terumah</i> inadvertently, as stated in Chapter 6, Halachah 6.</i>
What is implied? If one [of the two portions separated as <i>terumah</i>] fell into ordinary produce, it does not cause it to be considered as <i>dimua</i>. If the other [portion] fell into other produce, it does not cause it to be considered as <i>dimua</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Although one of the two is obviously <i>dimua</i>, since we do not know which one, we rule leniently with regard to both.</i> If, however, both fell into the same produce, it is considered as <i>dimua</i> according to the size of the smaller of the two.<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, the <i>terumah</i> has obviously become mixed with the ordinary produce. Nevertheless, since we do not know which of the two is <i>terumah</i>, we require him to be concerned only with the smaller amount.</i><br>Similarly, if a person other than a priest ate both of them, he should make restitution for the smaller one and its additional fifth.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">As is required when one partakes of <i>terumah</i> inadvertently, as stated in Chapter 6, Halachah 6.</i> What should he do with the two [portions separated]?<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Although both must be considered as <i>terumah</i>, because he does not have a definite obligation to give either to a priest.</i> He should give them to one priest and take the value of the greater portion from him.<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">For he is obligated to give at least the value of the smaller portion to the priest; for the remainder the priest must pay. Nevertheless, the price he pays for produce designated as <i>terumah</i> is less than that than he would pay for ordinary produce.</i>
[The following rules apply when a person] checks a barrel [of wine]<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">To make sure that it had not become vinegar.</i> and then leaves it to separate <i>terumah</i> [from it] on other [wine] until it becomes <i>terumah</i> in its entirety, [at which point,] he would give it to the priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the person took a barrel of wine and set it aside with the intent that he would consider an appropriate measure of wine from it as <i>terumah</i> for all the wine that he would continue to produce until the barrel became <i>terumah</i> in its entirety.</i> After a certain time, he checked the barrel again and discovered that it had become vinegar.<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Thus there is a question regarding the status of the produce that he separated previously. For if the barrel had become vinegar earlier, the separation of <i>terumah</i> would not be effective, as stated above.</i> [We rule that the wine] certainly remained wine for three days after the inspection. [The obligations for <i>terumah</i>] have certainly been met for all of the wine for which he considered the wine in the barrel as its <i>terumah</i> during those [three days]. From that time afterwards, there is a doubt [whether the wine had already turned to vinegar] and a second separation [of <i>terumah</i>] must be made.<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">As explained above.</i>
At three times [during the year], it is necessary to check the wine that one set aside to separate <i>terumah</i> from<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the person took a barrel of wine and set it aside with the intent that he would consider an appropriate measure of wine from it as <i>terumah</i> for all the wine that he would continue to produce until the barrel became <i>terumah</i> in its entirety.</i> lest it have become vinegar. They are: When the east wind blows after the Sukkos holiday, when grapes form,<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">When the flowers of the grape vine fall and the actual fruit begins to form.</i> when fluid begins to enter the unripened grapes.<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">When the juice of the grape begins to collect within the fruit.<br>Since these are times of change for grapes, there is also a possibility that wine will also be affected.</i> [When one has wine] from the vat,<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., freshly squeezed grape juice.</i> he may use it for the separation [of <i>terumah</i>] for 40 days with the presumption that it is still wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">For it is unlikely to turn into vinegar before then.</i>
[The following rules apply when a person] sets aside produce to separate <i>terumah</i> from until [the entire amount] becomes <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he sets aside produce to serve the same purpose as the wine mentioned in Halachah 24.</i> Although as an initial preference, one should separate <i>terumah</i> only from the same collection of produce,<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 3, Halachah 17. As explained there, after the fact, such a separation is acceptable.</i> if one separates <i>terumah</i> [in such a manner], we operate under the presumption that the produce continues to exist. If he discovers that they perished, he should entertain doubts that all [the produce for which he thought he separated <i>terumah</i> is in fact <i>tevel</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he should entertain the possibility that perhaps from the outset, the separation of the <i>terumah</i> was not acceptable.</i> For perhaps, he did not make any separation until [the produce set aside] perished. Therefore he should separate <i>terumah</i> a second time for them.<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Halachah 22.</i>

Chapter 6

The great <i>terumah</i> and <i>terumat ma'aser</i> may be eaten by priests, both adults and minors, both males and females and their Canaanite servants,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Because Canaanite servants do not have a separate personal identity, but instead are considered as part of their master's possessions. A Hebrew servant, by contrast, is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> as explained in Halachah 5.</i> and their livestock,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Sifra</i> states that this applies only to animal fodder. Produce that is fit for human consumption may not be given to an animal.</i> as [Leviticus 22:11] states: "When a priest will purchase a man, he is a financial acquisition...."<br>When the servant of a priest flees<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, his master is no longer obligated to provide him with his sustenance.</i> or the wife of a priest rebels against him,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The term "rebels" has a very specific meaning when used in this context: to deny a husband marital intimacy. In such an instance, her husband has the right to divorce her and is not obligated to pay for her sustenance, as stated in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i>, ch. 14. Nevertheless, until he divorces her, she is entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> they may still partake of <i>terumah</i>.
<i>Terumah</i> required by Scriptural Law may only be eaten by a priest whose lineage [has been affirmed].<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 20:2 defines this term as follows:

Anyone concerning whom two witnesses testify that he is a priest, the son of so-and-so the priest, and the descendant of so-and-so the priest, extending back until we reach a person whose lineage need not be checked, i.e., a priest who served at the altar.

The restriction of the consumption of <i>terumah</i> to such priests is alluded to in the Book of Ezra  2:62-63): "These sought their genealogical records, but they could not be found, so they were disqualified from the priesthood. [Nechemiah] told them that they should not eat of the most holy offerings [which includes <i>terumah</i>] until there would arise a priest [who would inquire of] the <i>Urim</i> and <i>Tumim</i> [concerning their lineage]."</i> Priests whose status is accepted on the basis of a prevailing assumption<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are regarded as priests by people at large even though there is no definite proof of their lineage.</i> may partake only of <i>terumah</i> required by Rabbinic decree.<br><i>Terumah</i> that is ritually pure, whether the great <i>terumah</i> or <i>terumat ma'aser</i>, whether required by Scriptural or Rabbinic Law is given only to a priest who is a Torah scholar<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For only a Torah scholar can be expected to be versed in the knowledge and practice of the laws of ritual purity. The <i>Sifri</i> (to Numbers 18:28  cites a Biblical verse as support for this concept.<br>This is also alluded to by a Biblical narrative. II Chronicles, ch. 30, relates how King Chezekiah affected a renewal of Torah observance among the Jewish people. Among his efforts was to renew the observance of <i>terumah</i> and tithes, as it is written (30:4): "He told the people... to give the portions of the priests and the Levites so that they could strengthen themselves in the Torah of God." <i>Chulin</i> 130b interprets this to mean that these gifts should be given only to a priest or Levite who "is strong in his observance of the Torah of God." In the revision of his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Challah</i> 4:9), the Rambam writes that it is desirable and advisable to follow this restriction, but there is no prohibition against giving <i>terumah</i> to an unlearned priest.</i> because it is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> that is ritually impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">We are concerned that the impure priest will cause the <i>terumah</i> to become ritually impure and then partake of it while it is in that state.</i> All of the unlearned people are presumed to be ritually impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries explain that we do not think that a common person will willingly transgress and eat <i>terumah</i> that is impure. Instead, the suspicion is that he will be impure and therefore make the <i>terumah</i> impure. Nevertheless, he will not realize what has transpired and will eat the <i>terumah</i> that he himself made impure.</i> For these reasons, one may give impure <i>terumah</i> to any priest one chooses.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">For it has to be burnt and any priest can perform that act. The Ra'avad differs with this ruling, maintaining that even such <i>terumah</i> should not be given to a priest who is unlearned. The Rambam's position can be supported based on the explanation given above that we do not suspect that the unlearned priest will deliberately transgress.</i>
When an Israelite woman marries a priest, even a minor three years and one day old,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If a girl is younger than that, her consecration is not binding, as stated in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:11.</i> she may partake of <i>terumah</i> and the breast and thigh [given the priests from the peace sacrifices].<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Which may be eaten by all the members of a priest's household (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbonot</i> 10:5).</i><br>According to Scriptural Law, she may partake of these foods from the time that she was consecrated. Our Sages, however, forbade her from partaking of them until she enters the <i>chupah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">According to Jewish law, marriage is a two-staged process involving <i>kiddushin</i> or <i>erusin</i> (consecration) and <i>nissuin</i> (living as man and wife). Today, it is customary to perform both stages at the same time. In the Talmudic era, by contrast, a significant amount of time - usually a year - was granted between the two. In this interim period, the woman was consecrated to her husband and relations between her and another man would be considered adulterous. Nevertheless, she lived in her parents' home and did not engage in intimacy with her husband. Nor was he obligated to support her. Our Sages feared that were a woman given <i>terumah</i> in this intermediate period, she might give it to her father or brother to eat.</i> [This is a] decree, [instituted] lest she give her father and brother <i>terumah</i> to eat while she is a consecrated woman in her father's home.
A deaf-mute and a mentally or emotionally unstable woman who is married to a priest may not partake of <i>terumah</i>. [This restriction applies] even if her father concluded her marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Both of these types of women are considered as incapable of marrying according to Scriptural Law. Since a deaf-mute's ability to communicate is so restricted, our Sages placed her in this category. Contemporary Rabbinic experts debate the status of a deaf-mute who was trained to communicate through other means.<br>Were such women to conclude marriages on their own, the marriage would not be binding (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:9). Nevertheless, the father of such a woman has the potential to conclude her marriage before she reaches the age of majority. If he does so, the marriage is binding according to Scriptural Law (<i>ibid.</i> 3:11). Even so, our Sages forbade such women from partaking of <i>terumah</i> for the reason stated by the Rambam.</i> [This is] decree, [instituted] lest a priest who is himself a deaf-mute marry a woman who is a deaf-mute and give her [<i>terumah</i>] to eat.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to a mentally sound priest, the marriage of a priest who is a deaf-mute is binding only according to Rabbinic decree. According to Scriptural Law, the marriage is of no consequence. Hence, such a priest's "wife" has no right to eat <i>terumah</i></i> For this reason, our Sages decreed that a deaf-mute who is the daughter of an Israelite<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, she is the daughter of a priest, she may partake of <i>terumah</i> by virtue of her father's privilege until she marries.</i> should not partake of <i>terumah</i> at all.
A non-priest is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>, as [Leviticus 22:10] states: "All non-priests<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">We have employed a literal translation, though somewhat clumsy in English, because of the exegesis of the verse later in the halachah.</i> shall not partake of the consecrated food."<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Negative Commandment 133) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 280) include this as one of 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> [This prohibition applies] even to a resident [worker] of the priest or his hired [worker], as [<i>ibid.</i>] states: "A resident [worker] of the priest or his hired [worker] shall not partake of the consecrated food."<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Negative Commandment 134) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 281) include this as one of 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> [The term] "resident [worker]" refers to one hired forever. [The term] "hired [worker]" refers to one hired for years.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Even when a priest is obligated to provide for his worker's sustenance, since the worker possesses an independent financial capacity, he is not considered as the priest's "financial acquisition" (see Halachah 1) and may not partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> A Hebrew servant is comparable to a resident [worker] or a hired [worker].<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Although a Hebrew servant is purchased by his master, he retains an independent financial capacity. Hence, even if he becomes <i>nirtzah</i> (see <i>Hilchot Avadim</i>, ch. 3), in which instance he must work for his master until the Jubilee year, he remains independent and may not partake of <i>terumah</i>.<br>We have followed the simple meaning of the Rambam's words which appear to interpret the verse as referring to an ordinary worker. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that in <i>Yevamot</i> 70a and other sources, our Sages interpret the verse as referring specifically to a Hebrew servant. Why then, he asks, does the Rambam single out a Hebrew servant? He explains that the Rambam is implying that there is no difference in this regard between a servant sold by the court and one who sells himself into slavery.</i><br>A woman of priestly lineage who is married to a non-priest is considered as a non-priest, as [implied by the wording] "all non-priests" - i.e., neither he nor his wife.
A non-priest who willfully partook of <i>terumah</i> - regardless of whether he was ritually pure or impure and regardless of whether [the <i>terumah</i>] was ritually pure or impure<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz notes that when a priest who is ritually impure partakes of <i>terumah</i> that is ritually impure, he is not liable for death. He asks: Why then is a non-priest liable for partaking of such <i>terumah</i>? He explains that for a priest who is holy by nature, impure <i>terumah</i> is considered defiled and he is not liable for partaking of it. For a non-priest, however, impure <i>terumah</i> is also considered holy and he is liable for partaking of it.</i> - is liable for death at the hand of heaven, as [<i>ibid.</i>:9] states: "They shall die because of it, for they have defiled it." He is liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the court must administer lashes if witnesses observed the transgression and administered a warning. And if they do administer lashes, the sinner's transgression is expiated and he does not receive punishment by the hand of heaven.</i> but does not make financial restitution for what he consumed, because [a transgressor] is never subjected to both lashes and a financial penalty.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle applying in many different contexts. See <i>Hilchot Na'arah Betulah</i> 1:11; <i>Hilchot Geneivah</i> 4:9; <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 16:12. The Radbaz notes that generally, whenever there is a financial penalty and the obligation for lashes, the person is given the financial penalty and freed of lashes. In this instance, the opposite is true. He is given lashes and freed of the financial penalty. The Radbaz explains that in this instance, the person was originally liable for death at the hand of heaven. Hence, it is inappropriate that the transgression be absolved through a mere financial payment.</i> If he partook [of <i>terumah</i>] inadvertently, [he must make restitution and] add a fifth, as [<i>ibid.</i>:14] states: "When a person eats sanctified food inadvertently, he should add a fifth."<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">One fifth of the new total; one fourth of the original amount.</i>
[Leviticus 22:12] states: "When the daughter of a priest will marry a non-priest, she should not partake of the sanctified <i>terumah</i>." There are two concepts included in this prohibition:<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This charge is considered as one of 613 mitzvot of the Torah by <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Negative Commandment 137) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 283) .</i><br>a) that if a woman engages in sexual relations with a man prohibited to her and thus become a <i>zonah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 18:1, the halachic definition of the term <i>zonah</i> is: "A Jewish woman who engaged in relations with a man she was forbidden to marry, violating a prohibition that is universally applicable, or a woman who engaged in relations with a <i>challal</i>."</i> or a <i>chalalah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The definition of the term <i>chalalah</i> is: "[A woman] born from [relations] forbidden to the priesthood. Similarly, any woman who is forbidden to the priesthood who engaged in relations with a priest becomes a <i>challalah</i>" (<i>ibid.</i> 19:1).</i> as we explained in <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i>, is forbidden to partake of the <i>terumot</i> forever,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz emphasizes that although she is prohibited to partake of <i>terumah</i>, she is not liable for death at God's hands for willfully doing so. Nor is she required to pay an additional fifth if she does so inadvertently.</i> as is the law with regard to any <i>challal</i>. For a <i>challal</i> is considered as a non-priest with regard to all matters;<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">None of the mitzvot and prohibitions associated with the priesthood apply to him. The Radbaz emphasizes that a <i>challal</i> is not entirely comparable to a non-priest, for a non-priest is liable for death at God's hand and a <i>challal is not subject to such a punishment.</i></i> and<br>b) that if she marries an Israelite, she is forbidden to partake of the portions of the sacrifices given the priests, the breast and the thigh forever,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in contrast to <i>terumah</i> as stated in the following halachah.</i> even if she is divorced or widowed.
She may, however, partake of <i>terumah</i> after [her husband] the Israelite divorces her or dies provided he did not leave any children that she bore, as [<i>ibid.</i>:13] states: "When the daughter of a priest will become a widow or a divorcee and she has no descendants,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">From that marriage.</i> she shall return to her father's home as in her youth. She may partake of the bread of her father."
According to the Oral Tradition,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Yevamot</i> 87a.</i> [that verse was interpreted] "from the bread," but not all the bread. She returns to partake of <i>terumah</i>, but not the breast and the thigh.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, if she willfully partakes of these foods even after being widowed or divorced, she is a transgressor and should be punished by lashes. See <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 19:4.</i>
[This concept applies] not only to the daughter of a priest, but to the daughter of a Levite or an Israelite. If she engage in relations with a man forbidden to her, since she became a <i>zonah</i>, she is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> forever, even if she has a descendant born to her from a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam explains in Halachah 12, as long as an Israelite woman who was married to a priest has a descendant from the priestly seed, she is permitted to continue partaking of <i>terumah</i> even though she is divorced or widowed. This halachah explains that there is an exception to that rule. If such a woman becomes a <i>zonah</i>, she can no longer partake of <i>terumah</i>.<br>The Radbaz maintains that such a woman also is not liable for death at the hand of God if she partakes of <i>terumah</i> willfully, nor an additional fifth if she does so inadvertantly.</i>
For this reason, a woman who was taken captive<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Whom we suspect engaged in relations, either willingly or unwillingly, with her captors and thus became a <i>zonah</i>. See the latter portion of <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i>, ch. 18.</i> may not partake of <i>terumah</i> even though she says that she was not defiled. Whenever the word of a captive woman who says that she was not defiled is accepted<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 18:21 which explains that if a woman says "I was taken captive, but I was not defiled" before witnesses testify to her being taken captive, her word is accepted.</i> or there is a witness<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>ibid.</i>:17 which states that in this instance, the testimony of one witness is acceptable. Moreover, the witness need not fit all the criteria that are usually required for witnesses in court.</i> [who testifies to this effect] and she is permitted to her husband,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he is a priest.</i> she is permitted to partake of <i>terumah</i>. When a woman engaged in sexual relations with an animal, she is not disqualified from the priesthood<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>ibid.</i>:1.</i> and may partake of <i>terumah</i>.
A daughter of an Israelite who has an offspring [she bore] from a priest may partake of <i>terumah</i> by virtue of her child. <sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Provided she does not marry an Israelite as stated in Halachah 17.</i> [This applies] whether the child is male or female or even a <i>tumtum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">A child whose genital area is covered by a mass of flesh and thus his gender cannot be determined.</i> or an <i>androgynus</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">A person with both male and female sexual organs.</i> Even the offspring of offspring until the end of time<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., her grandchildren or great-grandchildren who are descendants from a priest are alive even though her children themselves have died.</i> [enable her to partake of <i>terumah</i>], as [indicated by the prooftext]: "She has no descendants."<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the prooftext does not say, "She has no children" (Radbaz).</i>
Just as the descendants from an Israelite [born] to the daughter of a priest disqualify her [from partaking of <i>terumah</i>], so too, the descendants from a priest [born] to the daughter of an Israelite entitle her [to partake of <i>terumah</i>]. [This applies] even if the descendants are of blemished lineage.<br>What is implied? A daughter of an Israelite married a priest or the daughter of a priest married an Israelite and [that woman] gave birth to a daughter. A person who is an <i>ervah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Marriage between the two would be either adulterous or incestuous.</i> for the daughter entered into relations with her or [the daughter] married a <i>mamzer</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">A person of blemished lineage coming from either an adulterous or incestuous relationship.</i> [and the daughter gave birth to a baby which is a <i>mamzer</i>]. [Afterwards,] the daughter dies, but the <i>mamzer</i> remains alive.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Thus the offspring descendant that initially either enabled or disqualified a woman from partaking of <i>terumah</i> is no longer alive. Nevertheless, since there is a descendant of the relationship alive - even though the descendant is of blemished lineage - the woman's status remains the same as the Rambam concludes in the following halachah.</i>
If [the <i>mamzer's</i>] grandmother was the daughter of an Israelite who [married] a priest, she may partake of <i>terumah</i>. If she was the daughter of a priest who [married] an Israelite, she may not partake of <i>terumah</i>. Thus we learn that [the daughter of an Israelite] may partake of <i>terumah</i> by virtue of her descendants even though the lineage of that descendant is blemished, even though [the descendant] is not even [on the level of an ordinary] Israelite.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the version found in authentic manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The version found in the standard published text translates as "even if he is not Jewish" which is obviously incorrect as indicated by Halachah 16. See also Radbaz.</i> Needless to say that if a woman [who married a priest] has a daughter born to her by him - even if the daughter is married to an Israelite and even if she became a <i>challalah</i> - she may partake of <i>terumah</i> by virtue of the daughter of blemished lineage.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, as long as her husband, the priest, is alive, she may partake of <i>terumah</i> regardless. The entire issue of the status of her descendants arises only when her husband has died.</i>
Similarly, the daughter of a priest may not eat by virtue of her descendant from an Israelite husband, even if that descendant is a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, she remarries a priest she may partake of <i>terumah</i>, as stated in Halachah 18.</i><br>What is implied? A daughter of a priest married an Israelite and gave birth to a daughter that he conceived. That daughter married a priest and gave birth to a son that he conceived. [This son] is fitting to become a High Priest. He enables his mother<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Who is the daughter of an Israelite.</i> to partake of <i>terumah</i> and disqualifies his maternal grandmother [from doing so].<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance as well, obviously, as long as her husband, the Israelite, is alive, she is disqualified regardless. The entire issue of the status of her descendants arises only when her husband has died.</i> [This applies] even if [the priest's] mother dies. [The grandmother] can say: "[One should] not be like my grandson, the High Priest, who disqualifies me from partaking of <i>terumah</i>."
The descendants of a servant do not disqualify [a woman from partaking of <i>terumah</i>], nor do they enable [a woman to do so].<br>What is implied? The daughter of a priest married an Israelite or the daughter of an Israelite married a priest. A son was born to that woman. The son [then] went and became attached to a Canaanite maid-servant and conceived [a son] with her. [That son is] a servant.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 12:13.</i> [The following rules apply if the woman's son] dies and the servant remains alive: If the servant's paternal grandmother was the daughter of an Israelite who had married to a priest, she may not partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">For it is as if she has no offspring from her marriage to the priest.</i> If she was the daughter of a priest who had married an Israelite, she may partake [of <i>terumah</i>], because this offspring is not considered as a descendant, because the concept of parental lineage does not apply with regard to servants.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Yibbum ViChalitzah</i> 1:4 and <i>Hilchot Avadim</i> 9:3, among the implications of this concept is that we pay no attention to the parental lineage of a servant's father. Instead, it is as if he was conceived by his mother alone. There are other implications of this statement, as indicated by <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 15:6, <i>et al</i>.</i>
[The above concepts are also relevant in the following instance.] The daughter of an Israelite married a priest and he died. She had borne a son to him. Afterwards, she married an Israelite. [Hence,] she may not partake of <i>terumah</i>. If her Israelite [husband] died, but she bore him a son, she may not partake of <i>terumah</i> because of that son. If that son dies, she may partake of <i>terumah</i> by virtue of her first son.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Who is a priest.</i>
[Similar concepts apply when] the daughter of a priest marries an Israelite. [If] she bore him a son and then married a priest, she may partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">By virtue of her marriage to the priest.</i> If [her second husband] dies, but she had borne him a son, she may partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">By virtue of her son.</i> If her son conceived by the priest dies, she is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> because of her son conceived by [her first husband,] the Israelite. If her son conceived by the Israelite dies, she returns to her father's home as in her youth. She may partake of <i>terumah</i>, but not of the breast and thigh [of the peace offerings].<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachot 7-9.</i>
[Similar laws apply when] a daughter of an Israelite marries an Israelite first and has a son that she bore to him. If she marries a priest [after her first husband dies], she may partake of <i>terumah</i>. If [her second husband, the priest,] dies, but she bore him a son, she may partake [of <i>terumah</i>] by virtue of the son [she bore him]. For he enables her to partake of <i>terumah</i> as his father did.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, if her son conceived by the priest fathers children, she may continue to eat by virtue of them, even if her son dies. If, however, the son conceived by the priest dies childless (or he and his descendants die), she may no longer partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i>

Chapter 7

A priest who is ritually impure is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> whether it is ritually pure or ritually impure,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Negative Commandment 136) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 279) include this as one of 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> as [Leviticus 22:4] states: "Any person from the seed of Aaron who is afflicted with <i>tzaraat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tzara'at</i> is a physical affliction resembling, but not identical with leprosy, that comes as a result of gossip and imparts ritual impurity.</i> or is a <i>zav</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a physical affliction resembling, but not identical with gonorrhea that imparts ritual impurity.</i> may not partake of consecrated food." Which consecrated food may be eaten by all the descendants of Aaron, both male and female? We must say: <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">It does not refer to the breast and thigh of the peace offerings, for a woman of the priestly family who marries and is widowed or divorced may not partake of them (<i>Yevamot</i> 74b).</i><br>[Thus] any impure person who eats <i>terumah</i> that is ritually pure is liable for death at the hand of heaven. Therefore he is given lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If his transgression is observed by witnesses and they issued a warning. Whenever lashes are given on the earthly plane, the transgressor receives atonement and is no longer held liable for death by God.</i> as [<i>ibid.</i>:9] states: "And you shall protect My charge and not bear sin because of it [and die because of it]."<br>When an impure person partakes of <i>terumah</i> that is ritually impure, he does not receive lashes, although he transgresses a negative commandment,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">From the fact that <i>Sanhedrin</i> 83a,b emphasizes that an impure priest is not liable for death for partaking of impure <i>terumah</i>, the Rambam concludes that a transgression is involved.</i> for [impure <i>terumah</i>] is not holy.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Its impurity nullifies its holiness. See Chapter 6, Halachah 6 and notes, however.</i>
Impure [priests who have immersed to purify themselves] may not partake of <i>terumah</i> until the sun sets and three stars appear afterwards<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that this is approximately 20 minutes after sunset. This applies in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. In different latitudes, the duration of this time period varies.<br>This represents a stringency over other laws where ritual purity is required. In many such instances, as soon as one immerses himself, his purification is completed and no further wait is necessary. See <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 7:11.</i> [on the day the impure person immerses himself], as [implied by <i>ibid.</i>:7]: "And the sun will set and he will become pure"<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The simple meaning of the verse is that the person's purification process is not completed until the sun sets. The rules of Biblical exegesis, however, leave room for the meaning mentioned by the Rambam.</i> - until the sky will become pure of the light - "afterwards he may partake of the consecrated foods."
A priest who is ritually pure who ate <i>terumah</i> that is ritually impure is not liable for lashes, because [the prohibition is the result of] a positive commandment. For the verse states: "Afterwards he may partake of the consecrated foods."<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The Rabaz emphasizes that with regard to partaking of the sacrifices, in certain instances, an impure priest must also bring a sacrifice to mark his immersion from impurity.</i> [Implied is that,] after he becomes purified, he may partake of food that is in a state of holiness. Food which is impure, by contrast, he should not eat, even though he becomes purified. A prohibition that stems from a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I,e., as in the case at hand, there is no verse that says "Do not eat impure <i>terumah</i>," but from the positive commandment regarding pure <i>terumah</i>, we can conclude that partaking of the impure <i>terumah</i> is prohibited.</i> has the status of a positive commandment.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, its violation is not punishable by lashes.</i>
When [a priest] was partaking of <i>terumah</i> and he feels his limbs shudder to ejaculate.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Which would render him ritually impure.</i> He should hold his member<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">For he does not become ritually impure until the semen actually emerges and in this way, he could swallow the <i>terumah</i> first (<i>Niddah</i> 40a).</i> and swallow the <i>terumah</i>.
We may immerse a deaf-mute or intellectually or emotionally unstable [priest] and feed him <i>terumah</i> after nightfall on that day.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">These individuals are not responsible for their conduct. Nevertheless, their priesthood is intact. Hence, we immerse them to purify them and then enable them to partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> We guard them to make sure that they do not sleep after immersing themselves, because if they sleep, they are [considered] impure for perhaps they had a seminal emission<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., because of the doubt, we considered them impure.</i> unless a copper container is made for them.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Which would allow a seminal emission to be detected. Were the container to be made of cloth, the semen could be absorbed (Radbaz).</i>
Camel-riders are forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> until they immerse themselves and [wait] until nightfall, because we presume that they are ritually impure. [The rationale is that] the physical stimulation<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The literal meaning of the Hebrew is "warmth."</i> produced by riding on the flesh of a camel produces a drop of semen.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See the gloss of the Radbaz who states that the Rambam's wording implies that this ruling applies only when one rides bareback [see <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Even HaEzer</i> 23:6)]. He also explains why it applies only to camel-riders and not those who ride on other animals.</i>
When a woman engages in intimacy, she may immerse herself and partake of <i>terumah</i> in the evening,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., intimate relations cause both the man and the woman to become impure. To emerge from that impurity, they must each immerse in the <i>mikvah</i> and wait until nightfall (see Leviticus 15:18 .</i> provided she did not turn over during intimacy.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Intimate relations can cause a woman to become impure for two reasons:<br>a) as stated above, the very act of intimacy brings about ritual impurity;<br>b) she comes in contact with semen and touching semen makes one ritually impure (<i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i> 5:9).<br>As long as the semen is in the woman's vagina, it is not considered to have touched her, because it is touching her body internally, not externally. If, however, it flows outside her vagina and touches her externally, she does become impure. According to the Rambam, if the woman does not turn over in intimate relations, the semen will remain in her vagina and not flow outside. Hence, after immersing herself and waiting until nightfall, she may partake of <i>terumah</i>. If, however, she turned over in the midst of intimacy, the semen will flow out. Moreover, it is possible that it will not flow out immediately, but will do so over the course of the coming days. Now, semen is considered to remain viable until the third day after intimacy. Hence for three days, the woman must consider the possibility that the semen flowed out from her body and made her impure. Hence, she cannot immerse herself in the <i>mikvah</i> to regain ritual purity until three days have passed.<br>The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and maintains that even if the woman did not turn over during intimacy, as soon as she arises from bed, it is possible that semen will flow out from her vagina and touch her body. He quotes <i>Niddah</i> 41b in support of his explanation. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggest that the Rambam had a different text of that Talmudic passage and they validate his ruling.</i> If she turned over during intimacy she is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> for a duration of three days. For it is impossible that she will not discharge [semen] and become impure,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Because of it touching her body.</i> as will be explained in the appropriate place.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:11. There the Rambam states that the intent is not that semen remains viable for three full days, but that it can remain viable until the third day after relations.</i>
Since the primary obligation of <i>terumah</i> [separated] in the Diaspora is Rabbinic in origin,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 1, Halachah 1, according to Scriptural Law, the requirement to separate <i>terumah</i> applies only in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. The early Sages ordained that it should be observed in certain neighboring countries.</i> it is forbidden only to a priest who becomes ritually impure because of a discharge from his body: i.e., men who had a seminal emission, <i>zavim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a physical affliction resembling, but not identical with gonorrhea that imparts ritual impurity.</i> <i>zavot</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Women who become impure because they experienced menstrual bleeding at times other than her ordinary cycle.</i> <i>niddot</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Women who become impure because they experienced ordinary menstrual bleeding.</i> and women who give birth.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Who becomes impure as explained in <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i>, ch. 10. Since the obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> in these lands is only Rabbinic in origin, the Sages were lenient regarding the restrictions incumbent on those who partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> All of these individuals may partake [of <i>terumah</i>] after they immersed even if they did not [wait] until nightfall.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to <i>terumah</i> prescribed by Scriptural Law when such a wait is necessary, as stated in Halachah 2. The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam concerning this point and refers to <i>Bechorot</i> 27a in support of his position. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> offer different explanations of that passage that support the Rambam's interpretation.</i> Those who, by contrast, became impure because of contact with a source of ritual impurity - whether contact with a human corpse, a form of impurity which is impossible to purge in the present age<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For to purge the impurity resulting from contact with a human corpse, it is necessary to have the ashes from a Red Heifer sprinkled upon one's body and those ashes are not available in the present age.</i> or contact with [the corpse of] a crawling animal - need not immerse themselves [to partake of] <i>terumah</i> separated in the Diaspora.
For this reason, a male priest who is a minor who has not yet had a seminal emission or a female of the priestly family who has never experienced menstrual bleeding may partake of [<i>terumah</i> from the Diaspora] at all times without undergoing an inspection, for the presumption that they did not have a discharge that would render them ritually impure applies to them.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Once a male or a female attains majority, by contrast, we assume that they have become impure and they must immerse themselves before partaking of <i>terumah</i> (Radbaz).</i><br>A <i>metzora</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>metzora</i> is somebody who has been afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i>. <i>Tzara'at</i> is a physical affliction resembling, but not identical with leprosy, that comes as a result of gossip and imparts ritual impurity.</i> is considered as one who had a discharge that renders him impure,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For his impurity is also a result of a physical condition (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> provided he is declared impure by a priest whose lineage is established.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">For a person afflicted by <i>tzara'at</i> does not become ritually impure until a priest declares him so (<i>Hilchot Tuma'at Tzara'at</i> 9:2). With regard to the establishment of a priest's lineage, see the notes to Chapter 6, Halachah 2.<br>The Radbaz adds that the reason the impurity of <i>tzara'at</i> is not observed in the present age is that we have no priests whose lineage is established to declare people ritually impure. The physical conditions for the impurity may exist in the present age.</i>
A priest who is uncircumcised is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> according to Scriptural Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz writes that this prohibition applies even if he is uncircumcised with the Torah's permission, e.g., his brothers died because of circumcision, in which instance, caution is shown before circumcising him.</i> [This concept is derived as follows: Leviticus 22:10] speaks of a resident worker and a hired worker with regard to [partaking of] <i>terumah</i> and [Exodus 12:45] speaks of a resident worker and a hired worker with regard to [partaking of] the Paschal sacrifice. Just as with regard to the Paschal sacrifice, the resident worker and the hired worker spoken about refer to those who are uncircumcised and who are forbidden [to partake of] it,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i> 9:7.</i> so too, with regard to <i>terumah</i>, we learn that a resident worker and a hired worker who are uncircumcised are forbidden [to partake of] it. <sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (Negative Commandment 135) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 282) include this as one of 613 mitzvot of the Torah. There is, however, a slight difficulty with this classification. The Rambam maintains that any commandment that is derived through one of the Thirteen Principles of Biblical Exegesis does not have the status of a Scriptural command, nor is such a prohibition punishable by lashes (<i>Sefer HaMitzvot,</i> General Principle 2) and as the Rambam states here, there is no direct commandment prohibiting an uncircumcised person from partaking of <i>terumah</i>. Instead, it is derived by our Sages (<i>Yevamot</i> 70a) through an association of verses (<i>gezeirah shaveh</i>). Hence, seemingly, it should not be placed in that category. The Rambam notes this difficulty when discussing this mitzvah in <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> and states that since our Sages mentioned that this mitzvah was of Scriptural origin, he considered it as having that status.</i> If such a person partakes [of <i>terumah</i>], he is liable for lashes according to Scriptural Law.<br>A person who extends his foreskin<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one was originally circumcised, but underwent an operation so that it would appear that he was not circumcised. In the Hellenist and Roman periods, there were Jews who underwent such operations so that they would resemble non-Jews.</i> is permitted to partake of <i>terumah</i> even though he appears uncircumcised.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Scriptural Law, his original circumcision was effective and he can never revert to being uncircumcised.</i> According to Rabbinic Law, however, he must be circumcised again until he appears circumcised.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz writes that the intent is not that he is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> until he is circumcised again. Instead, the intent is that he is advised to undergo the operation and not to partake of <i>terumah</i> until he does so. The <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> and the <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>), however, say that such an individual is prohibited by the Rabbis from partaking of <i>terumah</i>.</i>
One who is born circumcised may partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Although it is necessary to draw a drop of blood from such a person's organ (<i>Hilchot Milah</i> 1:11), it cannot be said that such a person is uncircumcised.
