Hence, he is permitted to partake of <i>terumah</i> (Radbaz).</i> A <i>tumtum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">A child whose genital area is covered by a mass of flesh and thus his gender cannot be determined.</i> may not partake of <i>terumah</i> for there is a doubt whether he is uncircumcised.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Since his gender is undetermined, we do not know whether or not he must be circumcised.</i> An <i>androgynus</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">A person with both male and female sexual organs.</i> should be circumcised and then he may partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Although there are many unresolved questions about such a person's status vis-à-vis gender, there is no difficulty in him partaking of <i>terumah</i> for both males and females of the priestly family are permitted to partake of it.</i>
Although an uncircumcised priest and all those who are impure are forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>, their wives and their servants may partake [of it].<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">They are prohibited from partaking of <i>terumah</i> due to outside factors. Their membership in the priestly family is, however, intact. Hence, their wives and servants may partake of <i>terumah</i> (Radbaz).</i>
[The following laws apply to a priest] who has maimed testicles or a severed member. They and their servants may partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Although their wounds prevent such priests from serving at the Altar, they are still members of the priestly family. Hence, they and their servants may partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> Their wives may not partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Deuteronomy 23:2 and <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i>, ch. 16, a person with such wounds is forbidden to marry a natural born Jewess. Hence, if a priest is intimate with his wife after suffering such wounds, she becomes a <i>zonah</i> and a <i>chalalah</i> and hence, is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> If [such a priest] was not intimate with his wife after suffering these wounds, [his wife] may partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since they were not intimate, she has not become a <i>chalalah</i> and may partake of <i>terumah</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that this refers to an instance where the couple were married and then he suffered this wound. If, however, a priest suffers such a wound and then consecrates a woman, she may not partake of <i>terumah</i>. See the notes to the following halachah.</i> Similarly, if he married the descendent of converts, she may partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Ordinarily, a priest cannot marry a convert or the descendant of a convert. In this instance, however, because of his injury, the full measure of the holiness of the priesthood does not rest upon him and he may marry such a woman (<i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 16:1). Once he marries such a woman, she is entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i>, because she is the wife of a priest.</i>
When a priest with maimed testicles consecrates the daughter of a priest, she may not partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since this woman is prepared to engage in intimate relations that are forbidden to her, our Sages prohibited her from partaking of <i>terumah</i> even though she had not actually engaged in such relations. Moreover, she is no longer permitted to partake of <i>terumah</i> by virtue of her being a member of the priestly family, because she was consecrated, as stated in Chapter 8, Halachah 1.</i> [When a priest is] a eunuch who was brought to this state through natural means,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 2:14 for a more precise definition of the term used by the Rambam. As stated in <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 16:9, such a person is permitted to marry a native-born Jewess. Hence, there is no difficulty with the wife of a priest with such a condition partaking of <i>terumah</i>.</i> he, his wife, and his servants may partake of <i>terumah</i>. [When a priest is] a <i>tumtum</i> or an <i>androgynus</i>, their servants may partake of <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For in this context, there is no difference between them and the servants of an ordinary priest.</i> but not their wives.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">For there is an unresolved doubt if their marriages are acceptable. Needless to say, if a <i>tumtum</i> undergoes an operation and it is determined that he is a male, his wife may partake of <i>terumah</i> even if he consecrated her before the operation.</i>
When a [priest who was] a deaf-mute, a mentally or emotionally unstable person, or a minor purchased servants, they may not partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">These individuals are considered as not being responsible for their actions and their acquisitions are not valid. Hence, the servants they purchase by themselves are not their property according to Scriptural Law and may not partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> If, however, the local court or their guardian purchases servants for them or they acquire them by inheritance, [the servants] may partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Because the acquisitions are binding and they have become the property of a priest.</i>
When an <i>androgynus</i> [of the priestly family] engages in intimacy - whether through anal intercourse<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The literal translation of the Rambam's words would be "as a male would be the object of intimacy."</i> or vaginal intercourse - with a person who is disqualified from partaking of <i>terumah</i>, he is disqualified from partaking of <i>terumah</i> as a woman would be.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">For such relations would cause a woman to be considered as a <i>chalalah</i>. Since there is an unresolved question regarding the gender of an <i>androgynus</i>, we are stringent and consider the possibility that he is a female. And a female is disqualified from <i>terumah</i> if she engages in intimacy that would render her a <i>chalalah</i> regardless of whether it is vaginal or anal intercourse (Radbaz).</i> His servants also may not partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">For their right to partake of <i>terumah</i> is dependent on that of their owner.</i><br>Similarly, if he engages in intimacy with another <i>adrogynus</i> with whom relations would disqualify a woman from partaking of <i>terumah</i>, he is disqualified. Neither he nor his servants may partake of <i>terumah</i>. [The latter law] applies when he engages in intimacy through vaginal intercourse.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">For such relations would render a woman a <i>chalahah</i> and, because of the unresolved question regarding the status of the <i>androgynus</i>, we apply that stringency to him as well.</i> If, however, he is intimate through anal intercourse,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The literal translation of the Rambam's words would be "as a male would be the object of intimacy."</i> [the above does not apply], because a male does not disqualify another male from priestly [privileges].<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">A male priest does not become a <i>chalal</i> because he entered into a forbidden sexual relationship.<br>The Ra'avad and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> question the Rambam's ruling, noting that, seemingly, the person should be disqualified from partaking of <i>terumah</i> based on the same rationale as the first clause of the halachah: i.e., that the <i>androgynus</i> also has a feminine dimension and that feminine dimension will be disqualified through anal intercourse. Hence, they ask: Why does the Rambam only view this relationship as intimacy between males and hence, not able to disqualify the <i>androgynus</i>? Why not see it as a relation between a male and a female? Among the explanations given is that since both of the <i>androgynies</i> are engaging in relations as males, it is not appropriate to deem one a male and one a female.</i>
When a servant belongs to partners and one of them is a priest that does not entitle others to partake of <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (the conclusion of tractate <i>Terumot</i>) states that for a servant to partake of <i>terumah</i>, he must belong to priests entirely. If an Israelite has even a one-hundredth share in the ownership of the servant, he may not partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> this servant is forbidden to partake [of <i>terumah</i>]. Whenever [the ownership of a servant] entitles him to partake of <i>terumah</i>, it also entitles him to partake of the breast and the thigh [of the peace sacrifices].<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Which may be eaten by all the members of the priest's household.</i>
When a daughter of an Israelite marries a priest and brings servants [to his domain] - regardless of whether they are classified as <i>nichsei milog</i> or <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">When a woman marries, her husband receives the right to benefit from her property. As explained in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i>, ch. 16, there are two arrangements under which a husband is allowed to benefit from his wife's property:<br><i>nichsei milog</i> - in this instance, in the event of divorce or the husband's death, the property reverts to the ownership of the woman according to its value at that time, regardless of the profit or loss she suffers;<br><i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> - in this instance, the property is evaluated at the time of marriage; in the event of divorce or the husband's death, the woman receives that amount, regardless of the worth of the property at that time.<br>Servants can be classified in either of these categories. In both instances, they have the right to partake of <i>terumah</i>. With regard to servants who are <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i>, they are the husband's (the priest's) acquisitions, for it is as if he acquired them when he accepted financial responsibility for their worth. With regard to those who are <i>nichsei milog</i>, they are "the acquisition of his acquisition," i.e., property belonging to his wife whom he has acquired. See Halachah 20.</i> - they may partake [of <i>terumah</i>]. Similarly, when the servants of priests purchase servants or the servants of a priest's wife purchase servants, they may partake of <i>terumah</i>, as [intimated by Leviticus 22:11]: "[When a priest] will purchase a soul, the acquisition of his money, [he shall partake of it]." Implied is even an acquisition of an acquisition.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., servants acquired by servants who were acquired by the priest. The inference is derived based on the fact that the verse contains two derivatives of the word <i>koneh</i> - "acquire."</i> An acquisition of [a priest] who is permitted to partake of <i>terumah</i> may entitle others to partake [of <i>terumah</i>]. An acquisition who does not partake of <i>terumah</i> may not entitle others to partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">When the wife of a priest is not entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i> (see Halachot 20-22), her servants may also not partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i>
When the daughter of a priest marries an Israelite and brings servants [to his domain] - regardless of whether they are classified as <i>nichsei milog</i> or <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> - they may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The servants who are <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> certainly may not partake of <i>terumah</i> for they are now the property of an Israelite. Even the servants who are <i>nichsei milog</i> may not partake of <i>terumah</i>, for at this time, their owner - the Israelite's wife - is disqualified from partaking of <i>terumah</i>.</i>
[The following laws apply when] a widow marries a High Priest or a divorcee or a woman who underwent <i>chalitzah</i> - whether the daughter of a priest or an Israelite - marries an ordinary priest<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">These three types of relationships are forbidden, the first two by Scriptural Law, the latter by Rabbinic Law. Thus the woman will become a <i>chalalah</i> and hence, forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>. Since she is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>, her servants are likewise forbidden.</i> or [a priest] marries another one of the women with whom marriage is prohibited [by Scriptural Law].<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a woman who is forbidden to him because of one of the prohibitions that apply to all Jews, even non-priests. Nevertheless, if he engages in relations with that woman, she becomes a <i>zonah</i> and thus prohibited against partaking of <i>terumah</i>. In this instance as well, since she is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>, her servants are likewise forbidden.</i> If she brings servants [to his domain] which are classified as <i>nichsei milog</i> or <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> [to his domain], those classified as <i>nichsei milog</i> may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>],<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">For they are the woman's property, it is just that they are "on loan" to her husband, as it were.</i> even though he is required to provide for their sustenance. Those classified as <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> may partake of <i>terumah</i>, for they are the husband's property. If [a priest] married a <i>shniyah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">A relatively distant female relative with whom our Sages prohibited marriage as a safeguard against the Scriptural prohibitions of incest. See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 1:6.</i> she may partake [of <i>terumah</i>],<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Scriptural Law, she is the priest's wife and relations with him do not disqualify her, neither as a <i>zonah</i> or a <i>chalalah</i>.</i> but her servants who are <i>nichsei milog</i> may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Seemingly, since such a woman has the right to partake of <i>terumah</i>, her servants should also be so entitled. The Radbaz explains that since her marriage is not sanctioned by Rabbinic Law, her husband is under no obligation to sustain her. Hence, her servants do not receive the right to partake of <i>terumah</i>. Those servants that are <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> are permitted to partake of <i>terumah</i> according to all authorities, for they are her husband's acquisitions as explained above.</i>
When the daughter of a priest who is a widow is consecrated by a High Priest or one who is divorced is consecrated by an ordinary priest, they may not partake of <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., were it not for the reason stated by the Rambam, they would be permitted to partake of <i>terumah</i> by virtue of their membership in the Priestly family.</i> because they are designated for intimate relations that are forbidden according to Scriptural Law. Similarly, if they enter the <i>chupah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Entering the <i>chupah</i> means entering a private place with her husband. This is the act that constitutes the beginning of the second phase of the marriage relationship, <i>nissuin</i>.</i> without being consecrated, they may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>]. For [entering into] the <i>chupah</i> disqualifies them.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Even though a woman enters the <i>chupah</i> without consecration, that act causes her to be considered as designated for intimate relations that are forbidden.</i><br>If such a woman is widowed or divorced after consecration [but before marriage], she returns to suitability and may partake of <i>terumah</i>. If, however, she had married, she may not partake of <i>terumah</i>, because she already became a <i>chalalah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Through intimate relations with her husband.</i>
When the husband of the daughter of a priest who is himself a priest dies and she falls before <i>yevamim</i>, one of which is a <i>challal</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., her husband, the priest, had several brothers, one of whom was born from a relationship forbidden to the priesthood, and thus had the status of a <i>challal</i>.</i> she may not partake of <i>terumah</i> because of her connection to the <i>challal</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Since she could be married by the <i>challal</i>, she is disqualified even though she is a member of the priestly family.</i> [This applies] even if one of the acceptable [brothers] issued a <i>mamar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>maamar</i> is a Rabbinic institution in which a <i>yevam</i> declares his intention to marry his brother's childless widow by consecrating her through giving her money (<i>Hilchot Yibbum</i> 2:1).</i> to her, for a <i>maamar</i> does not constitute a complete acquisition of a <i>yevamah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">If another brother would perform <i>yibbum</i> with the widow, he would become her husband despite the <i>maamar</i> given by his brother. Thus the connection to the <i>yevam</i> who is a <i>challal</i> is still intact.</i>
[Although a <i>yavam</i> who is] a priest gives a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., instead of performing <i>chalitzah</i> which would absolve the connection between him and his <i>yevamah</i>, he gave her a bill of divorce. According to Scriptural Law, the bill of divorce is not effective at all. Nevertheless, according to Rabbinic Law, it disqualifies the woman from carrying out <i>yibbum</i> with any of the brothers (<i>Hilchot Yibbum VeChalitzah</i> 5:1).</i> to his <i>yevamah</i>, [despite the fact that] she becomes forbidden to him, she still remains connected to him.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Scriptural Law, their connection is still intact.</i> Hence, she is entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i>. [She is not disqualified,] because she is designated for relations that are forbidden [only] by Rabbinic decree, since a bill of divorce disqualifies a <i>yevamah</i> only according to Rabbinic decree.<br>Similarly, the daughter of a priest who underwent the rite of <i>chalitzah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">And thus became forbidden to a priest by Rabbinic decree.</i> or is a <i>shniyah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">A relatively distant female relative with whom our Sages prohibited marriage as a safeguard against the Scriptural prohibitions of incest. See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 1:6.</i> who became consecrated to a priest may partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Because she is a member of the priestly family. In contrast to the women mentioned in Halachah 21, these women are designated for relations that are forbidden only by Rabbinic decree. Hence, they are not disqualified.<br>Were the <i>yevamah</i> to be the daughter of an Israelite, she would not be permitted to partake of <i>terumah</i> despite the fact that she is bound to a priest through the obligation of <i>yibbum</i> (Radbaz).</i> [Also,] when an ordinary priest marries an <i>ailonot</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">A women whose female sexual characteristics are underdeveloped.</i> she may partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Although the priest may divorce her because she cannot have children, until he does so, he may be intimate with her. Hence, she is entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i>

Chapter 8

A fetus, a <i>yavam</i>, consecration, a deaf-mute, and a child of nine years old disqualify [a woman] from partaking of <i>terumah</i>, but do not entitle her to partake [of it].<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is explained in the halachot that follow.</i>
What is meant [by the above statement] with regard to a fetus? A daughter of an Israelite who was impregnated by a priest<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Without being married to the priest or after being widowed from him.</i> is not entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i> by virtue of the fetus.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as soon as the baby is born, the mother is entitled to eat by virtue of her child. Until it is born, however, the restrictions apply.</i> When the daughter of a priest, by contrast, was impregnated by an Israelite,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Without being married to the priest or after being widowed from him.</i> she is prohibited against partaking of <i>terumah</i> because of the fetus. [This is derived from Leviticus 22:13:] "And she shall return to her father's home as in her maidenhood." This excludes a pregnant woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Yevamot</i> 67b) explains that a pregnant woman is excluded, because she does not resemble a maiden.</i>
When an Israelite has relations with a daughter of a priest,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Without being married to the priest or after being widowed from him.</i> we do not suspect that she became pregnant. Instead, she may immerse herself [in the <i>mikveh</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">To purify herself from the impurity she contracted because of the relations in which she engaged (see Leviticus 15:18 . Note, however, Chapter 7, Halachah 7, and notes, from which it is apparent that there are times when a woman will have to wait longer.</i> and partake [of <i>terumah</i>] in the evening. If she was married to an Israelite and her husband died, she may immerse herself and partake of <i>terumah</i> in the evening [and continue doing so] for 40 days.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For even if she became pregnant, the pregnancy is not significant as the Rambam continues to explain. Hence, after 40 days passed, she should refrain from partaking of <i>terumah</i> until it is clear that she is not pregnant.</i> If her fetus is recognized [afterwards], retroactively, her [actions] are objectionable<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">She is required to make restoration of the <i>terumah</i> and add a fifth of its value as atonement.</i> from the fortieth day onward. For, throughout the forty days, the embryo is not considered as a fetus, merely as water.
When a daughter of an Israelite was married to a priest and he died, leaving her pregnant,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Without any other children.</i> her servants<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">She herself may also not partake of <i>terumah</i>, as stated in Halachah 2. This halachah is speaking about her servants that are <i>nichsei tzon barzel</i> and thus belong to the heirs of her husband's estate. Until the fetus is born, it is not a significant entity and the servants that belong to it do not have the right to partake of <i>terumah</i> because of it.</i> should not partake of <i>terumah</i> because of the fetus. For only a child that was born entitles others to partake [of <i>terumah</i>]. One which is not born does not entitle others to partake [of <i>terumah</i>]. Therefore if the fetus was a <i>challal</i> [and the woman had other acceptable children, the fetus] does not disqualify the servants from partaking of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam concerning this point and maintains that the right of the fetus to the servants is significant even before the fetus is born. Hence, he disqualifies them from partaking of <i>terumah</i>. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> offer interpretations of the relevant Talmudic portions that substantiate the Rambam's ruling.</i> Instead, they may partake of it by virtue of his brothers who are acceptable priests until this <i>challal</i> is born, at which point, the servants become forbidden to partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Until the estate is divided and each brother receives his share.</i>
What is meant [by the initial statement] with regard to a <i>yavam</i>? When a daughter of an Israelite is under the obligation to perform <i>yibbum</i> with a priest,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Regardless of the age of the prospective <i>yavam</i>.</i> she may not partake of <i>terumah</i>, for [<i>ibid.</i>:11] states: "When a priest will purchase a soul" and this [<i>yavam</i>] has not acquired [the woman] yet.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Although she was married to the brother of the <i>yavam</i>, the <i>yavam</i> does not acquire her until he performs <i>yibbum</i>. The Radbaz emphasizes that this ruling applies even if the <i>yavam</i> gave her a <i>ma'amar</i>, designating her as his wife.</i><br>When the daughter of a priest is under the obligation to perform <i>yibbum</i> with an Israelite, she is forbidden [to partake of <i>terumah</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Which, were she not obligated to her <i>yavam</i>, she could partake of by virtue of her membership in the priestly family.</i> because of her <i>yavam</i>, as [implied by the verse:] "And she shall return to her father's home as in her maidenhood." This excludes a woman under obligation to perform <i>yibbum</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>loc. cit.</i> 67b) explains that such a woman is excluded, because she may not return to her father's home.</i>
When a <i>yavam</i> who is a priest engages in relations with his <i>yevamah</i> by force or without intent or he merely uncovers her and does not complete the act of intercourse,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 1:10, the term "uncover" is of Biblical origin (Leviticus 20:18  and refers to the insertion of the corona alone in the woman's vaginal channel. "Completing relations" refers to the insertion of the entire male organ.</i> he acquires [his <i>yevamah</i>], as stated in <i>Hilchot Yibbum</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Yibbum VeChalitzah</i> 2:3.</i> Nevertheless, he does not entitle her to partake of <i>terumah</i> until he completes the act of intercourse with her consent.<br>When does the above apply? When [the <i>yevamah</i>] was widowed after consecration [alone].<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">And thus was not yet entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i> by virtue of her initial marriage.</i> If, however, she had been married, since she had been partaking of <i>terumah</i> previously,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., during her first marriage.</i> she is entitled to partake of it by virtue of [the inferior] acts of intimacy mentioned above.
What is meant [by the initial statement] with regard to a consecration? When a daughter of a priest is consecrated by an Israelite, she is forbidden to partake [of <i>terumah</i>], for he has already acquired her. When the daughter of an Israelite has been consecrated by a priest, she may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>] until she enters the <i>chupah</i>, lest she give <i>terumah</i> to the members of her father's household, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 6, Halachah 3, explains that the Sages forbade such a woman from partaking of <i>terumah</i> for this reason. According to Scriptural Law, she is entitled to partake of it.</i>
When an Israelite tells the daughter of a priest: "Behold, you are consecrated to me after 30 days," she may partake [of <i>terumah</i>] for these 30 days,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">By virtue of her ancestral right.</i> for the consecration has not taken effect yet.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">When a woman's husband words his statement in that manner, the consecration does not take effect until the 30 days pass (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 7:10).</i> If, however, he told her: "You are consecrated to me from the present and after 30 days,"<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, the wording he uses is confusing and there is a doubt whether the consecration takes effect immediately or only after 30 days (<i>ibid.</i>:12).</i> she is forbidden to partake [of <i>terumah</i>] immediately.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the doubt regarding her status.</i> Similar laws apply with regard to other conditional agreements of this type.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the man consecrated the woman conditionally, but stated that when the condition is fulfilled, the consecration would take effect from the present time (see <i>ibid.</i>: 6:16).</i>
What is meant [by the initial statement] with regard to a deaf-mute? When a daughter of a priest is married to an Israelite who is a deaf-mute, she may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>], since he acquires her by virtue of the ordinance of our Sages who instituted marriage for him.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">According to Scriptural Law, a deaf-mute does not have the capacity to consecrate a woman as his wife. Our Sages, however, desired that these individuals be given a chance to enjoy the basics of family life (see <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 4:9, 11:4).</i> When a daughter of an Israelite marries a priest who is a deaf-mute, she may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>], because he does not acquire [her] according to Scriptural Law,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">And the acquisition ordained by our Sages does not have the power to supercede Scriptural Law.</i> because he is not of [adequate] intellectual capacity.
[When a woman] was consecrated by a priest of ordinary mental capacity, but he did not marry her until he became a deaf-mute, she may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For her consecration did not entitle her to partake of <i>terumah</i> and her marriage to a deaf-mute (through <i>yibbum</i>) does not give her this right either.</i> [In the above situation, if after consecrating a woman, a priest] died and she fell before a <i>yavam</i> who is a deaf-mute, she may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, the <i>yibbum</i> performed by the deaf-mute creates a marriage-bond that is effective according to Scriptural Law. Nevertheless, the relations with which he engages with her resemble the inadvertent relations mentioned in Halachah 6 and hence do not entitle the woman to partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> If, however, he married her when he was of ordinary mental capacity and then became a deaf-mute, she may [continue] to partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">For she is married according to Scriptural Law.</i> If he died and she fell before a <i>yavam</i> who is a deaf-mute and he performs <i>yibbum</i> with her, she may [also continue to] partake [of <i>terumah</i>]. [The rationale for the leniency is] that she was partaking of <i>terumah</i> beforehand.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">And the relations with the <i>yavam</i> restore her to her previous status.</i><br>When the wife of [a priest who is] a deaf-mute bears him a child, she may partake [of <i>terumah</i>] by virtue of her child.
What is meant [by the initial statement] with regard to a child of nine years old?<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam speaks about a boy "nine years and one day old." Nevertheless, the intent is that he has completed nine years of life and entered his tenth year. Even a portion of a day is considered as an entire day. Thus as soon as a child reaches his ninth birthday, he is nine years and one day old. Hence, for the purpose of brevity, we have translated as above.</i> [When a daughter of a priest] engaged in relations with a child of nine years old who is forbidden to her, since sexual relations in which he engages are significant,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Although such a child is a minor and not entirely sexually potent, at this age, he is somewhat physically mature. Hence, relations in which he engage are significant to a certain extent. See <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 11:3; <i>Hilchot Yibbum</i> 1:16; <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 1:13.</i> she is disqualified from the priesthood and forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>, because she becomes a <i>zonah</i> or a <i>chalalah</i>, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 6, Halachah 7.</i> [This applies] even if [the child] is sexually impotent.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., and he inserted his organ in hers without an erection. The Radbaz explains that this is the fundamental new concept taught by this halachah. Otherwise, it would be obvious that since relations with this child are significant and they are forbidden to this woman, they disqualify her from partaking of <i>terumah</i>.</i><br>When a daughter of an Israelite marries a priest who is nine years old, even though the relations in which he engages are significant, she is not entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i> because of [this] child, because he cannot acquire her [as his wife] until he reaches majority.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">For a male cannot acquire a wife until he reaches majority. Such a marriage is not significant even according to Rabbinic Law (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 11:6-7).</i><br>If there is a doubt whether [a child forbidden to a daughter of a priest] is nine years old<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, she would be forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> or not,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, she would be permitted.</i> she is forbidden to partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the doubt.</i> [This same ruling applies] if she married a boy thirteen years old and there was a question whether he manifested physical signs of maturity or not.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">For a child does not reach maturity - and hence, a marriage that he enters is not significant - until he manifests bodily signs, two pubic hairs for a male, that he has reached maturity. Attaining the age of maturity is itself not sufficient (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 2:10-11).<br>Seemingly, this clause is also speaking about an instance where the daughter of a priest is marrying a youth who disqualifies her and the question is whether he is of sufficient physical maturity for the marriage to be significant. In that instance, however, it would be proper to speak of consecration, not marriage. For this reason, Rav Yosef Korcus defines this clause as referring to a daughter of an Israelite who is marrying a priest and the question is whether the marriage entitles her to partake of <i>terumah</i> or not.</i>
All of the relationships [mentioned above] that do not entitle a woman to partake of <i>terumah</i> prescribed by Scriptural Law also do not entitle her to partake of <i>terumah</i> prescribed by Rabbinic Law. [This is] a decree lest they have them partake of <i>terumah</i> prescribed by Scriptural Law.
The [following] individuals neither disqualify [the daughter of a priest from partaking of <i>terumah</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">If the individual involved is an Israelite fit to marry this woman.</i> or enable [the daughter of an Israelite] to partake of it:<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">If the individual involved is a priest.</i>a rapist, a seducer,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one who seduced a girl under majority. In that instance, even though the relations were with consent, since the girl is a minor, they are considered to have been carried out against her will.</i> or a mentally or emotionally unstable person.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In the first two instances, since the relations were carried out outside the context of marriage, they are not significant with regard to <i>terumah</i>. In the latter instance, since the marriage of an intellectual or emotionally unstable person is of no significance whatsoever (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 11:6), the relations are considered as taking place outside the context of marriage.</i> [This applies] provided she is not forbidden to them, in which instance, they disqualify her through relations, because she becomes a <i>zonah</i> or a <i>chalalah</i>, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 6, Halachah 7. In such an instance, the woman is disqualified even if the relations were carried out under duress.</i>
When a woman married a priest who was intellectually or emotionally unstable or a priest raped her or seduced her and she bore him a child, she may partake of <i>terumah</i> because of her son.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is a priest, he entitles his mother to partake of <i>terumah</i>. See Chapter 6, Halachah 12.</i> There is a doubt concerning the matter, for since she was not consecrated to him, it is possible that she was impregnated by another person. Nevertheless, we operate under the presumption that the child was conceived by [the priest] who engaged in relations with her. [This applies] provided rumors did not circulate concerning her with another person. Instead, everyone gossips about her and this priest.<br>Similar concepts apply when an Israelite who is intellectually or emotionally unstable engages in relations with the daughter of a priest or [an Israelite] raped or seduced her and she became pregnant. She may not partake of <i>terumah</i> because of the fetus. If the fetus is aborted, she may partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., she resumes her previous status.</i>
When witnesses concerning a [<i>sotah</i>] warning and witnesses concerning [a <i>sotah</i>] meeting testify against a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>sotah</i> is a woman suspected of adultery who is subjected to a test of her fidelity by drinking special water, the "bitter water" mentioned by the Rambam, prepared in the Temple. For a woman to be required to take this test, two witnesses must testify that she was warned by her husband not to enter into privacy with a particular man and two witnesses must testify that she in fact entered into privacy with the said person (<i>Hilchot Sotah</i> 1:1).</i> married [to a priest], she is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> until she drinks the bitter water, because there is a question whether she is a <i>zonah</i>. If her husband dies before she drinks the water or she is one of the women who are absolved from drinking<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Sotah</i> 2:2 which mentions fifteen women that are placed in this category.</i> and do not receive the money due them by virtue of their <i>ketubah</i>, she is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> forever.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">For the <i>sotah</i> test is necessary to very whether or not she was faithful to her husband and that test is not administered unless the husband is alive (<i>ibid.</i> 2:7).</i> Whenever the wife of a priest says: "I have been unfaithful," she is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This is not speaking about a <i>sotah</i>, but rather an ordinary woman married to a priest.<br><i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 24:23 states that, in this situation, we do not accept the woman's word and we require her to remain married to her husband, for we suspect that she was not unfaithful and made this statement only so that she would be divorced and free to marry someone else. Nevertheless, her word is accepted with regard to <i>terumah</i> and her husband must provide her with other food.</i>
When a daughter of an Israelite who has not attained majority marries a priest without her father's consent, whether in his presence or in his absence, she may not partake of <i>terumah</i> even though her father consecrated her.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Until a girl attains majority, she cannot marry without her father's consent. Thus it is as if the marriage is conditional until he consents and if he does not consent, it is nullified (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 3:13, 22:5). Hence, we do no allow her to partake of <i>terumah</i>, for if the marriage is nullified, she would have no right to partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> [The rationale is that] her father might object and then she would retroactively be unfit [to partake of <i>terumah</i>]. [The reason] he remains silent even though he sees is because he is angry because she married without his consent.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">And does not desire to speak his mind until he calms down.</i>

Chapter 9

A woman may partake of <i>terumah</i> until her bill of divorce reaches her hand or the hand of her agent whom she appointed to receive it.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This latter point requires qualification as the Rambam proceeds to state.</i><br>Whenever there is a doubt whether or not a woman was divorced, she may not partake of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For a Scriptural prohibition is involved and we follow the general principle: Whenever there is a question regarding a point of Scriptural Law, we rule stringently. Hence, even though her husband is liable to provide her with her sustenance in such a situation (<i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 5:13), he may not give her <i>terumah</i>.</i> When a woman appoints an agent to receive her bill of divorce, she is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> immediately.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is a corollary to the point mentioned beforehand. Since a woman is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> whenever there is a doubt that she was divorced, she is forbidden as soon as she appoints her agent, for at any time, he may receive her bill of divorce. Even though the matter is not entirely in the agent's hands, for the bill of divorce must be given on the husband's initiative, we follow the presumption that the agent will fulfill the mission with which he is charged (Radbaz).</i> If she said: "Receive the bill of divorce for me in this-and-this place," she is not forbidden [to partake of <i>terumah</i>] until the agent reaches that place.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For the bill of divorce is not effective if given elsewhere (<i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 9:34).</i><br>If she sent an agent to bring her the bill of divorce, she may partake of <i>terumah</i> until the bill of divorce reaches her hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For in this instance, the agent is acting in place of the husband and the divorce is not completed until he gives the woman the bill of divorce (<i>ibid.</i> 6:5).</i> When a person tells his wife: "This is your bill of divorce; [it is effective] one hour before my death"<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Such an arrangement was often made when a couple were childless so that if the husband died the woman would be free of the obligation of <i>yibbum</i>.</i> she is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> immediately.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For we fear that he might die at any moment. Hence, from the previous hour onward, she would have partaken of <i>terumah</i> without having the right to do so.</i>
[The inhabitants of] a city under siege, [the voyagers on] a ship in danger of sinking at sea, and a suspect to be judged [for a crime worthy of capital punishment] are presumed to be alive.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">And if they are priests, their wives may continue partaking of <i>terumah</i>.</i> Needless to say, this applies to one who goes on a caravan journey.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">We are speaking about a caravan journey through the desert. Although there is a certain amount of danger to the travelers, we operate under the presumption that they are alive until we receive information otherwise.</i><br>[In the following instances,] however: a city was captured by besieging forces, a ship was lost at sea, or a person was going out to be executed by a gentile court,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The officers of a gentile court are likely to accept bribes. Hence, the fact that one was sentenced to death is not necessarily proof that he died.</i> a person dragged by a wild beast, one upon whom a landslide fell, or one carried away by a river, we regard the individuals with the stringencies appropriate to both the living and the dead.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In these situations, there is a high likelihood - but no definite proof that the person died. Hence, his wife must assume that he is dead and accept all the stringencies that state would apply. At the same time, she cannot act on the assumption that he is dead and remarry. The Rambam continues explaining the implications of this status vis-à-vis <i>terumah</i>.</i> Therefore if among the women were the daughter of a priest married to an Israelite<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">She must presume her husband is alive and may not partake of <i>terumah</i> for that reason.</i> or the daughter of an Israelite married to a priest,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">She must presume her husband is dead and may not partake of <i>terumah</i> for that reason.</i> they may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<br>If, however, a person was sentenced to death in a [Jewish] court and was taken to the place where he will be stoned,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">A person sentenced to be executed may be given a reprieve from execution if a redeeming factor is found for him (<i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 13:1). Nevertheless, once he has already been brought to the place of execution, it is very unlikely that this will happen (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> we presume that he is dead and his wife may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if she is the daughter of an Israelite married to a priest. If she is the daughter of a priest married to an Israelite, she may partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i>
If a woman left her husband while he was in his death throes in another country, she may not partake of <i>terumah</i>, whether she is the daughter of a priest married to an Israelite or the daughter of an Israelite married to a priest. [The rationale is that] most people in their death throes die.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the daughter of the Israelite may not partake of <i>terumah</i>. On the other hand, not all die. Hence, since a daughter of the priest married to an Israelite was not partaking of <i>terumah</i> beforehand, this is not considered sufficient reason to allow her to do so (Radbaz).</i><br>If one witness testifies that [a woman's husband] has died and one testifies that he has not died, she may not partake [of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Although a woman is not given permission to remarry in such an instance (<i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 12:18), she is still not permitted to partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i>
[When a man is married to two wives and] one of the wives tells the other that their husband died, since [the other wife] cannot marry by virtue of this testimony,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 12:16 states that, if no one contradicts the testimony, whenever a witness testifies that a woman's husband died, his or her word is accepted with the exception of five women: another wife of that man, a woman who is her husband's <i>yevamah</i>, the woman's mother-in-law, her mother-in-law's daughter, and her husband's daughter from another wife. In all these instances, we fear that there is enmity between these women and the man's wife and they will testify falsely so that she will marry another man and hence, be forced to accept a divorce from her husband.</i> she may continue to partake of <i>terumah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad does not accept the Rambam's ruling and maintains that even though the woman is not allowed to remarry, she should not be allowed to partake of <i>terumah</i>. For perhaps these women are telling the truth. The Radbaz supports the Rambam's ruling.</i> on the presumption that her husband is alive until a person upon whose testimony is sufficient to enable her to marry testifies concerning her. [The same ruling applies] if such testimony is given by any of the five women whose testimony is not accepted if they say that her husband died.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 12:16 states that, if no one contradicts the testimony, whenever a witness testifies that a woman's husband died, his or her word is accepted with the exception of five women: another wife of that man, a woman who is her husband's <i>yevamah</i>, the woman's mother-in-law, her mother-in-law's daughter, and her husband's daughter from another wife. In all these instances, we fear that there is enmity between these women and the man's wife and they will testify falsely so that she will marry another man and hence, be forced to accept a divorce from her husband.</i>
When [a priest] frees his servant, from the time he transfers<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording allows for the interpretation that this ruling applies whether he gives the bill of emancipation to the servant or to another person on behalf of the servant (see <i>Hilchot Avadim</i> 6:1).</i> his bill of emancipation<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The formal legal contract freeing him from slavery.</i> to him, he disqualifies him from partaking of <i>terumah</i>. Whenever a servant is given his freedom, but his bill of emancipation is withheld as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Avadim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Avadim</i> 5:4, <i>et al</i>, there are certain situations where a servant is deserving of his freedom. Nevertheless, he does not receive the status of a freed servant until he receives his bill of emancipation. Even so, he is forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i> from the time he becomes worthy of freedom.</i> he is, nonetheless, forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>.
There is a doubt [whether the transfer of the ownership of a servant is effective in the following situation]. A person composed a legal document transferring his property - which included servants - to another person. He [did not give the document to that person directly, but instead] gave it to another person on his behalf.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In such a situation, we say that since generally, it is considered desirable to receive a gift, the intended recipient acquires the property unless he lodges an objection at the time he hears about the gift. See <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 4:2.</i> The recipient [of the present] remained silent and afterwards, protested. There is a doubt whether his protests reflected his initial disposition<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., that he never desired to receive the present and thus it never left the domain of its initial owner (<i>ibid.</i>:3).</i> and thus [the servants] have never left the initial domain or whether his protest after his initial silence is [interpreted as] a renunciation of his initial position.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">And thus, he is considered to have acquired the property - and the servants - and then to have renounced ownership of it and them.</i> Therefore, [the servants] may not partake of <i>terumah</i>. [This applies] whether the second master<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The recipient of the present.</i> was an Israelite and the first master, a priest or the first master was an Israelite and the second, a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For in either situation, there is a possibility that their present owner is an Israelite.</i>
When an Israelite rents livestock from a priest, he may feed it <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Because the rental does not interrupt the priests's ownership. The Israelite must, however, transfer ownership of the <i>terumah</i> to the priest before feeding it to the animal.</i> When a priest rents livestock from an Israelite, although he is obligated to provide it with food, he may not feed it <i>terumah</i>, because it is not his financial acquisition.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">There are, however, contexts where rental is considered equivalent to purchase. See <i>Hilchot Sechirut</i> 7:1.</i>
When an Israelite receives a cow from a priest for the sake of fattening it and has it evaluated so that its increase in value will be split,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This was a popular arrangement in the Talmudic era. The owner of livestock would give it to a shepherd to fatten. The shepherd would have it evaluated and accept responsibility for its value although he did not actually pay the priest anything. Afterwards, when it has been fattened, the value which the shepherd accepted responsibility for is returned to the owner and the two share the profits equally. See <i>Hilchot Shluchin VeShutafim</i> 8:1-4 and <i>Hilchot Malveh ViLoveh</i> 8:12 for more details concerning this arrangement.</i> he may not feed it <i>terumah</i> even though the priest has a share in its increase in value. If, by contrast, a priest receives a cow from an Israelite for the sake of fattening it and has it evaluated, he may feed it <i>terumah</i>. [The rationale is that] although the Israelite has a share in its increase in value, its body belongs to the priest, because he has [accepted responsibility] for its value.
When a cow belonging to an Israelite gives birth to its firstborn, the owner may feed it <i>terumah</i>, for the firstborn belongs to the priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the law mentioned in the first clause of Halachah 7 applies.</i> A person may store vetch<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">A type of legume used as animal fodder.</i> that is <i>terumah</i> in his dovecote. He need not worry that his doves will come and eat it.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Even a priest may not feed his doves <i>terumah</i>, for <i>terumah</i> may only be given to domesticated animals. Nevertheless, everyone, even Israelites, need not take precautions against an animal eating vetch that is <i>terumah</i> on its own initiative. The Radbaz explains that the rationale is that the obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from vetch is merely Rabbinic in origin. Implied is that if the obligation was Scriptural in origin, one could not take such leniency. It must be noted, however, that not all authorities consider the obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from vetch as Rabbinic.</i>
It appears to me<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This introduction precedes a law which the Rambam derived through his own process of deduction without an explicit source in the previous Rabbinic literature.</i> that if a priest sold his cow to an Israelite and took payment, he can no longer feed it <i>terumah</i> even though the purchaser has not drawn it into his domain as of yet. [The rationale is that] according to Scriptural Law, the transfer of money completes a transaction, as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Mekach UMemcar</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 3:1. There it is explained that the Rabbis required the purchaser to draw the article into his possession for the transaction to be completed.</i> [Conversely,] if an Israelite sold a cow to a priest, he should not feed it <i>terumah</i> until he draws it into his domain<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">And thus completes the transaction.</i> even though he already made payment.

Chapter 10

When a non-priests partakes of <i>terumah</i> unknowingly, he must make restitution for the principal and add a fifth.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 26, the intent is one fifth of the new total. Thus if a person eats the value of four measures of grain, he must pay five. Moreover, he may not use grain that is <i>terumah</i> to make restitution, he must use ordinary grain (which is more expensive). This fifth becomes considered as <i>terumah</i> itself and must be eaten in a state of ritual purity. See also Halachah 15.</i> Even if he knows that it is <i>terumah</i> and that he is warned against partaking of it, but he does not know whether or not he is liable for death,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">At the hand of heaven.</i> he is considered to have acted unknowingly and he must make restitution for the principal and add a fifth.
A person who eats an article [that is <i>terumah</i>] that is ordinarily eaten, drinks something that is ordinarily drunk, or smears himself with something ordinarily used for that purpose [is liable],<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since these are the ways in which one may benefit from <i>terumah</i> (see Chapter 11, Halachah 1), these are the ways for which one is liable for partaking of it.</i> as [derived from Leviticus 22:15]: "And they shall not defile the sacraments of the children of Israel." This includes one who smears himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The implication of the verse is that just as eating involves tangible physical satisfaction, so too, smearing oneself produces tangible physical satisfaction.</i><br>Whether one partakes of <i>terumah</i> which is ritually pure or ritually impure unknowingly, one must make restitution for the principal and add a fifth.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">It appears that in contrast to the intentional violation of the prohibition mentioned in Halachah 5, in this instance, the transgressor should pay the full value of the <i>terumah</i> even if it is ritually impure. The rationale is that here a person is receiving atonement for his transgression. Hence, he is required to make full payment.</i> He is not liable for a fifth until he eats an olive-sized portion, as [indicated by <i>ibid.</i>:14]: "When one will eat a sacrament unknowingly"; eating implies consuming no less than an olive-sized portion. Just as one is liable for eating an olive-sized portion, so too, [one is liable for] drinking an olive-sized portion.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz explains at length why although with regard to most prohibitions, the minimum measure for which one is liable for drinking is a <i>revi'it</i> of a <i>log</i>, a larger measure than an olive-sized portion, in this instance an exception is made. The basis of his explanation is that this prohibition focuses on "eating" <i>terumah</i>, and as stated above, intaking an olive-sized portion constitutes eating.</i>
[The following laws apply if a person] ate <i>terumah</i> and then ate again, drank and then drank again. If there is sufficient time to eat a half a loaf of bread<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., an equivalent of three egg-sized portions. If a person stretches out his consumption of an olive-sized beyond this time span, it s not considered as "eating," for he will not have ingested a significant amount at once. The Rabbis mention different opinions with regard to this time span, referred to as <i>k'dai achilat pras</i>, some as brief as 2 minutes and some as long as 9 minutes. Based on Shiurei Torah, the suggested practice is to consider <i>k'dai achilat pras</i> as 4 minutes with regard to eating matzah on Pesach, but 9 minutes with regard to eating on Yom Kippur. See also <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 14:9 and notes.</i> from the time he began to eat until he concluded or sufficient time to drink a <i>revi'it</i> from the time he began to drink until he concluded,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The time to drink a <i>revi'it</i> is much less than <i>k'dai achilat pras</i>. The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, maintaining that the latter measure of time should be applied in this instance as well. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> cite sources to support the Rambam's view and note that he mentions this measure of time for drinking both with regard to the prohibition against drinking on Yom Kippur (<i>Hilchot Shevitat Esor</i> 2:4) and the prohibition against drinking gentile wine (<i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 14:9). They do note, however, that in <i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i> 8:11, however, the Rambam mentions the time span of <i>k'dai achilat pras</i> even with regard to drinking.<br>They differentiate between the rulings as follows: In all the instances involving prohibitions against eating, the Rambam mentions the time to drink a <i>revi'it</i> because a person will not feel satisfaction if his drinking the minimum measure is stretched out over a longer span of time. In <i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i>, by contrast, the concept involves considering a person ritually impure because of his having ingested a quantity of liquid. As long as he does not wait longer than <i>k'dai achilat pras</i>, the liquid is still collected in his digestive system and has not begun to circulate throughout his body.</i> [all he consumes] is combined to comprise an olive-sized portion.
<i>Terumah, terumat ma'aser</i>, whether the latter is from <i>d'mai</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Produce that we are unsure whether or not the tithes have been separated. The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:59) explains that the term is a composite of two Aramaic words <i>da mai</i> which mean: "What is this?", i.e., the person is unaware of the status of the produce with which he is dealing.</i> or from produce from which the tithes were definitely [not separated], <i>challah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The portion of dough separated and given to the priests.</i> and the first fruits can all be combined together to comprise an olive-sized portion<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if one eats a little bit of any combination of these forbidden substances and the entire amount is an olive-sized portion, one is liable.</i> for which one is liable for death<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">If the transgression was intentional.</i> or [restitution, plus] a fifth,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If the transgression was performed unknowingly.</i> for they are all called <i>terumah</i> [at different times in the Torah].<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Challah</i> is referred to as <i>terumah</i> in Numbers 15:20 and the first fruits are referred to as <i>terumah</i> in Deuteronomy 12:17.</i><br>According to law, one should not be liable for a fifth for [the unknowing consumption of] <i>terumat ma'aser</i> that is <i>d'mai</i>, just as one is not liable for the second tithe [from <i>d'mai</i>], as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 5:4. Since we are speaking about <i>d'mai</i>, produce from which separations are being made because of a doubt, there is no obligation according to Scriptural Law to add a fifth. With regard to <i>ma'aser sheni</i>, our Sages felt that if the obligation of an additional fifth was imposed, people would ignore the obligation to separate <i>ma'aser sheni</i> from <i>d'mai</i> entirely. With regard to <i>terumat ma'aser</i> from <i>d'mai</i>, they felt the converse is true. Were the obligation of an additional fifth not imposed, people would not treat the obligation to separate <i>d'mai</i> seriously.</i> Nevertheless, our Sages said: If one is not liable for a fifth, people will treat it with disdain.
When a person partook <i>terumah</i> intentionally [after] receiving a warning, he is liable for lashes and is not required to make financial restitution.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">A person never receives both corporal punishment and a financial penalty. Hence, he is liable only for lashes. See the notes to Chapter 6, Halachah 6, which explain why here he is punished by lashes and not required to make restitution.</i> If he did not receive warning, [he is required to make financial restitution].<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">When he makes restitution, the produce he gives the priest is considered as ordinary produce and not as <i>terumah</i> (<i>Terumot</i> 7:1).</i> If [the <i>terumah</i>] was ritually pure, he is required to make restitution for the principal, but is not required to add a fifth.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">An addition of a fifth was required only in the case of inadvertent transgression. In his Commentary to the Mishneh (<i>Terumot</i> 7:1), the Rambam explains that the additional fifth was instituted for atonement and that was possible only when the transgression was violated unintentionally. When it was violated intentionally, the sin is too great for atonement to be granted in an ordinary manner.</i> If [the <i>terumah</i>] was ritually impure, he is required to pay only as if it were wood, because it is fit only to use as fuel.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">For <i>terumah</i> can only be eaten only when it is ritually pure.</i> Accordingly, if one ate berries or pomegranates or the like that were <i>terumah</i> that became impure, he is not obligated to make restitution, because these are not fit to be used as fuel.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since they have no value, there is no need for restitution.</i>
When a person eats <i>terumah</i> that is <i>chametz</i> on Pesach, whether willfully or unknowingly, whether it is ritually impure or pure, he is exempt from financial liability.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's decision, noting that it is the subject of a difference of opinion between the Sages of the Mishneh (see <i>Pesachim</i> 32a) and the opinion stated by the Rambam does not appear to be accepted in a definitive manner. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> provide interpretations of that passage that justify the Rambam's ruling.</i> Even if he separated <i>terumah</i> from <i>matzah</i>, but it became <i>chametz</i> [before he ate it], he is exempt. <sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesachim, loc. cit.,</i> speaks about an instance where a person steals <i>terumah</i> that is matzah from a priest and ate it. Even if it became <i>chametz</i> in the thief's domain in which instance, the thief caused the priest a loss, the thief is not liable financially.</i> He is not even required to pay as if it were wood, because it is not fit for use as fuel. [Instead,] since it is forbidden to benefit from it,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Chametz UMatzah</i> 1:2,4, <i>et al</i>.</i> it is of no value whatsoever.
If, however, one unknowingly ate <i>terumah</i> on Yom Kippur,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">When a person is forbidden to eat. In this instance, the prohibition rests on the person (the <i>gavra</i> in yeshivah terminology) and not the <i>cheftza</i>, the article in question. Hence he is liable.</i> ate <i>terumah</i> that was perforated,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., dough that is <i>terumah</i> is discovered with holes in it. Our Sages fear that the holes were made by a poisonous snake who deposited his venom in the dough. Hence, they forbade partaking of it (<i>Hilchot Rotzeach UShemirat Nefesh</i> 12:1).</i> drank wine that was <i>terumah</i> that was left open,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This too was forbidden for a similar reason. We fear that a poisonous snake drank from it and deposited his venom there (<i>ibid.</i>:6-7). In these two instances, although the substances are forbidden, they are forbidden only in consideration for the person's wellbeing. There is no ritual prohibition resting on the foods. Hence one is liable.</i> smeared himself with wine and oil [that were <i>terumah</i>] at the same time, or drank oil and vinegar [that were <i>terumah</i>] at the same time,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 11 states that one drinks oil or smears oneself with wine, he is liable only for the principal, for as the commentaries state there, that is not the customary manner of benefiting from these substances. In this halachah, the Rambam clarifies that if one combines the substances as he states here, this is considered as the normal pattern and one is liable.</i> chewed raw kernels of wheat,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad notes that there is a difference of opinion concerning this matter in the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Terumot</i> 6:1). The opinion cited by the Rambam is advanced by Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi while the majority of the Sages differ. Hence, he maintains that the Sages' view should be followed for, as stated in the following halachah with regard to barley, eating uncooked kernels of wheat is harmful. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> justify the Rambam's view. As stated in <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 3:2, one is required to make a blessing when eating such kernels. This indicates one is deriving benefit and if one derives benefit from <i>terumah</i>, he is required to make restitution.</i> or swallowed vinegar,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">It is uncommon to partake of these foods in this manner. Nevertheless, it is not so infrequent a practice to reduce one's liability.</i> he is liable to make restitution for the principal and add a fifth.
When a person is satisfied and is disgusted by his food, but continues eating <i>terumah</i> despite the fact that he is satisfied, he is not required to add a fifth [when making restitution, for the prooftext cited above] states: "When one will eat...." [Implied is that he is when he eats in an ordinary manner] and not when he harms himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Note the parallel with regard to the prohibition against eating on Yom Kippur (<i>Hilchot Shivitat Esor</i> 2:7). There too the Rambam rules that consuming food in such a state is not considered as "eating."</i> Similarly, when one chews raw kernels of barley, he is not liable, because he harms himself.
When a non-priest swallowed prunes of <i>terumah</i> [whole<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, he chewed them before swallowing them, the second person is not liable at all. For once they have been chewed, they are entirely worthless (Radbaz).</i> unknowingly] and then regurgitated them, and another person came and also ate them unknowingly, the first person is required to make restitution for the principal and add a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">For swallowing the fruit whole is also considered eating.</i> and the second person is obligated to pay the first one as if the prunes were wood.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">After the person swallowed the prunes, the sanctity of <i>terumah</i> within them is considered to have been desecrated. For that reason, the first person who swallowed them is required to make restitution. They become his property and the second one is liable as one who damages the property of the first.</i>
When one feeds <i>terumah</i> to workers<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Whom he agreed to supply with meals in addition to their wages.</i> or to guests, they are required to make restitution for the principal and add a fifth, for they acted unknowingly.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 6:3), the Rambam elaborates on why the guests and not the host are responsible, citing the principle (<i>Kiddushin</i> 42b) that a person cannot act as an agent to perform a transgression for a colleague. Instead, the transgression is solely the responsibility of the person who performed it, in this instance, the people who partook of the food.</i> He must pay them for their meal,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the full price of the meal were it to have been prepared from ordinary produce.</i> for ordinary produce is more valuable than the <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">For this reason, it is preferable for the workers to have the employer pay them rather than having him pay the principal for the <i>terumah</i> (Radbaz).</i> since a person's soul is repelled from forbidden food.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Note a similar ruling in <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 16:14. This principle is stated by the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Terumot, loc. cit.</i>) in explanation of the reason the employer is required to reimburse his workers. Seemingly, he was required to provide them with a meal. He did and they ate to their satisfaction, why then is he liable to them? Our Sages explain, based on the above principle, that since the food was forbidden, their souls did not derive true satisfaction from it and hence, he is required to give them a meal.</i>
When [a non-priest] feeds <i>terumah</i> to his children who were below majority<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The children are not liable, for they are not obligated in the observance of the mitzvot.</i> or to his servants whether they are above or below majority,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The servants are not liable, for they have no independent financial capacity.</i> he must pay the principal, but not the additional fifth.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 15 which states that whenever a person is required to pay only the principal, the grain that he pays is not considered as <i>terumah</i>.</i> [This ruling also applies to] one who partakes of <i>terumah</i> from the Diaspora,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">For it is forbidden only according to Rabbinic Law and the Sages did not require this additional payment.</i> one who eats or drinks less than an olive-sized portion,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">One is not liable in this instance, because the prooftext requiring payment of the additional fifth mentions "eating," and eating or drinking less than an olive-sized portion is not considered as "eating" [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 7:3)].</i> a nazarite who unknowingly drank wine that was <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Wine is forbidden to a nazirite whether it is <i>terumah</i> or not and our Sages explain that eating a forbidden substance is not considered "eating." In this context, a distinction can be made between eating on Yom Kippur which is an <i>issur gavra</i>, i.e., the food is not forbidden, the person is forbidden to partake of it, and wine for a nazirite, which is an <i>issur cheftza</i>, the wine itself becomes forbidden for him (<i>Pri Megadim, Eshel Avraham</i> 202:11). This explanation resolves an objection lodged by the Ra'avad who cites a <i>Tosefta</i> that rules differently than the Rambam.</i> one drinks oil [without it being mixed with other liquids] and one who smears himself with wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In the latter two instances, these are not ordinary ways of benefiting from these products.</i>
When the daughter of a priest who was married to an Israelite or disqualified [from partaking of <i>terumah</i> for other reasons] partook of <i>terumah</i>, she must make restitution for the principal, but she is not required to add a fifth.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since she was entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i> previously, a distinction is made between her and other non-priest. See the <i>Sifra</i> to Leviticus 22:12.</i><br>When a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The daughter of an Israelite married to a priest who was therefore entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i> [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 8:1)].</i> was partaking of <i>terumah</i> and she was told: "Your husband died," or "...divorced you," she is required to pay only the principal.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">As in the previous clause.</i> If the <i>terumah</i> was <i>chametz</i> on the day preceding Pesach, she is exempt from making restitution.
