[The rationale is that then] the time is pressing and she hurried to eat without investigating.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that since it is a pressing time, it is considered as if the transgression was violated due to forces beyond her control (<i>ones</i>).</i><br>[These same laws apply] when a servant was partaking [of <i>terumah</i>] and he was told: "Your master died and left an heir who does not entitle you to eat,"<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., his son was a <i>challal</i> or he had no sons and his daughter was married to an Israelite (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> "...sold you to an Israelite," "...gave you to him as a present," or "...freed you," and when a priest was partaking [of <i>terumah</i>] and he discovered that he is the son of a divorcee or a woman who underwent <i>chalitzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to the servant, the same logic that applies with regard to the women mentioned previously applies to him. Since he was allowed to partake of <i>terumah</i> previously, he is not held responsible for the additional fifth. With regard to the priest who was disqualified, we find that he still has a certain vestige of connection to the priesthood, as evidenced by the fact that were he to bring an offering in the Temple, it would be acceptable after the fact (<i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 6:10). Therefore it is sufficient for him to make restitution for the principal [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>)].</i> In all of these instances, if these individuals had <i>terumah</i> in their mouths when they discovered that they were forbidden to partake of it, they should spit it out.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Even though by spitting it out, he is spoiling the <i>terumah</i>, that is preferable to swallowing it. For until he swallows it, he is not considered to have partaken of it (Radbaz).</i>
[Similarly, when a priest] was partaking [of <i>terumah</i>] and he was told: "You became impure," "The <i>terumah</i> became impure,"<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In these instances, when he began eating, he was permitted to do so, it is only afterwards, that he or the <i>terumah</i> became impure.</i> "You were impure," or "the <i>terumah</i> was impure,"<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Thus from the outset, he should not have partaken of the <i>terumah</i>.</i> he should spit it out. [The same ruling applies] if he discovered that [the <i>terumah</i>] was <i>tevel</i>, the first tithe from which <i>terumat ma'aser</i> had not been separated,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, it is forbidden to partake of it until the appropriate separations are made.</i> or <i>ma'aser sheni</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The second tithe which cannot be eaten outside of Jerusalem unless it is redeemed.</i> or consecrated property that was not redeemed, or [when partaking of the <i>terumah</i>,] he tasted a bug.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">And the disgusting taste of the bug prevented him from swallowing the <i>terumah</i> [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>)].</i>
When there are two containers, one of <i>terumah</i> and one of ordinary produce, and <i>terumah</i> fell into one of them, but it is not known which one, we operate under the supposition that it fell into the one which [contained] <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, although there is reason to suspect that the <i>terumah</i> fell into the other container, we consider it as ordinary produce. The rationale is that we were operating under the <i>chazzakah</i>, prevailing assumption, that the container was filled with ordinary produce. Hence, unless there is a strong likelihood otherwise, we continue to operate under that perspective.<br>This reflects a principle that has ramifications beyond the laws of <i>terumah</i>. Although generally, we maintain that when there is a doubt regarding a Scriptural prohibition, we rule stringently, in this instance, since there is a plausible explanation for the lenient ruling, it is accepted. See also Chapter 13, Halachah 13-14, and the <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i>, sec. 111.<br>There is, however, a point that has to be clarified. In Chapter 13, it is explained that this principle applies with regard to matters of Rabbinic Law, but not matters of Scriptural Law, and yet, here we are dealing even with questions involving <i>terumah</i> of Scriptural status. Nevertheless, there is no difficulty, because here we are not speaking about whether it is permissible to partake of the mixture or not. That question was discussed in Chapter 13, and the ruling was rendered that it is forbidden. Here the question concerns financial restitution: Is the non-priest required to pay for the produce that he ate? With regard to this point, the Rambam explains that we can use the above reasoning.</i><br>If it is not known which one is <i>terumah</i> and a non-priest partakes of one of them, he is not liable for payment.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">For the additional fifth. The principal, i.e., the remaining container, must certainly be given to the priest. For if the one which was eaten was <i>terumah</i>, the second one should be given to him as payment for the first. And if the one that was eaten was not <i>terumah</i>, than the remaining one is and it must be given to the priest.</i> [The rationale is that when a person seeks] to expropriate money from a colleague, the burden of proof is on him.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Thus if the priest seeks to expropriate the additional fifth, he must prove that the container consumed was <i>terumah</i>.<br>This is a fundamental principle in Jewish Law. Whenever there is a doubt with regard to the ownership of money or movable property, the person in physical possession of the property in question is allowed to maintain possession until the claimant proves his claim. See <i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 20:5, <i>et al</i>.</i> He must treat the other one as if it were <i>terumah</i>. If another person partakes of the other one, however, he is also exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">From the additional fifth. For in this instance as well, we are not certain that he partook of <i>terumah</i>.</i> If, however, one person eats them both, he must make restitution for the smaller one.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">For he definitely partook of <i>terumah</i>. Nevertheless, since we do not know which one was <i>terumah</i>, he can only be held liable for the smaller one, because of the principle stated above.</i> If he did so intentionally, he is required to make restitution for the principal. If he did so unknowingly, he is required to make restitution for the principal, plus a fifth.
When a person eats the additional fifth unknowingly, he must [make restitution for it and] add another fifth.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">For the fifth originally added becomes considered as <i>terumah</i> and compensation must be made for it.</i> For the fifth is considered as the principal with regard to all matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it must be eaten in a state of ritual purity.</i> Similarly, he continues to add a fifth for every fifth forever.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">When Leviticus 5:24 speaks of adding a fifth, it uses a form that could be interpreted as plural, "its fifths." The implication is that he may have to add many fifths [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 6:1)].</i><br>Whenever a person makes restitution for the principal and the additional fifth, [the grain] he gives is <i>terumah</i> with regard to all matters<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if it becomes mixed with ordinary grain, the laws of <i>dimua</i> (the mixture of <i>terumah</i>) apply (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> [with one exception]. If they were sown, the grain that grows is ordinary produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast, grain growing from <i>terumah</i> is considered as <i>terumah</i> (Chapter 11, Halachah 21).</i> If the priest wishes to forgo [the payment], he cannot.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">For we are not speaking about a mere financial payment owed the priest, but a means of attaining atonement.</i> Whenever, [by contrast,] a person makes restitution only for the principal,<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 5 and Halachot 11-12.</i> [the grain] he gives is ordinary grain<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., none of the restrictions of <i>terumah</i> apply to it.</i> and if the priest desires to forgo payment, he may.
[The following laws apply if] a daughter of an Israelite partakes of <i>terumah</i> and then marries a priest. If [she partook] of <i>terumah</i> that had not been acquired by a priest,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it had been separated, but had not been given to a priest.</i> she may make restitution of the principal and the additional fifth to herself.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">She must separate this grain to receive atonement. Nevertheless, since as a priest's wife, she is entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i>, she may take the <i>terumah</i> she separates as her own.</i> If she partook of <i>terumah</i> that a priest had acquired, she is required to make restitution of the principal to its owner,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">That priest.</i> but she may keep the additional fifth as her own. For whenever a person makes restitution and pays an additional fifth, he may give the additional fifth to whichever priest he desires.
If she was not able to make restitution before she was divorced,<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Or she was widowed and left childless (Radbaz).</i> she can no longer make restitution to herself and she is like a person who never married a priest at all.
Whenever a person partakes of <i>terumah</i> whether unknowingly or intentionally, he may make restitution only from ordinary produce from which the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes have been separated. Restitution may be made from <i>leket, shichichah, pe'ah,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Matanot Aniyim</i> for a definition of these terms. They are acceptable for this purpose, because once they are acquired by a poor person, they become his private property.</i> ownerless grain,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumah</i> need not be separated from such grain or from the presents for the poor.</i> and grain from the first tithe after <i>terumat ma'aser</i> was separated i.e., even if the great <i>terumah</i> from that crop had not been separated, for the person separated the tithes before the <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that we are referring to an instance where the person separated the first tithe while the crop is still stalks of grain and gave it to the Levite before it has been winnowed. As explained in Chapter 3, Halachah 13, in such an instance, it is not necessary to separate the great <i>terumah</i>. If, however, it is ordinary grain from which the separations were not made in the proper order, it cannot be used to make restitution for <i>terumah</i>.</i><br>One may make restitution from the second tithes and consecrated property that were redeemed, even though they were not redeemed in an appropriate manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 6:5), the Rambam explains that this refers to the second tithe that was redeemed using an unminted coin or consecrated property that was redeemed with land. The commentaries have questioned this interpretation, noting that usually redemption with such articles is not effective at all (see <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 4:9; <i>Hilchot Arachin</i> 7:1). They cite the Rambam's Commentary to <i>Berachot</i> 7:1 which interprets this term as referring to a situation where the principal was paid, but the additional fifth that is required was not.</i> And one may make restitution using new grain<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">New grain refers to grain harvested after the <i>omer</i> offering. Old grain refers to grain from the previous harvest.</i> for old grain. One may not, however, make restitution from one type [of grain] for another type [of grain]. [This is derived from Leviticus 22:14:] "And he shall give the priest the sanctified [food]." [Implied is that it must be the same] as the sanctified food he ate.
When a person eats zucchini from the sixth year,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In the seventh year and thus there is no way he can acquire the zucchini of the sixth year.</i> he should wait until [he acquires] the zucchini of the eighth year to make restitution from them. For he cannot pay his debt from the crops of the seventh year, as will be explained in that place.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shemitah VeYovel</i> 6:10 which states that the crops of the Sabbatical year may not be used to pay debts, for this is comparable to using them for commercial purposes which is forbidden.</i>
When a person ate <i>terumah</i> that was ritually impure, he makes restitution from ordinary produce, whether pure or impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The produce he gives as restitution will be considered as impure <i>terumah</i>. Hence, there is no difficulty in him giving impure produce.</i> If he partook of <i>terumah</i> that was ritually pure, he should make restitution with ordinary grain that is pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">For if the grain is impure, it will not be an adequate replacement.</i> If he made restitution from ordinary grain that was impure whether intentionally or unknowingly, the restitution he made is accepted, but he must make restitution again from ritually pure grain.
When a person partook of <i>terumah</i> belonging to a <i>chaver</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">A person who is careful to observe the laws of ritual purity. See Chapter 6, Halachah 2; <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i>, ch. 10.</i> he should make restitution to him. If he partook of <i>terumah</i> belonging to a common person,<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Who is not necessarily careful concerning the laws of ritual purity.</i> he should make restitution to a <i>chaver</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">So that the laws of ritual purity are adhered to.</i> and take its worth from him and give it to the common person whose <i>terumah</i> he ate.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">So that he is reimbursed for his loss.</i> For we do not give articles that require ritually purity to a common person.
When [an Israelite] stole <i>terumah</i> from his maternal grandfather who was a priest and consumed it [unknowingly], and afterwards, his maternal grandfather died, he may not make restitution to himself,<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he is an heir, since we are speaking about stolen property, he may not maintain possession of it, but instead must remove it from his domain and give it to someone else. Note the parallel in <i>Hilchot Gezeilah</i> 8:2-3.</i> but rather to another heir<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>terumah</i> belonged to his maternal grandfather, it is not appropriate that the restitution be given to someone who does not share a family connection with him. Hence he may give it to any member of the family who is a priest, e.g., one of his uncles (Radbaz).</i> from [the priestly] tribe. Similarly, if he inherited <i>terumah</i> from his maternal grandfather and partook of it, a creditor collected <i>terumah</i> [as payment] for a debt and he partook of it or a woman [received it as part of the money due her by virtue of] her <i>ketubah</i> and she partook of it, they must make restitution for the principal and the additional fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This is speaking about an instance where the person did not know that the produce he ate was <i>terumah</i> and thus transgressed unknowingly.</i> to a priest who is a <i>chaver</i> and that <i>chaver</i> gives them the monetary equivalent of the <i>terumah</i> at the time they partook of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">To explain this ruling: Since these individuals are not entitled to partake of <i>terumah</i>, they must atone for partaking of it by making restitution and adding a fifth. Nevertheless, the <i>terumah</i> legitimately belonged to them. Hence, after the priest receives the grain given for atonement, he should reimburse the person for the worth of the <i>terumah</i>.</i>
When a person steals <i>terumah</i> but does not consume it, he should pay twice its worth<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">As is required when making restitution for theft (Exodus 22:3 .</i> to the owners.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Although <i>terumah</i> is consecrated, it belongs to its owners. For even an Israelite has the right to give it to the priest he desires.</i> He may make this payment according to the worth of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Which is less expensive than ordinary grain. When making restitution to atone for partaking of <i>terumah</i>, it is necessary to pay with ordinary grain. In this instance, however, he need not atone for partaking of <i>terumah</i>. All that is necessary is to pay twice the amount of the article he stole.</i> If he stole it and ate it, he must pay twice the principal and a fifth of the principal: i.e., the principal and an additional fifth from ordinary grain,<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">To atone for partaking of <i>terumah</i>.</i> and the principal according to the worth of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">To atone for the theft.</i>
When a person steals <i>terumah</i> that is consecrated to the Temple treasury<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a priest was given <i>terumah</i> and consecrated it to the Temple treasury [the Ramban's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 6:4)]. We are forced to say this because <i>terumah</i> separated by an Israelite must be given to a priest and may not be given to the Temple treasury.</i> and eats it, he is not required to pay a double amount, for a double amount is not paid to the Temple treasury, as explained in the appropriate place.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Geneivah</i> 2:1. When speaking about the thief's obligation to pay double, Exodus 22:8 states: "He shall pay his colleague double," i.e., his colleague, another human, and not the Temple treasury (<i>Bava Metzia</i> 57b).</i> He must, however, make restitution for the principal and add two fifths, one fifth [to atone] for partaking of <i>terumah</i> and one fifth [to atone] for benefiting from consecrated property.<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">This is the standard penalty to atone for this transgression (<i>Hilchot Meilah</i> 1:3,5).</i><br>To whom does he make restitution? If [the <i>terumah</i>] was the size of an olive<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">For the liability for <i>terumah</i> depends on the produce being the size of an olive.</i> and it was not worth a <i>p'rutah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">For the liability of misusing consecrated property depends on it being worth a <i>p'rutah</i>,</i> he should make restitution to the priests. If it is worth a <i>p'rutah</i> - whether or not it is the size of an olive - he should pay the Temple treasury.
Why does the prohibition against benefiting from consecrated property fall [on this grain when it is already forbidden because of] the prohibition against <i>terumah</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's question is based on the principle that, generally, once an object is forbidden because of one prohibition, it does not become forbidden again, because of a second one (see <i>Keritot</i> 14a).</i> Because the <i>terumah</i> was forbidden to a non-priest and permitted to a priest. Once he consecrated it, it became forbidden to a priest. Therefore a prohibition was added to it even for an Israelite in the manner explained in the laws of forbidden relationships and forbidden foods.<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 17:9; <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 14:19. In these sources, it is explained that one of the exceptions to this principle is an <i>issur mosif</i>, a negative commandment that increases the scope of the prohibition, including entities that were not originally forbidden. See the Ramban's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i>
When a person obtains <i>terumah</i> by robbery,<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between <i>geneivah</i> (theft) and <i>gezeilah</i> (robbery) can be explained as follows: Theft implies taking a colleague's property discreetly. Robbery, by contrast, involves taking something by force against the will of its owner (<i>Hilchos Geneivah</i> 1:3).</i> he must make restitution for the principal and add one fifth.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">And only one fifth, in contrast to the law regarding a thief in Halachah 23, where he is required to atone for the theft as well.</i> [The rationale is that] the fifth that he is liable for [to atone for partaking of] <i>terumah</i> fulfills his obligation for robbery,<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad questions the Rambam's ruling, asking why the person is not liable for an additional fifth to atone for taking a false oath concerning the robbery, as required by <i>Hilchot Gezeilah</i> 7:1-2. The commentaries explain that according to the Rambam, it is sufficient to give one fifth, for that atones for both transgressions. Although in Halachah 24, the Rambam requires two different fifths to be given, that is because one is required to atone for partaking of consecrated property. That is a different type of transgression and that requirement is not paralleled with regard to the transgression against taking a false oath.</i> as [implied by Leviticus 22:14:] "And you shall give the priest the sacred [food]." He is liable only for the fifth [associated with atonement for] the sacred [food]. If he obtains <i>terumah</i> through robbery and feeds it to another person, that person must make restitution for the principal and add a fifth.<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">For he must atone for partaking of <i>terumah</i> unknowingly, as in Halachah 10.</i><br>Whenever we have said that a person must make restitution for the principal and add a fifth, [the intent is that] if he ate grain worth four [<i>zuz</i>], he must pay five<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the fifth is one fifth of the new total. See parallels in <i>Hilchot Arachin</i> 4:5; <i>Hilchot Meialah</i> 1:5, <i>Hilchot Gezeilah</i> 7:5, <i>et al</i>.</i> from the type of grain from which he partook. Whenever we mentioned [payment of] the principal and two fifths, [the intent is that] if he ate grain worth four [<i>zuz</i>], he must pay six. Whenever we mentioned [payment of] two principals and one fifth, [the intent is that] if he ate grain worth four [<i>zuz</i>], he must pay nine.<br>Whenever he makes restitution, he must pay for the worth of the grain at the time he partook of it whether its value depreciated at the time he made restitution or appreciated.<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad questions the Rambam's ruling, asking why is the person not required to pay the worth at the time of the robbery if the grain depreciated in price. The Radbaz explains that this is, in fact, the Rambam's intent and this halachah is speaking about an instance when he ate the <i>terumah</i> directly after stealing it. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that this halachah is not necessarily speaking about a thief, but about any person who partakes of <i>terumah</i> unknowingly. The time when he partakes of the <i>terumah</i> is equivalent to the time of theft.</i>

Chapter 11

<i>Terumah</i> may be used for eating, drinking, and smearing upon oneself, for smearing upon oneself is equivalent to drinking, as [indicated by Psalms 109:18]: "It has entered his innards like water and like oil into his bones." And drinking is like eating.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The intent is that a person may benefit from <i>terumah</i> in all the ways one ordinarily benefits from produce.</i><br>[One should] eat a substance fit to be eaten, drink a substance fit to be drunk, and smear a substance fit to be smeared. One should not smear wine and vinegar.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For it is not common to smear these substances.</i> One may, however, smear oneself with pure oil<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">One may not, however, burn pure <i>terumah</i> oil. Since it is fit to be used for a person's direct satisfaction, it should not be used merely as kindling fuel (Radbaz).</i> and may kindle impure oil. It is referred to as oil [fit for] burning universally.
It is permitted to compress dates that are <i>terumah</i> and collect them as a cake of dried dates.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For this is considered as benefiting from dates as food.</i> It is, however, forbidden to make them into beer.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylon, it was customary to make beer out of dates. Nevertheless, it is forbidden to do this from dates which are <i>terumah</i>, because one should not make <i>terumah</i> which is food into a liquid (the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, <i>Terumot</i> 11:3).</i> [For the same reason,] we do not make dates into honey, nor apples, wine, nor fall produce into vinegar.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Although fall grapes are not of the same quality as ordinary summer grapes and thus might be used for vinegar, since this is a deviation of the ordinary way in which the grapes are used, it is not appropriate to use <i>terumah</i> produce in this manner.</i> Similarly, with regard to other produce, we do not change them from their natural state if they are <i>terumah</i>; the only exceptions are olives and grapes.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For using them to produce oil and wine respectively is considered an ordinary - if not the preferable way - of using them. Indeed, when mentioning these products, Numbers 18:12 refers to them as "oil" and "wine," not as olives and grapes [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 11:3)].</i><br>If one transgressed and made food into a beverage, one should drink it.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For it is still considered as <i>terumah</i> and it should not be wasted.</i> When a non-priest partakes of date-honey, apple-wine, or the like that is <i>terumah</i> inadvertently, he is not liable to make restitution.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">For they are not considered as the fruit itself, merely as its by-products.<br>The Ra'avad maintains that if these products were already given to a priest, the non-priest must make restitution for their value. For taking them is equivalent to stealing. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>maintain that the Rambam would also accept this ruling, for in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>ibid.</i>:2), he states that the person who partakes of these products is liable for the principal, but not for an additional fifth.</i> If he partook [of such products] intentionally, he should be given stripes for rebellious conduct.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The punishment given for violating a Rabbinic prohibition. He is not considered as liable for lashes, because he is not liable for the transgression of a Scriptural prohibition for the same reason mentioned in the previous note.</i>
We may not place dried dates and dried figs [that are <i>terumah</i>] into brine.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Because this ruins them. Putting these fruits into brine would allow the juice they contained to be extracted.</i> One may, however, place wine in brine.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is done to improve the flavor of the brine.</i> We do not place fragrant herbs in oil, because doing so removes it from the category of food and makes it oil for smearing. One may mix wine, honey, pepper, and the like, in order to partake of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">It was common to make such a mixture in the Talmudic era.</i>
We do not boil wine that is <i>terumah</i>, because this reduces its quantity.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even though boiling it improves its taste and fragrance [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>ibid.</i>)].</i> We do not pickle onions that are <i>terumah</i> in vinegar that is <i>terumah</i>, because this spoils the vinegar.<br>We do not mix grain with legumes.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">For this ruins the grain and grain is more important than legumes (Radbaz).</i> All substances that become distinct from each other when they are sorted through a sieve may be mixed together. When the land of Judah was laid waste,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In the Roman conquest.</i> [the people] began mixing one type of grain with another<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">There was a sparsity of grain and the people did not care to differentiate one species from the other.</i> and one type of legumes with another. They did not, however, [mix] grain with legumes.
In the same way as ordinary flour is sifted, so too, a priest [sifts flour] that is <i>terumah</i>. He sifts [what is fit] to eat and discards the bran.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it is <i>terumah</i> and he will thus be discarding bran that is <i>terumah</i>. This is allowed since he is doing the same as he would with ordinary produce.</i> If he desires to make fine flour, he should sift it many times until he produces a <i>kab</i> or two <i>kabbim</i> from a <i>se'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>se'ah</i> contains six <i>kabbim</i>. Nevertheless, because the person is sifting the flour carefully, he may be left with only one or two <i>kabbim</i> from the entire <i>se'ah</i>.</i> He should not discard the remainder, because it is fit to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Hence it is forbidden to destroy it.</i> Instead, he should place it in a private place.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Where it will not be taken by another person.</i>
Oil that is <i>terumah</i> should not be used to seal an oven or a range,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">For this will be using it for a purpose other than a person's direct physical benefit.</i> nor should it be smeared on a shoe or sandal.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Applying oil to leather objects strengthens them (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> Nor should one smear it on his foot while [the foot] is in a shoe or sandal.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Because the oil will be applied to the shoe and the sandal directly.</i> He may, however, smear [oil that is <i>terumah</i>] on his foot and put on a shoe or smear his entire body and then roll on a new leather mat. Even though they will be smeared, he need not show concern.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Once the oil has been applied to his flesh, its sacred quality is divested. Hence, the fact that afterwards, the oil becomes applied to the leather is not significant.</i> He should not, however, place oil on a marble tablet to roll upon because he causes it to be ruined.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">One certainly is not allowed to place oil that is <i>terumah</i> on a leather mat, because that will cause it to be absorbed in the mat and thus not applied to the person's skin. One might, however, think that it is permitted to use a marble tablet for this purpose, because none of the oil will be absorbed. Nevertheless, a certain amount of oil will remain on the tablet and thus be ruined. This is forbidden (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
Whenever anyone partakes of <i>terumah</i> - even fruit<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Washing one's hands before partaking of ordinary fruit is, by contrast, considered a sign of haughtiness (<i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 6:3). With regard to bread, however, our Sages ordained that one must wash even before partaking of ordinary bread.</i> - he must wash his hands [before doing so. This applies] even if his hands are pure, as will be explained in the appropriate place.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i> 8:8. See also the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Challah</i> 1:9).</i> It may not be eaten on a table at which a non-priest [is dining; this is] a decree lest he partake of it. <i>Terumah</i> from the Diaspora<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Which is separated only by virtue of Rabbinic decree.</i> may be eaten on a table at which a non-priest [is dining] and, like ordinary food, does not require that one wash his hands before partaking of it.<br>We do not smear oil that is <i>terumah</i> with soiled<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The intent is not necessarily impure hands, but any hands that one has not watched carefully to make sure that they are pure. For if one touches oil with such hands, it is impure by Rabbinic decree (<i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTuma'ah</i> 8:8-10).</i> hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For this would make the <i>terumah</i> impure and that is forbidden. Instead, one must wash his hands before applying oil to his body (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> If, however, [such oil] fell on his flesh, he can rub it with soiled hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">For once it comes in contact with his flesh, its sacred quality departs and the fact that one's hands are impure is not significant (<i>ibid.</i>, based on <i>Keritot</i> 7a).</i> One may apply oil that is <i>terumah</i> to an infant during the seven days after he was born [even though he is uncircumcised].<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">And it is forbidden for an uncircumcised person to make use of <i>terumah</i>, as stated in Chapter 7, Halachah 10.</i> For a newborn is not considered as uncircumcised for the first seven days [of his life].<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In his gloss to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, <i>B'nei Yaakov</i> notes that although this question is left unresolved by the Babylonian Talmud (<i>Yevamot</i> 71a), the Rambam's ruling is based on the Jerusalem Talmud where the question is resolved.</i>
A priest may smear oil that is <i>terumah</i> on himself and then bring his daughter's son who is an Israelite and roll him on his back.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Even though his Israelite grandson will come in contact with the oil and benefit from it, that is of no concern for once it touches the flesh of the priest, its holiness departs and even a non-priest may benefit from it.</i> If he smears oil [that is <i>terumah</i>] on his body and enters a bathhouse, a non-priest may massage him<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> offers this interpretation in order to maintain the standard version of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. He, however, suggests that the version is in error and that the proper version is that he can attend to him.</i> in the bathhouse even though [the oil] will be applied to him.
When the daughter of a priest applied <i>chilbah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A pungent herb.</i> which is <i>terumah</i> to her hair, an Israelite woman is not permitted to apply [the remaining chilbah] to [her hair]. She may, however, rub her hair together with the hair [of the daughter of the priest].<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Although the <i>chilbah</i>will also be applied to the hair of the Israelite woman, since it was first applied to the hair of the priest's daughter, its holiness has departed and it can then be applied to the Israelite woman's hair.</i><br>Why were priests given permission to apply <i>chilbah</i> which is <i>terumah</i> to their hair?<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">For this is not considered direct physical benefit, like eating.</i> Because it is not fit for human consumption [as food].<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is not normally eaten, because its taste is too sharp. If, however, it was entirely unfit for human consumption, there would be no obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from it (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, see Chapter 2, Halachah 8, and Chapter 12, Halachah 7).</i>
The stems of figs, dried figs, wild figs, carobs, the insides of melons,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The seeds and the juice in the melon [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ediot</i> 3:3)].</i> the peels of melons, <i>esrogim</i>, and cucumbers even if they do not contain any food, and the extremities of the vegetables that are discarded by homeowners [when preparing vegetables],<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The tops and stems of vegetables that are cut off when preparing a vegetable for cooking (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> are forbidden to non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Even though they are not fit to be eaten, since they are in contact with the food, they are considered like food (Radbaz).</i> The extremities of the vegetables that are cut off by gardeners, by contrast, are permitted to non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">For they cut off only those leaves that are not at all fit to be eaten, a homeowner, by contrast, will discard even those that are slightly undesirable (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). The Rambam is apparently relying on a version of the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Terumot</i> 11:4) which is different than the standard printed version of the text.</i><br>The casings of beans and sesame seeds are forbidden if they contain food. If they do not contain food, they are permitted.
The seeds of <i>esrogim</i> are permitted to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">For they are bitter and are not fit to be eaten (Radbaz).</i> The seeds of olives, dates, and carobs, [by contrast,] even though they were not collected by a priest, are forbidden to a non-priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">For usually, there is a certain amount of food attached to them.<br>The commentaries note that the Rambam's ruling here represents a reversal of his position in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 11:5) where he rules more leniently with regard to carob seeds.</i> With regard to other seeds, [the rule is]: If they were collected and have moisture, so that they can be sucked, they are forbidden to a non-priest. If they were discarded, they are permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that since it is only fit to be eaten under pressing circumstances, there is no obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> according to Scriptural Law. Instead, the obligation is Rabbinic in origin. It is a decree, instituted so that it not be exchanged with other produce. Since it has been discarded, it will not be exchanged. Hence, the decree was never applied in such situations.</i>
Bran from wheat: Fresh bran is forbidden, for it is fit to be eaten by humans. Aged bran is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">For it is no longer considered as food.</i> Until when is the bran considered fresh? For the entire time that people are accustomed to beat the grain at the granaries.
Lower quality and slight rotten kernels of grain that are <i>terumah</i> are forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For they are still fit to be eaten.</i> If they already produce dust,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If they are aged to the degree that they produce dust, they are no longer considered food and there is no prohibition against partaking of them.</i> they are permitted. When one pours water over dregs [of grapes] that are <i>terumah</i>, the first and second batches are forbidden to non-priests, but the third is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">For the influence of the grapes is not significant at that time. The Radbaz states that this leniency applies even if the mixture has the flavor of wine.</i> If one does not pour water over them, but instead, strains off the wine from the seeds, even the third straining is forbidden to non-priests.
When one has cleared the kernels of wheat that are <i>terumah</i> from a grainheap, we do not require the owner to sit and collect the [leftover] kernels one by one and [only after collecting them] bring ordinary grain there. Instead, he may sweep the area in the ordinary manner, and then bring ordinary grain there.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that there will be some grain that is <i>terumah</i> is not significant.</i> Similarly, when a jug of oil that is <i>terumah</i> spills, we don't require [the owner] to sponge it up until [the earth] is dry.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is taken from the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 11:6).</i> Instead, he may conduct himself as he does with ordinary [oil].<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Even though some oil that is <i>terumah</i> will be left to spoil on the ground.</i>
When a person pours out oil that is <i>terumah</i> from a jug, he should continue to pour until the stream [of oil] ceases and the oil begins to drip, little by little. Once three drops have dropped one after the other, it is sufficient. It is permitted to place ordinary oil in that jug.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Even though there will be some remnants of the oil that was <i>terumah</i> there, we do not require him to clean the jug thoroughly (<i>ibid.</i>:8).</i> If, however, he did not place ordinary oil and leaned the jug on its side until the remnants [of the oil] collected together, those remnants are <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that he has diverted his attention from the oil is not significant; we do not suspect that other oil was added to the jug (<i>ibid.</i>).</i>
A priest may fill a lamp with oil fit to be burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., oil that is <i>terumah</i> that is ritually impure and hence, fit to be used as fuel for a lamp.</i> and give it to an Israelite to ascend to a loft and enter a room to perform tasks on behalf of a priest,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The oil should be burnt for the sake of the priest and not for the sake of an Israelite. Nevertheless, since the priest derives benefit from the Israelite's acts, this is permitted.</i> but not for the sake of an Israelite. If they were partners, it is permitted.
When an Israelite was a guest at a priest's [home]<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">When referring to this law, the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 331:19) mentions the converse situation as well. A priest visited an Israelite and the Israelite kindled a lamp for him using impure oil that is <i>terumah</i>. The Israelite may allow the lamp to continue burning after the priest departs.</i>  and the priest kindled oil fit to be burnt for him and then departed, he is not required to extinguish [the lamp] until it burns out on its own.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since the priest originally benefited from it, there is no prohibition in allowing it to continue to burn for the sake of the Israelite.</i> An Israelite may dip a wick in the lamp of a priest<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even though it contains oil from <i>terumah</i> that is impure.</i> and kindle it so that he may proceed [using] it.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishneh LiMelech</i> explains that according to the Rambam, the prohibition against an Israelite benefiting from impure oil that is <i>terumah</i> is Rabbinic in origin. Our Sages did not impose their decree in a situation where a Jew would be forced to walk in the dark. Because of the danger involved, the Israelite is entitled to do this even without the permission of the priest (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
When livestock belonging to a priest was standing next to livestock belonging to an Israelite or when garments belonging to a priest were being woven next to garments belonging to an Israelite, oil that is fit for burning may be kindled because of them without the permission of the priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since the priest is deriving benefit from the oil, it may be kindled without asking him. And since he is deriving benefit from it, the Israelite may also derive benefit.<br>The Radbaz emphasizes that this applies only when the priest does not object. If, however, he specifically lodges an objection, it is forbidden.</i><br>Similarly, we can light oil that is fit to be burnt in synagogues, houses of study, and dark alleyways without the permission of a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In all these instances, it is a mitzvah that benefits people at large to kindle the light and our Sages did not institute their decrees when doing so would impede the performance of such a mitzvah.</i> And a person who does not have ordinary oil to kindle a Chanukah lamp may kindle oil that is fit to be burnt without permission of a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that he is performing a mitzvah. Since mitzvot were not given for our personal satisfaction (<i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 28a), he is not considered to be deriving personal benefit from kindling the Chanukah lamp. Nevertheless, if he has other oil, it is preferable for him to use that rather than impure <i>terumah</i> oil.</i> We may kindle oil that is fit to be burnt above [the bed of] a sick person with the permission of a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">A priest must explicitly give permission for the oil to be kindled there [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 11:10)]. In contrast to the previous instances, the priest's permission is necessary in this situation, because only one individual - not people at large - are benefiting from the light (Radbaz, <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
When a daughter of an Israelite who is married to a priest frequently visits her father, he may light [such oil] with her permission.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since she is married to a priest, she is permitted to benefit from the kindling of this oil. And thus, all the laws mentioned above with regard to a priest, also apply with regard to her.</i> It is permitted for a priest to kindle oil fit to be burnt in a house of mourning or at a wedding celebration<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">For this is also considered as a mitzvah that benefits people at large.</i> even though a multitude of people are present. We do not suspect that the people will partake of it. At a wedding celebration, [this will not take place] because they will not touch it because of their garments are clean,<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The garments worn by people at a wedding celebration are very clean and they will not want to soil them with oil that was used for a lamp [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 11:10)].</i> and in a house of mourning, the people will not feel free to do so because of their mourning.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">They will be too preoccupied with their mourning to consider anything else (<i>ibid.</i>).</i>
When a person sows <i>terumah</i> unknowingly, he should turn it over.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">And thus destroy what he sowed (<i>ibid.</i> 9:1). This will be beneficial because he will be able to sow a new crop of ordinary produce instead.</i> If he did so intentionally, he must maintain [the crop].<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">This will cause him to suffer a loss, for as the Rambam states in the following halachah, the produce of such a field may not be eaten by non-priests. Thus he will not be able to sell it at the price of ordinary produce. And yet he will have to till his land just as it was ordinary produce.</i> Once it reaches a third of its growth, whether he sowed it intentionally or unknowingly, he must maintain [the crop].<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">For at this point, the crop is already considered significant (see <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 2:3-5). Thus destroying it would be similar to destroying <i>terumah</i> which is forbidden.</i> If it was flax, even if it reached a third of its growth and even if he sowed it intentionally, he must turn it over.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">And destroy it, so that he will not make use of it.</i> This is a penalty so that he should not sow it with the intention of benefiting from its fibers.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">We fear that he will interpret the prohibition as applying only to using the flax seeds as food and not to using the plant's fibers for other purposes.</i>
Produce that grows from <i>terumah</i> is considered as ordinary produce with regard to all matters<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Hence all the agricultural obligations that are incumbent on ordinary produce are incumbent upon it, as stated in Halachah 27. Although <i>Terumot</i> 9:4 states that produce which grows from <i>terumah</i> is <i>terumah</i>, the intent is that it is forbidden to non-priests. This was one of the decrees enacted by our Sages on the day the students of the School of Shammai outnumbered those of the School of Hillel [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 9:4), based on <i>Shabbat</i> 1:3].</i> except that it is forbidden to non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it does possess the sacred quality that characterizes <i>terumah</i>.</i> Our Sages decreed that it be forbidden to non-priests like <i>terumah</i> [as a safeguard], lest a priest maintain possession of impure <i>terumah</i> with the intent of sowing it and producing [a crop of] ordinary produce and this lead to undesirable consequences. Therefore, it is permitted to eat the products [of such plantings] with impure hands<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">When one hands are in a state of impurity, because of touching food that is ritually impure, as a result of a Rabbinic decree. It is not necessary to wash one's hands before partaking of such produce.</i> and a person who has immersed himself that day<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">To be purified from ritual impurity, a person must immerse himself and then wait until nightfall that day. The immersion itself is not sufficient to bring one to a state of ritual purity.</i> may partake of them like ordinary produce.
Produce grown from produce grown [from <i>terumah</i>] are like ordinary produce with regard to all matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">They may even be eaten by non-priests.</i> [This applies] even to types of produce whose seeds do not decompose<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Plants like onions or garlic (<i>Terumot</i> 9:4) that grow from an existing bulb. Although that bulb is produce grown from <i>terumah</i>, since it is forbidden only according to Rabbinic Law, when it is planted again and new produce grows from it, the Rabbinic prohibition can be nullified.</i> provided the new growth exceeds the root<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a simple majority is sufficient. We do not require 60 times its substance.</i> in the second generation of produce. [In such a situation,] the new growth elevates the root even if the seed does not decompose and the entire [plant] is permitted to be eaten by non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's ruling, stating that if the seed does not decompose, even the second generation produce that grows is forbidden. He supports his ruling based on <i>Terumot</i> 9:4 which makes such statements with regard to <i>tevel</i>, produce from which the <i>terumot</i> and tithes have not been separated. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, support the Rambam's position.</i><br>The same applies with regard to [produce that grows from] <i>terumah</i> from the Diaspora,<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since such <i>terumah</i> is forbidden only by Rabbinic decree and it is not a common matter, our Sages did not enforce their decree with regard to it.</i> <i>terumah</i> mixed with ordinary produce,<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the produce which is <i>terumah</i> is mixed with less than 100 times its volume and thus it is not nullified (see ch. 13), if the mixture is planted, the produce that grows is permitted to all.</i> extra <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">This is referring to a situation similar to those described in Chapter 3, Halachah 6, when originally, the person gave less than the required amount of <i>terumah</i> and hence was required to separate <i>terumah</i> again. The second separation is considered as <i>terumah</i> only because of Rabbinic decree and produce which grows from it is permitted.</i> or seeds from garden vegetables that are not eaten themselves, e.g., turnips and radishes. Although the turnips and radishes themselves are <i>terumah</i>, [the produce] growing from their [seeds] is like ordinary produce with regard to all matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Since the seeds themselves are not edible, they are not considered as <i>terumah</i> (although the vegetables themselves are). And since they are not considered as <i>terumah</i>, the produce that grows from them is also not considered as <i>terumah</i>.</i>Similarly, when a person sows flaxseed that is <i>terumah</i>, the plants growing from it are permitted to non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since flaxseed is usually not eaten, the requirement to separate <i>terumah</i> is only Rabbinic in origin. Hence, when the <i>terumah</i> from flaxseed is sown, it is permitted (Radbaz). The Ra'avad, however, differs concerning this and the previous instance and maintains that produce that grows from this type of <i>terumah</i> is not permitted.</i>
When a person sows <i>terumah</i> that is impure, even though the produce that grows is ritually pure, it is forbidden to partake of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Even by priests. Instead, it must be burnt, as was required of the seeds from which it grew [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 9:7)].</i> [The rationale is that] since the <i>terumah</i> that was sown was forbidden to be eaten, it was already cast off. [Hence, it remains forbidden].
If he cut off the leaves that grew and then another set of leaves grew and he cut them off, the produce that emerge afterwards is permitted to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">By a priest. It is permitted because planting it purified it from impurity. It is not permitted to an Israelite, however, because it is still the first generation of offspring from <i>terumah</i> and not the second (Ra'avad, as explained by Radbaz and <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
When plants of ordinary produce became impure and afterwards, they were sown and designated as <i>terumah</i>, they are permitted [to be eaten. The rationale is that] sowing them caused them to be considered pure and they did not become impure while they were <i>terumah</i> so that they would be forbidden.
When a stalk [of grain] was in the midst of a grain heap and one straightened the edges of the grain heap,<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This obligates that <i>terumah</i> and tithes be separated from the produce as stated in <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 3:13.</i> this stalk is considered <i>tevel</i>, because it was in the grain heap when it was straightened. If [that stalk] was planted,<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Before <i>terumah</i> was separated from that grain heap.</i> and then it was singled out and designated as <i>terumah</i>, there is an unresolved question if it is <i>terumah</i>. Since it was planted, it is possible to say that it was released from the categorization as <i>tevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's words require some qualification. Certainly, the fact that the stalk is replanted does not cause the categorization as <i>tevel</i> to be released, as evident from <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 6:6. The question here is whether the designation as <i>terumah</i> remains or not.</i> and is considered as produce for which all the required work was not completed.<br>If, however, it was designated as <i>terumah</i> before it was planted, it is <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that it was replanted does not change its own status.</i> Therefore if one ripped off [a portion] of it and ate it willfully, he is liable for death [at the hand of heaven]. If he did so unknowingly, he must [make restitution and add] a fifth. If he bent over and ate from the ground with his mouth, his intent<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">To eat in this manner.</i> is of no consequence because of the approach of people at large [and] it is not the ordinary practice for people to eat in this manner. Therefore he is not liable for death, nor for the [additional] fifth if he acted unknowingly.
The obligations of <i>leket, shichichah, pe'ah, terumah</i> the tithes and the tithe given to the poor apply with regard to a field of produce that grew from <i>terumah</i>. Poor Israelites and poor priests come and take these presents. The poor priests eat those that they acquire. The poor Israelites sell theirs to the priests for the price of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Which is less than the price of ordinary produce. This is necessary, because as stated in Halachah 21, it is forbidden for an Israelite to partake of such produce.</i> Similarly, a Levite must sell his tithes to a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">It is, however, forbidden to sell the second tithe. Instead, this produce must be given to the priests (Radbaz).</i>
A person who crushes such produce is praiseworthy. When a person threshes it with an animal, what should he do?<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the animal partakes of the produce, the owner will be benefiting from produce grown from <i>terumah</i>. And if he muzzles the animal to prevent it from doing so, he will be violating the prohibition (Deuteronomy 25:4 : "Do not muzzle an ox while it is threshing."</i> He should tie a bucket around the animals neck and place the type of grain it is threshing in it. Thus he will neither be muzzling the animal, nor feeding it <i>terumah</i>.

Chapter 12

It is forbidden to cause the <i>terumah</i> of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> to contract ritual impurity,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., to cause it to contract ritual impurity directly.</i> as [is the rule with regard to] other sacred articles.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 18:2. The <i>Sefer Mitzvot Gadol</i> considers this as one of the 613 mitzvot. The Rambam does not, however.</i> One should not bring it to a state of ritual impurity,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">To bring about a situation where it is likely that the <i>terumah</i> will contract impurity.</i> nor should one spoil it. Instead, he should partake of pure [<i>terumah</i>] and kindle impure <i>terumah</i>.<br>It is permitted to cause <i>terumah</i> from the Diaspora to become impure, even with impurity mandated by Scriptural Law. Although it is only ritually impure<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">One might still think that causing it to become impure would be forbidden, because although it is impure by Rabbinic decree, it is not impure according to Scriptural Law, and it is forbidden to spread ritual impurity.</i> because of the impurity stemming from the Diaspora which is a Rabbinic decree,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam states in <i>Hilchot Tumat Meit</i> 11:1-2, our Sages decreed that everyone living in the Diaspora is considered as if he became impure because of contact with a human corpse. Now impurity of Rabbinic origin is less severe than impurity of Scriptural origin. Nevertheless, since the obligation to separate this <i>terumah</i> is only Rabbinic in origin, our Sages did not forbid making it incur Scriptural impurity.</i> [this leniency is granted] because the obligation to separate [such <i>terumah</i>] is also of Rabbinic origin. For this reason, a woman may separate <i>challah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Which is bound by the same rules as <i>terumah</i>.</i> while in the <i>niddah</i> state in the Diaspora, for she is cautioned only against eating it, not against touching it, as we explained.
If a doubt arose whether <i>terumah</i> became impure, it should not be eaten,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Lest it have become impure and it is forbidden to partake of impure <i>terumah</i>.</i> nor should it be burnt.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Lest it not be impure and the person would be destroying <i>terumah</i> unnecessarily.</i> Instead, one should place it aside until it becomes definitely impure, [at which time,] it should be burnt. There are situations in which doubt has arisen that mandate that <i>terumah</i> should be burnt as will be explained with regard to [the laws of] ritual purity and impurity.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i> 13:13.</i>
When a question arises whether a jug of wine has become ritually impure, one should not do anything with it. One should not move it from its place, nor reveal it. Instead, he should leave it in its place until it definitely becomes impure and must be burnt.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is ruling according to the opinion of Rabban Gamliel in <i>Terumot</i> 8:8. Other opinions in the mishnah maintain that the <i>terumah</i> should be protected that it does not become impure and still others maintain that it should be left in a place where it is likely to become impure so that we will not have to wait for the mandate to destroy it.</i> We are not concerned that perhaps it will be eaten.
[The following rules apply when] a jug [of wine that is <i>terumah</i> which is pure] is broken in an upper vat and the lower vat [contains ordinary wine that is] impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">And thus we are concerned that the pure <i>terumah</i> will flow into the vat of impure wine. In such a situation, an ordinary person will not be able to partake of it, because of the <i>terumah</i> that has become mixed with it. And a priest will not be able to partake of it, because the wine that is <i>terumah</i> has become impure. Thus it will not be useful at all.</i> If it is possible for a person to save a <i>revi'it</i> of wine in a state of purity,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">When both his hands and the articles he uses are ritually pure [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 8:10)].</i> he should do so.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Even though by doing so, he will cause the wine which is <i>terumah</i> to mix with the impure wine and thus the entire mixture will become unfit for use. It is, nevertheless, desirable for him to do this, because in this way, he will be saving at least a <i>revi'it</i> of wine that is pure <i>terumah</i>.</i> If not, he should save [the wine] with his hands without washing them even though he imparts ritual impurity [to the <i>terumah</i>],<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he will make the wine ritually impure in this manner, it will become ritually impure anyway. (Moreover, the ritual impurity that he will impart to it will be only Rabbinic in origin; see also the notes to the following halachah.) And in this way, he will prevent the <i>terumah</i> from mixing with the ordinary produce and disqualifying it.</i> as will be explained [in the laws pertaining to] ritual purity.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i> 8:8,10; 9:5.</i>
When does the above apply? To a jug of wine, [and then] provided that the contents of the lower vat are less than 100 times the amount [that falls in], in which instance, the <i>terumah</i> wine will mix with the ordinary wine and everything will be disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For, as stated above, an ordinary person will not be able to partake of it because of the <i>terumah</i> and even a priest will not be able to partake of it because the <i>terumah</i> will have become impure.</i> If, however, the lower vat contained 100 times the amount of the jug and thus [the wine that descends] will be nullified because [the mixture] is 101 times the original<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 1.</i> or the jug contains oil, he should allow it to descend and become impure instead of making it impure with his own hands. [The rationale is that] the entire quantity is fit to use as fuel and there will be no great loss.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even though the oil that is <i>terumah</i> becomes impure and mixed with the contents of the lower vat, the person will not suffer the loss of the entire mixture, because it can be used as fuel.</i><br>Similarly, if a jug of oil is spilling,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">To the earth and thus will be spoiled entirely.</i> if it is possible for one to save a <i>revi'it</i> of oil in a state of purity, he should do so. If not, he should save it while ritually impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">From a comparison to the wording used in Halachah 4, the <i>Or Sameach</i> rules that with regard to wine, one is permitted to save the wine only if merely his hands are ritually impure. If his entire person is ritually impure, he is forbidden to do so. In this instance, by contrast, the person may save the oil even if his person is ritually impure.<br>The difference between the two instances can be explained as follows. With regard to wine, what the person is doing is saving the ordinary wine, not the <i>terumah</i>, because there is no use for impure wine. Hence, he is permitted to do so only if he does not impart ritual impurity of Scriptural origin to the <i>terumah</i>. With regard to the oil, he is saving the oil that is <i>terumah</i> - for impure oil can be used as fuel. Hence, he is allowed to do so even if he will be making it ritually impure.</i> Since the jug is broken, he is not adjured against saving it in a state of ritual impurity, because he is agitated.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Over the possible loss of his money.</i>
[The following laws apply when] one was traveling from place to place, carrying loaves that are <i>terumah</i>, and a gentile tells him: "Give me one of them. If not, I will touch all of them and make them ritually impure."<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Rabbinic decree, a gentile is considered as ritually impure and can convey that impurity to other objects. See <i>Hilchot Tumat Meit</i> 1:13-14.</i> He should place one of them on a rock,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">So that the gentile can take it. If one of the loaves is ritually impure, he should leave that loaf for the gentile.</i> but he should not place it in his hand so that he will not be a direct cause of the <i>terumah</i> becoming ritually impure.
[The following laws apply with regard to] <i>chilbah</i> and vetch that are <i>terumah</i>. Since they are not fit for human consumption,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Under ordinary circumstances. Nevertheless, they are eaten by humans from time to time. Otherwise, there would be no obligation to separate <i>terumah</i> from them. See Chapter 2, Halachah 8, and Chapter 11, Halachah 9.</i> it is permitted to carry out all the actions [connected with their preparation] in a state of ritual impurity. One must be careful only when placing them in the water to soak. For if one placed them in the water to soak while ritually impure, one will have made them ritually impure directly.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">And that is forbidden. Even though the person will be causing the produce to contract ritual impurity later, in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ma'aser Sheni</i> 2:3), the Rambam explains that this stage of preparation is singled out because it is when the produce comes in contact with the water that it becomes fit to contract ritual impurity.</i> Once one has soaked them, however, one need not be concerned with ritual impurity at the time one crushes the vetch<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">To remove their coatings.</i> or when one feeds them to an animal. For this reason, one may give <i>chilbah</i> and vetch that are <i>terumah</i> to a priest who is a common person.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 6, Halachah 2, it is forbidden to give <i>terumah</i> that is ritually pure to a priest who is a common person, for we fear that he will be ritually impure and yet will not be aware of his state. In this instance, however, there is no difficulty for we are not concerned with this <i>terumah</i> becoming ritually impure.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that this ruling is a minority opinion in the Mishnah (<i>Challah</i> 4:9) and the majority of the Sages rule that it is forbidden to give such <i>terumah</i> to an unlearned priest. Why then, he asks, does the Rambam accept the minority view. (Significantly, in his own Commentary to the Mishnah, the Rambam states that the majority opinion is accepted.)</i>
We do not entrust <i>terumah</i> to a priest who is an unlearned person, because he is easygoing about it and may partake of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he knows that he is a priest and that <i>terumah</i> should be eaten by priests. He does not consider himself impure and does not understand why he should not be allowed to partake of the <i>terumah</i>. Even if it is sealed with a wrapper, we fear that he will break open the wrapper and partake of the <i>terumah</i> (Radbaz).</i> We may, however, entrust [<i>terumah</i>] to an unlearned Israelite if it is stored in an earthenware container sealed with a wrapper,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is sealed closed, the Israelite will realize that there are questions of ritual purity involved and will not dare to touch it.</i> provided it is not produce that is already made fit to contract ritual impurity. [This is] a decree lest it be moved by his wife while she is in the <i>nidah</i> state.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">A woman in such a state can cause an article to become impure by moving it even though she does not touch it. See <i>Hilchot Mitamei Mishkav UMoshav</i> 8:2-3. Nevertheless, both the Ra'avad and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> question the Rambam's ruling, for seemingly, since the produce has not become fit to contract ritual impurity, why would the woman's moving it cause the produce to contract impurity?</i>
We do not separate <i>terumah</i> from olives in a state of ritual purity for an unlearned person.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Quite often the owner of the produce would be an unlearned person whom we assume is not precise in his observance of the laws of ritual purity. Nevertheless, he would often employ workers or hire a vat owner who was precise in his observance so that his produce would comply with the laws of ritual purity. The first clause is speaking about a situation where the owner of the produce had already put olives in the press and squeezed the oil from them before asking the person precise in his observance to separate <i>terumah</i>. Since the olives became subject to contract ritual impurity because of the oil, we assume that they became ritually impure through contact with an impure person. Hence, if the person who was precise in his observance would separate <i>terumah</i>, there is a probability that it is impure, but people will think that it is pure, because of the reputation of the person who is precise in his observance. Hence, he is forbidden to separate <i>terumah</i> from it.</i> We may, however, prepare ordinary olives for him in a state of ritual purity.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a person who is precise in his observance may perform the entire process, crushing the olives for their oil and separating <i>terumah</i> .</i> [This leniency was granted] so that the operator of the vat could earn his livelihood.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since the owner of the produce is not precise in his observance of ritual purity, it would be preferable not to separate <i>terumah</i> for him under any circumstances. Nevertheless, leniency was granted so that the owner of the vat could earn his livelihood.</i><br>How should this process be carried out?<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after preparing the oil and separating the <i>terumah</i>, what precautions should he take that the owner of the produce does not cause the <i>terumah</i> to contract ritual impurity?</i> He should separate an amount of produce sufficient for <i>terumah</i> and place it in a utensil that cannot contract ritual impurity, e.g., a stone utensil. When the unlearned person comes to take the ordinary produce and the <i>terumah</i>, we tell him: "Be careful not to touch the <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he is permitted to touch the utensil, but not the <i>terumah</i> itself.</i> Otherwise, the produce will become <i>tevel</i> again."<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Although this is not true, he is given this warning so that he will be careful not to touch the <i>terumah</i>. We are confident that he will heed the warning, because even the unlearned people were careful in the observance of the prohibition against partaking of <i>tevel</i> (see <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> 9:1).</i>
When an Israelite performs the tasks associated with the preparation of his produce while ritually impure, we should not harvest grapes with him. Needless to say, we should not tread the grapes with him,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In the first instance, the grapes being picked are considered fit to contract ritual impurity only by Rabbinic decree (<i>Hilchot Tumat Ochalin</i> 11:1). In the second instance, the wine is already starting to flow and the impurity is of Scriptural origin. Hence, the Rambam uses the expression: "Needless to say."</i> because the <i>terumah</i> will be made ritually impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the wine will be ritually impure, and thus the <i>terumah</i> separated from it will be ritually impure and of no value. Hence, performing these tasks is forbidden, because one is aiding a Jew in the performance of a transgression. For it is forbidden to cause produce from which <i>terumah</i> has not been separated to become impure [<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 55a; the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 4:9)].</i> One may, however, carry barrels to<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">For these barrels are empty.</i> and from the vat for him.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">For the wine has already been rendered impure.</i>
When olives and grapes have become ritually impure, we may squeeze them and separate <i>terumah</i> [from the oil or wine].<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., and the wine or oil is considered as ritually pure. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that we are speaking about a situation in which the olives or grapes had become impure with a person who himself had come in contact with a source of ritual impurity. Thus the grapes and olives are considered as ritually impure to the second degree. Making the grapes and the olives impure does not make the wine and the oil impure, because something that is impure to the second degree does not convey impurity to ordinary produce (see <i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i> 11:2). Hence once this wine and oil has been squeezed out, <i>terumah</i> that is ritually pure can be separated from it.
