The Radbaz emphasizes that in this instance as well, we are speaking about using less than an egg-sized portion of produce at a time as will be explained.</i> If [grapes or the olives] became impure after they themselves were designated as <i>terumah</i>, they should be squeezed [using] less than an egg-sized portion at a time.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Tumat Ochalin</i> 9:2 explains that as long as the impure produce is less than an egg-sized portion, the fact that it comes in contact with the liquids is not significant.</i> In such an instance, the liquid that flows from them is permitted to be drunk by the priests. Indeed, it would be fit to be used for libations on the altar. [The rationale is that] the liquid is considered as set aside within the fruit.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Thus touching the fruit is not considered as touching the liquids (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> The reason that [our Sages] spoke of less than an egg-sized portion<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">And not an egg-sized portion itself.</i> is that this is a decree lest one use more than an egg-sized portion and the liquid become impure because of [contact with] the egg-sized portion.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">For with regard to <i>terumah</i>, impurity of the second degree can impart impurity of the third degree (<i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i> 11:3).</i><br>If the produce was impure to the third degree, one may tread upon it in the vat and the liquid produced from it can be used for pure <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it came into contact with the olives or grapes that were impure.</i> [The rationale is that] ritual impurity of the third degree does not bring about ritual impurity of the fourth degree with regard to <i>terumah</i>, as will be explained with regard to the concept of ritual purity.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah, loc. cit.</i>:2</i>
When a loaf of bread that is <i>terumah</i> becomes impure, it should be discarded among the firewood<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if it is placed in the woodpile, people will not regard it as food and they will not partake of it. The point of this entire halachah is to emphasize appropriate safeguards so that people will not partake of impure <i>terumah</i>. Needless to say, it is desirable to burn the impure <i>terumah</i> immediately. The point of this halachah is to provide guidelines should one desire to save it to use as fuel.</i> until one uses it for fuel. Similarly, if oil [that is <i>terumah</i>] becomes impure, it should be placed in a repugnant container until it is used as fuel so that it will not create a stumbling block for others, [lest] they partake of it.<br>When wheat [kernels] become impure, one should parboil them<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">So that they will no longer be fit to be used to make flour.</i> and place them in a repugnant container until they are no longer fit to be eaten and then use them as fuel for an oven or range. Liquids that are not fit to be used as fuel, e.g., wine, should be buried.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that wine that is <i>terumah</i> that became impure could also be used for its fragrance. Nevertheless, our Sages did not desire that such wine be set aside for this purpose, lest one partake of it unknowingly.</i>
When wine that is <i>terumah</i> is left uncovered, since it is forbidden to drink it,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Lest a poisonous snake have drank from the wine and deposited its venom there [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 8:4); see also <i>Hilchot Rotzeach UShemirat Nefesh</i> 11:7-8)].</i> it should be poured out. Similarly, figs, grapes, marrow, squash, watermelon, and cucumbers that are <i>terumah</i> that were discovered to be perforated are forbidden to be eaten because of the mortal danger involved.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Here also we fear that the produce was perforated by a poisonous snake who deposited its venom there (<i>ibid.</i> 12:2).</i> What should be done with them? They should be discarded in the sea or buried.
When a dough was kneaded with water that was left uncovered,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">And is thus forbidden for the reason mentioned in the notes to the previous halachah (<i>ibid.</i> 11:15).</i> it should be burnt even though it is <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Even though one will be wasting <i>terumah</i>, we show concern for the danger that could be involved.</i>
It is forbidden for a priest to take <i>terumah</i> or any other of the presents given to him until they were separated by the owners [of the produce], as [implied by the verses, Numbers 18:12]: "The first [of the produce] which they will give to God I have given to you" and [<i>ibid.</i>:19]: "...that the children of Israel will offer up to God are given to you." [Implied is that] the children of Israel must offer it up and then [the priests] can acquire it.<br>[The priests] should not take [these presents] after they have been set aside except with the knowledge of the owners. For the owners have the right to give them to any priest that they desire, as [<i>ibid.</i> 5:10] states: "A person's sacraments will belong to him."<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., though he may not make use of them, the right to give them away is his.</i> [Nevertheless,] if [a priest]<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that this applies even to an unlearned priest. Although <i>terumah</i> should not be given to such a priest lest he cause it to become impure, if he takes it, we do not expropriate it from him.</i> takes them without the owner's knowledge, he acquires them. For all that belongs to the owner is the right to apportion them and that right is not financially significant.
When <i>terumah</i> was given to a priest and he found other entities in it, it is forbidden for him [to take them]. This is comparable to theft, for perhaps other people placed them [in the <i>terumah</i>] to take them [afterwards].<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we suspect that a person understood that the <i>terumah</i> was set aside, not to be used, and deposited other articles there for safekeeping.</i>
An Israelite is not required to trouble himself with <i>terumah</i> and bring it from the granary until a city or from a desert until a settled area. Instead, the priests should go out to the granaries and the Israelites should give them their portions there. If they did not go out [to collect the <i>terumah</i>, the owner] should separate it and leave it in the granary. Our Sages ordained that if there are beasts or animals who would eat it there and it is not protected from them, [the owner] should take the trouble of dealing with it and bringing it to the city. He may collect a wage for bringing it [to the city] from the priest. [This is done,] because if he separated it and left it for the animals and beasts, God's name would be desecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">For <i>terumah</i> is referred to as "sanctified," allowing it to be destroyed in this manner desecrates God's name (Radbaz).</i>
It is forbidden for the priests and the Levites<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">To whom the tithes are given.</i> to assist [farmers] in the granaries in order to receive the agricultural presents given them.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">This applies to the presents required by Scriptural Law. They may, however, render assistance in order to receive those mandated by Rabbinic Law (<i>Hilchot Bikkurim</i> 5:13).</i> Whoever renders such assistance desecrates the holiness of God's name. Concerning such people is applied [the malediction, Malachi 2:8]: "You have defiled the covenant of Levi." It is forbidden for an Israelite to allow [a priest] to help him. Instead, he should grant them their portion with honor.
When a person gives <i>terumah</i> to a priest on the condition that he returns it,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">And then the <i>terumah</i> would belong to the Israelite who - although he could not partake of it - could sell it to a priest.</i> he has fulfilled his obligation to give.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">For such a present is a valid transfer of property. See <i>Hilchot Lulav</i> 8:10; <i>Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 3:9.</i> It is, however, forbidden to do so, for [the priest] is considered like one who helps in the granaries.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he is performing a favor for the owner in return for his <i>terumah</i>.</i> Similarly, it is forbidden for [the priests] to seize the <i>terumot</i> and the tithes. It is even forbidden for them to make a verbal request for them. Instead, they should take them with honor, for they are eating and drinking from the table of God.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Since the presents are God's, as it were, they should be treated with honor.</i> These presents are given to Him and He apportioned them to them, as [Numbers 18:8] states: "Behold I have given to you the safeguard of My <i>terumah</i>."<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the verse implies that <i>terumah</i> belongs to God and that He grants it to the priests.</i>
A person should not give <i>terumah</i> to the watchman of his vat, nor a firstborn animal to the watchman of his flock, nor the presents [given when an animal is slaughtered]<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Bikkurim</i>, ch. 9.</i> to the shepherd of his animals. If he gave them [to these individuals], he desecrated [their honor] unless he gave them their wages for watching first.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">If he gives them the presents first, it would appear that he is bargaining with them to reduce their wages. This would be similar to a priest assisting in the granary which is forbidden.</i><br>One Israelite<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz emphasizes that such statements may be made only by an Israelite. A priest is forbidden to do so, for this is similar to helping in the granaries.</i> may tell another Israelite: "Here is a <i>sela</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">A silver coin of the Talmudic period.</i> Give <i>terumah</i>...", "...a firstborn...", or other presents [given to the priests] "...to so-and-so, the priest, who is my daughter's son, or "...my sister's son," or the like.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since the owner has the right to do what he wants with the presents, he is permitted to receive gifts for them as well.</i>
When does the above apply? When the owner desired to give [the presents] to one of two priests or [tithes] to one of two Levites gratis and his colleague told him: "Here is this and give to that one." If, however, an owner told a priest or a Levite: "Here is this portion. [Give me something in exchange] for my privilege to give it to whom I want," this is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">For such an arrangement looks like the person is selling the <i>terumah</i>.</i> Similarly, it is forbidden to sell <i>terumah</i> as merchandise even though he purchases it from a priest and sells it to a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">For it was granted to the priests for consumption and not for commercial purposes.</i>
There are ten individuals to whom <i>terumah</i> is not given in the granaries even though they may partake of it or grant others the right to partake of it:<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., their wives and servants may partake of it.</i><br>a deaf-mute, an intellectually or emotionally unstable person, or a child who does not know how to spread out his hands to recite the priestly blessing,<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is taken from the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>. Although <i>Hilchot Tefilah</i> 15:4 states that a priest should not bless the people until he is old enough to grow a beard, that can be interpreted as meaning that he should not bless the people alone. With other priests, however, he may bless them.</i> these [are not given <i>terumah</i>], because they lack sufficient knowledge;<br>a <i>tumtum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">A person with a massive of flesh covering his sexual organs that prevents his gender from being known.</i> and an <i>androgynus</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">A person with both masculine and feminine sexual organs.</i> because they are unique types of people;<br>a servant, lest passersby in the field [see him taking <i>terumah</i>] and give testimony that he is a priest;<br>an uncircumcised person and a ritually impure person,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">They are forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>, but they could be taking for their wives, children, or servants.</i> because they are abhorrent;<br>a woman [married to a priest], lest she be divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">And thus become forbidden to partake of <i>terumah</i>.</i> and [to prevent her from] entering into seclusion [with someone in the granary];<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">For these are not places frequented by multitudes of people. <i>Yevamot</i> 99b mentions these two rationales as separate views. The Rambam, however, does not see them as mutually exclusive and combines them both.</i> and<br>a person who marries a woman who is not appropriate for him;<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Yevamot, loc. cit.</i>) interprets this as meaning a woman whom the priest is forbidden to marry.</i> he was penalized in that [<i>terumah</i>] would not be allocated to him in the granaries until he divorces her.<br>In all the above instances, [<i>terumah</i>] may be sent to their homes and allocated to them as is the law regarding other sacramental foods dependent on the boundaries [of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>] with the exception of a person who marries a woman who is not appropriate for him, an uncircumcised person<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that <i>Yevamot, loc. cit.</i>, does not place an uncircumcised person in this category if his failure to circumcise himself comes as a result of forces beyond his control, e.g., his brothers died because of circumcision. He suggests that perhaps the Rambam had a different version of that passage.</i> and a ritually impure person, to whom [<i>terumah</i>] is not sent at all.

Chapter 13

<i>Terumah</i> becomes nullified in a mixture 101 times the size of the original quantity.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:16, the Rambam explains:

Why did [the Sages] choose the figure of 100 for <i>terumot</i>? For <i>terumat ma'aser</i> is one hundredth of the entire crop, and yet it causes the entire crop to be "sanctified," as [Numbers 18:29] states: "its sacred part." Our Sages said: "An entity which must be separated from it sanctifies it, if it returns to it.

Nevertheless, from Halachah 13-14, it appears that according to Scriptural Law, <i>terumah</i> is nullified when mixed with a majority of ordinary produce and the verse is cited merely as a support.</i><br>What is implied? When a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> falls into 100 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce and all the produce becomes mixed together,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, if the produce which is <i>terumah</i> is distinct, it is sufficient for him to remove it.</i> he should separate one <i>se'ah</i> and give it to the priest. The remainder is permitted [to be eaten by] non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:15, the Rambam explains: "Why is it necessary to separate [a measure of] <i>terumah</i> and not a measure of <i>orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard? Because <i>terumah</i> is the property of the priests." I.e., from a ritual perspective, it is not necessary to remove the <i>se'ah</i>, for the existence of the <i>terumah</i> has been nullified. Nevertheless, from a financial perspective, it is necessary to give the priest his due. This is the explanation of the concluding clause.</i><br>[Accordingly,] whenever the <i>terumah</i> is a substance which the priests do not care about, e.g., <i>terumah</i> from wild figs, carobs, Edomite barley and the like, it is not necessary to separate [a hundredth for the priest]. Instead, since it fell into a 100 times its amount, it is nullified because of its minimal size and the entire mixture is permitted to non-priests.
If a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell into less than 100 [<i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce], the entire mixture becomes <i>miduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">And it is forbidden for a non-priest to partake of it.<br>In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Demai</i> 1:3), the Rambam explains that Exodus 22:23 uses the term <i>dima</i> as a synonym for <i>terumah</i>. Hence our Sages referred to a mixture of <i>terumah</i> and ordinary produce in this manner.</i> It should be sold to a priest at the price of <i>terumah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Which is far less than the price of ordinary produce. Since the <i>terumah</i> is not nullified, we have to consider the possibility that every kernel is <i>terumah</i>.</i> with the exception of that <i>se'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Which is given to him without cost.</i><br>When does the above apply? When produce becomes mixed with its own kind. If, however, produce becomes mixed with produce of another type, [the ruling is dependent on whether] the flavor [of the <i>terumah</i>] is recognizable or not. If the entire mixture has the flavor of <i>terumah</i>, it is all considered as <i>miduma</i> and should be sold to the priests with the exception of the value of the <i>terumah</i>. If the flavor of the entire mixture is that of the ordinary produce, the entire mixture is permitted to non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since the flavor of the <i>terumah</i>is not recognizable, it is considered as nullified. This principle applies with regard to all the Torah's prohibitions [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 2:7)].</i>
When a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> falls into 100 <i>se'ah</i> and one <i>se'ah</i> was removed from the mixture<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">As required by Halachah 1.</i>, if that <i>se'ah</i> fell into other produce, the question whether the mixture is considered as <i>miduma</i> is determined according to the proportion [of <i>terumah</i> in the first mixture].<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the <i>se'ah</i> was taken out to be given to the priest, it is not considered as <i>terumah</i>. Instead, we calculate the proportion of <i>terumah</i> in the first mixture, on that basis, determine how much of the <i>se'ah</i> that fell is considered to be <i>terumah</i> and then see if that amount is one hundredth of the new mixture or not. For example, if one <i>se'ah</i> fell in one hundred <i>se'ah</i>, we consider the <i>se'ah</i> that was removed as slightly less than 1/100th <i>terumah</i>. Thus if it fell into a <i>se'ah</i> or more of ordinary produce, the second mixture is permitted [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 5:5)].</i>Similarly, if a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell into less than one hundred <i>se'ah</i> [of ordinary produce] and the entire mixture became <i>miduma</i> and then some of this mixture fell into other [produce], the question whether the mixture is considered as <i>miduma</i> is determined according to the proportion [of <i>terumah</i> in the first mixture].<br>What is implied? Ten <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell into 90 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce and the entire mixture became <i>miduma</i>. If ten <i>se'ah</i> from this mixture fell into less than 100 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce, the mixture is considered <i>miduma</i>, because in the ten <i>se'ah</i> of the [original] mixture, there was at least one <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">And thus a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> is being mixed with less than 100 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce.</i> If less than ten <i>se'ah</i> [of <i>terumah</i>] fell into [the original mixture], the [second] mixture is not considered as <i>miduma</i> [when one <i>se'ah</i> of the first mixture fell into 100 <i>se'ah</i>].
When does the above apply? With regard to substances that do not become blended together, e.g., wheat kernels with wheat kernels or flour with flour.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, every entity remains discrete. It's only that an observer cannot distinguish between the <i>terumah</i> and the ordinary produce (see Radbaz).</i> When, however, substances blend together, e.g., oil that is <i>terumah</i> mixes with ordinary oil or wine that is <i>terumah</i> mixes with ordinary wine, we follow the majority. If the majority is <i>terumah</i>, should the mixture fall into other produce, the ruling is the same as if [the first mixture] was [entirely] <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., instead of calculating the percentage of <i>terumah</i> alone in the new mixture, we consider the first mixture as if it were <i>terumah</i>. Only if the second mixture is 100 times as large as the first is it permitted.</i> If the majority of the [first] mixture is ordinary produce, should that mixture fall into other produce, [the entire first mixture] is considered as ordinary produce and there is never a difficulty concerning a mixture of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">We do not calculate the percentage of <i>terumah</i> in the second mixture. Even if the <i>terumah</i> is more than one hundredth of the second mixture, that mixture is permitted.</i> Nevertheless, in all instances, the entire [first] mixture is forbidden to non-priests.
When a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> falls into 100 <i>se'ah</i>, [a <i>se'ah</i> of the mixture] was removed, another [<i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i>] fell in, [another <i>se'ah</i>] was removed, and another fell in, the ordinary produce is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since the mixture was permitted, it is considered as if the <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> that fell into it does not exist. We do not consider it as existing within the mixture, so that were it to be combined with other <i>terumah</i>, the entire mixture would be considered <i>miduma</i>.</i> until there is a majority of <i>terumah</i> [in the mixture]. Thus if more than 100 <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell into 100 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce, <i>se'ah</i> after <i>se'ah</i> [in above manner], the entire mixture is considered as <i>miduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam concerning this figure, maintaining that the mixture is considered as <i>miduma</i> if 51 <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fall into the ordinary produce in the above manner. His rationale is that since a <i>se'ah</i> is removed from the mixture, it is possible that he is removing a <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce. Hence, after 51 <i>se'ah</i> fell and 50 <i>se'ah</i> were removed, it is possible that there is a majority of <i>terumah</i> in the mixture. The Radbaz justifies the Rambam's ruling, explaining that it is logical to assume that each <i>se'ah</i> that is removed has an proportionate amount of <i>terumah</i> and ordinary produce.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell into 100 [<i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce] and before one <i>se'ah</i> was removed, another <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell [into the mixture]. If [the owner] was aware of the first <i>se'ah</i> before the second <i>se'ah</i> fell, [the mixture] does not become <i>miduma</i>. Instead, he should remove two <i>se'ah</i> and the remainder is permitted. [The rationale is that] since it should have been taken out,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the entire mixture would be considered as permitted.</i> we consider it as if it was taken out. If, however, he did not become aware of the first <i>se'ah</i> until after the second fell in, the mixture is considered <i>miduma</i>. It is as if both <i>se'ah</i> fell in at the same time.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad comments on the Rambam's ruling, noting that he is following what appears to be the minority opinion in <i>Terumot</i> 5:8. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> questions the intent of the Ra'avad's comments and asserts that according to the <i>Tosefta</i>, the majority opinion also accepts the distinction the Rambam makes here. This interpretation is borne out by the Rambam's Commentary to that mishnah.</i>
The waste products of <i>terumah</i> are not combined with it [a mixture of it and ordinary produce] to cause the ordinary produce to be forbidden. The waste products of ordinary produce, by contrast, are combined with it to cause <i>terumah</i> to be nullified in a mixture.<br>What is implied? A <i>se'ah</i> of high quality wheat that is <i>terumah</i> fell into 100 <i>se'ah</i> of wheat of low quality that is ordinary produce. The owner ground the entire quantity. Even though there is much bran in the ordinary produce and a small amount in the <i>terumah</i> and thus the flour from the <i>terumah</i> is more than one hundredth of the flour from the ordinary produce,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">For high quality grain produces more flour and less bran than lower quality grain. Thus more of the lower quality grain is bran and more of the higher quality grain is flour.</i> it is nullified. For we measure the flour with the bran and [together,] it is 101 times [the original amount of <i>terumah</i>]. If, however, a <i>se'ah</i> of low quality wheat that is <i>terumah</i> falls into [slightly] less 100 <i>se'ah</i> of high quality wheat that is ordinary produce and [the owner] ground the entire quantity, [the weight of] the flour that is <i>terumah</i> will be one hundredth of [the weight of] the flour that is ordinary produce. Hence it is nullified because the mixture is 101 times the original amount [of <i>terumah</i>], for the weight of the ordinary produce increased and that of the <i>terumah</i> decreased.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad notes that the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Terumot</i> 5:9) goes even further and says that the bran in the <i>terumah</i>, since it is considered waste and not food, can be considered as part of the ordinary produce and if there is 100 times the weight of the flour from the <i>terumah</i> when this bran is added to the ordinary produce, the <i>terumah</i> is nullified. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggest that the Rambam does not mention this point, because he feels that the Babylonian Talmud - according to which halachah is decided - does not accept it. Nevertheless, it appears that the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 99:1) follows the Ra'avad's view, although the Rama states that as a stringency, the Rambam's perspective should be followed.</i>
When a <i>log</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">A measure of liquid weight of the Talmudic period.</i> of clear wine that is <i>terumah</i> fell into 100 <i>lugin</i> of cloudy wine<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the dregs had been removed from the wine that is <i>terumah</i>, but had not been removed from the wine which was ordinary produce.</i> that is ordinary produce, we do not remove the dregs from the wine [and only then calculate whether the ordinary wine is 100 times the <i>terumah</i>]. Instead, we nullify the <i>log</i> of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Counting the dregs of the ordinary produce as part of the mixture.</i> Similarly, if a <i>log</i> of cloudy wine [that is <i>terumah</i>] fell into a 100 <i>log</i> of clear wine, we do not remove the dregs in [the wine that is <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is apparently saying that in this instance, the dregs of the <i>terumah</i> wine are counted and unless the ordinary wine is 100 times the amount of that wine including its dregs, it is considered as <i>miduma</i>. See Chapter 11, Halachah 13, which states that the dregs are considered as <i>terumah</i>.<br>The Ra'avad differs and maintains that in this instance, like the one described in the previous halachah, the dregs of the <i>terumah</i> are not counted, because they are wastes. The Radbaz justifies the Rambam's ruling, explaining that wine dregs are different than the wastes mentioned in the previous halachah, because they have the flavor of wine and can produce wine.</i>
When a <i>log</i> of water fell into 99 <i>lugim</i> of wine and then a <i>log</i> of wine that is <i>terumah</i> fell into the mixture, the entire mixture is considered as <i>miduma</i>, because water does not nullify [the existence of] wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad explains the rationale for this ruling as follows: The water is not considered as the same type as wine. Hence, it cannot nullify it unless the flavor of the wine is no longer noticeable. The wine, by contrast, is considered its type and it requires 100 times the weight of the <i>terumah</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that this can be understood as the Rambam's intent.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell into less than 100 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce and then other ordinary produce fell into the mixture so that there was more than 100 times [the weight of the <i>terumah</i>]. If [the second batch of produce was added] unknowingly, [the <i>terumah</i>] is nullified because there is 101 [times the original weight]. If he mixed it intentionally, the entire mixture is considered as <i>miduma</i>, because we do not nullify the existence of substances prohibited by Scriptural Law as an initial preference.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz notes that in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:25, the Rambam writes:

It is forbidden to nullify a substance forbidden by Scriptural Law as an initial and preferred measure. If, however, one nullified it, the mixture is permitted. Nevertheless, our Sages penalized such a person and forbade the entire mixture. It appears to me that since this is a penalty, we forbid this mixture only to the person who transgressed and nullified the prohibited substance. For others, however, the entire mixture is permitted.

In the present instance, however, it appears that the produce is considered as <i>miduma</i>, not only for the person who mixed together, but for everyone. The Radbaz differentiates between the two situations, explaining that in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i>, the Rambam is speaking about a forbidden substance. Hence, if it was considered forbidden, it would have no value entirely. In our halachah, even if the mixture is considered <i>miduma</i>, it can be sold to priests and thus, it will not be wasted entirely.</i>
It is, however, permitted to nullify <i>terumah</i> from the Diaspora [by mixing it] with a majority of permitted substances<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since the prohibition is of Rabbinic origin, one may nullify it as an initial preference, as stated in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:26.</i> and eat it during the time when one is ritually impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz explains that the mixture may be eaten by a priest even when he is ritually impure or by a non-priest.</i> Not only that, if a person possesses wine that is <i>terumah</i> from the Diaspora, he should take one log of this wine and mix it with two <i>lugim</i> of ordinary [wine]. Thus there are three <i>lugim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">And the wine that is <i>terumah</i> is nullified, because it is mixed with a majority of ordinary produce.</i> Afterwards, he can add another <i>log</i> of the <i>terumah</i> wine into the three <i>lugim</i> and then take one <i>log</i> from the four and drink it.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs, maintaining that once there is no longer a majority of ordinary produce, the mixture is forbidden. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> justifies the Rambam's ruling, explaining that once the <i>terumah</i> is nullified, it does not become a factor again if other <i>terumah</i> is added.</i> He may then add another <i>log</i> [of <i>terumah</i> wine] and take out a <i>log</i> and drink it. Similarly, he may continue adding a <i>log</i> of <i>terumah</i> and removing a <i>log</i> until all the wine that is <i>terumah</i> is completed. Thus he can nullify several <i>lugim</i> [of <i>terumah</i>] in two <i>lugim</i> of ordinary produce.
When one sows <i>terumah</i> next to ordinary produce and cannot identify which produce is <i>terumah</i> and which is ordinary produce, the entire batch is permitted even if there were 100 rows of <i>terumah</i> and [only] one row of ordinary produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that the produce that grows from <i>terumah</i> is not <i>terumah</i> and is forbidden to non-priests only as a stringency (Chapter 11, Halachot 21-22). Hence, if there is any confusion about which produce is <i>terumah</i>, it is all permitted.</i><br>When does the above apply? With regard to produce whose seed decomposes in the earth, e.g., wheat, barley, and the like.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For then there is no trace of the original plant.</i> If, however, the seeds do not decompose, e.g., garlic and onions,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, the new plant grows from a bulb of the original one and that original plant never decomposes entirely.</i> even if 100 rows are ordinary produce and one row is <i>terumah</i>, the entire mixture is <i>miduma</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For the <i>terumah</i> is distinct and has not become mixed with the ordinary produce [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 9:5)].</i> If the entire crop is harvested,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Without paying attention to the prohibition.</i> <i>terumah</i> can be nullified in a mixture of 100 times its weight, but, as an initial and preferred option, one should not harvest the crop.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">For as stated above, as an initial preference, a prohibited substance should not be nullified. It must, however, be emphasized, the Rambam's intent is not to let the produce remain in the ground forever. Instead, it should be harvested as produce which is <i>miduma</i> and sold to priests at the price of <i>terumah</i>.</i>
When there are two containers of produce, one containing <i>terumah</i> and one containing ordinary produce and it is not known [which contains the ordinary produce and which contains the <i>terumah</i>], if [the contents of] one of these containers falls into ordinary produce, [the mixture] is not considered as <i>miduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since we are unsure of the identity of the produce that fell into the mixture, we do not rule it forbidden because of the doubt. Instead, we say that the ordinary produce fell into it.<br>This halachah involves produce that is forbidden as <i>terumah</i> according to Rabbinic decree, e.g., <i>terumah</i> from the Diaspora or a mixture of <i>terumah</i>and a majority of ordinary produce. These principles are also applied in other contexts, see Chapter 10, Halachah 14, and <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 111:1).</i> [This same law applies if produce that is] <i>terumah</i> fell into one of two containers [of ordinary produce], but one does not know which one and afterwards, one of those containers fell into ordinary produce.<br>Similarly, if one sowed [the grain from] one of the two containers, the produce that grows is considered as ordinary produce with regard to all matters.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the original was <i>terumah</i></i> [The produce in] the remaining container is considered as <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the doubt applying to its status.</i> If [the contents of] the second container fell into other produce, [the mixture] is not considered as <i>miduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The same principle applies here. Since we are unsure if the identity of the produce which fell into the mixture, we do not rule that it is forbidden because of the doubt.</i> Similarly, if another person sowed [the contents of] the second container, the produce that grows is considered as ordinary produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since each person asks concerning the status of the produce that he sowed individually, both are permitted, because in each instance, there is a doubt.</i><br>If [the contents of] both containers fall into one mixture of other produce, the mixture is considered <i>miduma</i> according to the amount of produce in the smaller container.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the larger container contained one <i>se'ah</i> and the smaller container contained half a <i>se'ah</i>, we require the mixture to contain 50 ½ <i>se'ah</i> to be permitted, not 101 <i>se'ah</i>.</i> If one person sowed both of them,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the same person is asking about both plantings of produce. Hence, there is more room for stringency.</i> if the produce is a species where the seed decomposes, the produce that grows is considered as ordinary produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Since no trace of the original produce remains, we rule leniently.</i>If the seed does not decompose, the produce that grows is <i>miduma.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the produce concerning which a doubt arose originally continues to exist, stringency is called for.</i><br>When does the above apply? When one sowed [the contents of] the second container before the first batch of produce was harvested.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">For then it is considered as if he sowed them both together.</i> If, however, he harvested the first batch of produce before sowing the second, the produce that grows is considered as ordinary produce even when the seed does not decompose. [The rationale is that] produce that has been reaped and produce that is growing are not considered as indicators of each other's status.
When there are two containers [of produce], one containing ordinary produce and the other, containing <i>terumah</i> and two <i>se'ah</i>, one of ordinary produce and one of <i>terumah</i>, and both <i>se'ah</i> fall into these containers, [one into each], the produce is permitted. We assume that the ordinary produce fell into [the container of] ordinary produce and that the <i>terumah</i> fell into [the container of] <i>terumah</i>. [This applies] even though the weight of the ordinary produce is not greater than that of the <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">But rather they are of the same weight. Were there to be a majority of ordinary produce, according to Scriptural Law, the existence of the <i>terumah</i> would already be nullified and thus there would be greater room for leniency. Nevertheless, as the Rambam continues to explain, even when there is not a majority, since the entire question is one of Rabbinic Law, we allow leniency. If, however, there is a majority of <i>terumah</i>, even when it is forbidden only according to Rabbinic Law, the mixture is forbidden.</i><br>When does the above apply? With regard to <i>terumah</i> in the present era, for the requirement is of Rabbinic origin.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 26.</i> If the <i>terumah</i> is mandated by Scriptural Law, [the above ruling does not apply unless] the weight of the ordinary produce is greater than that of the <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In such an instance, the existence of the <i>terumah</i> would already be nullified and thus there would be greater room for leniency, since then the question is one of Rabbinic Law. The above leniency applies only with regard to such questions and not to questions involving Scriptural Law.</i>
When a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> falls into a grainheap<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">From which <i>terumah</i> and the tithes have not been separated.</i> and [the owner] states: "The <i>terumah</i> of this grainheap is in its midst," the borders of the <i>terumah</i> are defined where the <i>se'ah</i> fell and the entire mixture becomes <i>miduma</i> because of the <i>terumah</i> which fell in and the <i>terumah</i> of the grainheap.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Though in and of itself, the amount of <i>terumah</i> which fell in the grainheap would not disqualify it, when it is combined with the <i>terumah</i> that was designated it does.<br>The Ra'avad appears to have had a different version of the Jerusalem Talmud, <i>Terumot</i> 3:3, the Rambam's source. Hence he differs with the Rambam's ruling..</i><br>If he said: "The <i>terumah</i> of the grainheap is in its northern portion,"<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This law applies even if no <i>terumah</i> has fallen into the grainheap.</i> we divide the grainheap in half, and then the northern half in half. Thus the northern most quarter of the grainheap is <i>miduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Because there is less than one hundred times the weight of the <i>terumah</i> in that corner.</i>
If there were two grainheaps before a person and he said: 'The <i>terumah</i> for both grainheaps is in one of them," they are both considered <i>miduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The one which contains the <i>terumah</i> is certainly <i>miduma</i>. Since we do not know which one that is, they are both considered as <i>miduma</i>.</i><br>If there were two <i>se'ah</i> of grain and one grainheap before a person and he said: "One of these <i>se'ah</i> is considered <i>terumah</i> for this grainheap," one of them is <i>terumah</i> and he does not know which.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Hence he must observe the restrictions of <i>terumah</i> with regard to both of them.</i> If there were two grainheaps and one <i>se'ah</i> before him and he said: "This is <i>terumah</i> for one of the grainheaps," [the <i>se'ah</i>] is <i>terumah</i> and [<i>terumah</i> has been separated from] one of the grainheaps, but he does not know which one is no longer <i>tevel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Hence <i>terumah</i> must be separated in a conditional manner. One must bring other produce and say: "If <i>terumah</i> has not been separated for this grainheap, than this is <i>terumah</i> for it. But if it is the other grainheap from which <i>terumah</i> has not been separated, it is <i>terumah</i> for that."</i>

Chapter 14

[The following laws apply when] there were fifty dark figs and fifty<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The same laws also apply if there are 30 light figs and 70 dark ones, or any other combination of numbers (Radbaz).</i> light figs and one fig that is <i>terumah</i> fell among them. If it was light, the dark figs are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Because the fig that was <i>terumah</i> was not dark.</i> and the light figs are considered <i>miduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Because there are not enough to nullify its presence. We do not count the dark figs together with the light figs, because there is no possibility to mix the two with each other.</i> If it was dark, the dark figs are <i>miduma</i> and the light figs are permitted. If it is not known whether it was light or dark, it can be nullified when the mixture is 101 times [the weight of] the original fig.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Although the fig that was mixed in has a specific color, both types of figs can be counted together to nullify it, for it is possible to press all the figs into a single cake of figs (Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura, <i>Terumot</i> 4:7). Alternatively, since we do not know which type of fig fell in, the fact that it was of a specific color is not significant to us (<i>Yayin Malchut</i>).</i> If he knew what type of fig it was but forgot, they are all considered <i>miduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that since at the outset he knew the color of the fig and there are neither enough dark figs or light figs to nullify it, the fig is considered as forbidden. And once it is forbidden, his forgetting its color does not cause it to become permitted again (Radbaz).</i>
Similar laws apply when there are cakes of pressed figs or blocks of pressed figs and a cake or block of [figs that are] <i>terumah</i> falls among them. If it is not known whether the one which fell in was a block or a cake, the cakes and the blocks are combined and the <i>terumah</i> can be nullified if their [combined weight] is 101 times that of the <i>terumah</i>. [Similarly,] when there are large cakes of figs and small ones and a cake of figs [that is <i>terumah</i>] falls among them, but it is not known whether the cake which fell in was large or small, [the existing cakes] can nullify the one which fell in whether by weight or by number.<br>What is implied? A cake [of figs] fell into a mixture of 100 other cakes, both large and small. We assume that a small cake<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, we know that a large cake fell in, but we are uncertain about its size, we cannot merely count 101 cakes both large and small to nullify it (Rabbi Akiva Eiger).</i> fell in and one may remove one of the small cakes.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since there were 101 cakes, 101 times the number of cakes that fell in, the cake that was <i>terumah</i> could be nullified. We assume that it was small and hence, to fulfill the obligation to remove a cake, we remove a small one.</i> If a cake fell into 40 cakes, 20 of them weighing four <i>litra</i> each and 20 of them, weighing one <i>litra</i> each, since the combined weight is 101 <i>litra</i>, we assume a small one fell in and remove one of the small ones.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder are permitted, because it is possible that there was 101 times the weight of the <i>terumah</i> in the mixture. It is sufficient to remove a small one. The rationale is that since the <i>terumah</i> has been nullified, the removal of the cake is required only as a financial matter: to give the priest his due. Hence, to receive a larger cake, the priest must prove that a larger cake did indeed fall in.</i>
Flour and finely sifted flour cannot be combined together to nullify <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., there is one container of flour and one container of finely sifted flour. <i>Terumah</i> fell into one of the containers, but we do not know which one. We do not say that the two containers of flour should be considered like the two groups of figs and considered as a single entity. Instead, we judge them individually. The rationale is that once the <i>terumah</i> becomes mixed with the flour or the finely sifted flour, it is part of one mixture and not the other. Hence it is not appropriate to combine them (Radbaz).</i>
When a <i>se'ah</i> of wheat which is <i>terumah</i> falls on the mouth of the storage vat of wheat, we do not measure it against the 101 times [its weight or ordinary grain found] in the storage vat. For we do not make such an assessment unless the <i>terumah</i> has been mixed with the ordinary produce<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">And in this instance, that is not true, for it is positioned at the top of the storage container.</i> or we do not know where the <i>terumah</i> fell.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">And in this instance, we do.</i><br>What should be done? We consider the <i>terumah</i> as if it is wheat placed above barley and separate the <i>terumah</i> itself which fell unto the storage vat with some of the ordinary produce upon which they fell as one would be collecting wheat from barley.
[The following laws apply when] there are two containers or two storage vats and <i>terumah</i> fell into one of them. It became mixed with [the contents of the container or storage vat] and it is not known which one it fell into. If the two storage vats were in the same building, they are considered as if they were combined and the <i>terumah</i> is nullified if the entire quantity is 101 times its weight. It is as if they were both contained in a single storage vat. Moreover, the containers are considered as combined together even if they are in two separate buildings, for it is possible to gather the two in one building.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 12:4), the Rambam writes that containers are frequently moved and in the process of their being moved, the two containers could be combined. Hence, we view them as if they were combined at present. This ruling is also quoted in other contexts; see <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 111:7).</i> If, however, the two containers were in two cities, they are not combined.
How should he remove the <i>se'ah</i> which fell in?<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as stated in Chapter 13, Halachah 1, one <i>se'ah</i> must be removed from the mixture and given to the priest. How should that be done in the present instance? For the <i>terumah</i> fell into only one of the containers and we do not know which one.</i> If he desires to remove it from one of them, he may. If he desires to remove half from one and half from the other, he may.<br>[The following laws apply when] there are jugs filled with dried figs that are ordinary produce and [the owner] pressed a <i>litra</i> of figs that are <i>terumah</i> into the opening of one of them, but he does not remember which one. If there are 101 jugs, the <i>terumah</i> is considered nullified. He should take one jug and sell it to a priest except for the worth of the <i>litra</i> [of <i>terumah</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Although only the figs on the top of the opening are <i>terumah</i>, we require himself to sell the entire jug, because of the impression that might be created (<i>Shita Mekubetzet, Beitzah</i> 4a).</i> and the remainder [of the jugs] are permitted. If there are less than 100 jugs, [the figs at] the openings [of the jugs] are considered <i>miduma</i> and those at the bottom are permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since we are talking about compressed figs, they will not mix with the contents of the jugs, but instead will be found on the top of a jug. Hence, when considering nullifying the figs, only the tops of the jugs are considered, but not the bottoms. Therefore we require 101 jugs, not 101 times the weight of the initial amount of <i>terumah</i>.<br>The Radbaz explains that this situation differs from that described in Halachah 1. In that situation, although the light figs and the dark figs could be distinguished from each other, they were all mixed together. Hence, it is possible to speak about one type being combined with the other to nullify the <i>terumah</i>. In the situation described by our Halachah, the tops and the bottoms will always remain discrete.</i>
Similar laws apply if one pressed [a <i>litra</i> of] figs into the opening of a <i>kaveret</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>kaveret</i> literally means "bee-hive." Here we are talking about a storage compartment that is built like a bee-hive.</i>or into the top of a cake.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we require 101 of the containers and count only the figs at the openings of the containers.</i> If he pressed them onto the top of a cake, but does not know whether he pressed them onto the northern portion [of the cake] or its southern portion, nor does he know which cake he pressed them onto, we look at all the figs as if they were separate entities and [the figs that are <i>terumah</i>] should be nullified based on weight. If all the cakes weigh 100<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., without the <i>terumah</i>, 101 <i>litra</i> with the <i>terumah</i>.</i> <i>litra</i>, the <i>terumah</i> is nullified<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we consider the entire mixture as a single entity unlike the previous instances where the bottoms of the containers were considered as separate from the tops. In the previous instances, he knew that he pressed the <i>terumah</i> onto the tops of the container. Therefore only the tops are considered. In the present instance, he does not know the portion of the cakes unto which he pressed the figs. That lack of knowledge works to his advantage, enabling us to count in the entire mixture.</i> provided each of the cakes weighs more than two <i>litra</i> so that in each cake, the <i>terumah</i> is nullified because there is a majority of ordinary produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to considering the status of the entire mixture, we must consider the status of each cake individually. Each cake must have enough figs to nullify the presence of the <i>terumah</i> according to Scriptural Law.</i> The rationale is that when there is a doubt concerning the presence of <i>terumah</i>, it is nullified when there is a majority of ordinary produce.
When <i>terumah</i> is definitely [present in a mixture], it is forbidden if the mixture is 100 [or less times its weight]. If there is merely a doubt concerning the presence of <i>terumah</i>, it is forbidden if there are only fifty. It is permitted only if one adds a majority [of the new mixture].<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as the Rambam explains in the following halachah, one must add enough figs so that there is 101 times the amount of <i>terumah</i> that fell in (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> If there are more than fifty, one need not add such a majority.<br>What is implied? One fig that is <i>terumah</i> fell into 99 figs and they are all present. They are all forbidden to non-priests, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 13, Halachah 1, it is necessary to have 101 times the amount of <i>terumah</i>.</i>
If, [after a fig that is <i>terumah</i> fell into 50 other figs, and then] one of the mixture becomes lost, it is possible that one of the ordinary figs was the one that was lost, but it is possible that it was the fig that fell in. Hence, the mixture is forbidden until one adds ordinary produce to it from another source,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">It is, however, permitted to add such produce. We do not apply the principle that, as an initial preference, one should not nullify the presence of a forbidden substance, because here we are not certain that there is a forbidden substance present, for one fig has been lost.</i> adding 51 figs to the entire quantity. If one fig fell into 51 figs and one of the mixture was lost, the remainder is permitted to non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since one fig has been lost, 51 figs are sufficient. We do not require 101. Note th contrast to Chapter 15, Halachah 2.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell into less than 100 <i>se'ah</i> of the first tithe from which <i>terumat ma'aser</i> was not separated or into [produce from] the second tithe or consecrated property that was not redeemed and the entire mixture became <i>miduma</i>. If [the <i>terumah</i>] fell into the first tithe, the <i>terumat ma'aser</i> should be designated,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">So that the mitzvah of separating it has been fulfilled.</i> and the entire mixture sold to the priests with the exception of the worth of the <i>terumah</i> that fell into it and the worth of the <i>terumat ma'aser</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For such produce must be given to the priests as a present. The majority of the mixture, however, belongs to its owner. Even though it is being given to the priest, because the owner may not make use of it, the priest must reimburse the owner for its value. Nevertheless, as mentioned previously, the value of produce that is <i>terumah</i> is less than that of ordinary produce.<br>The Radbaz mentions another possible solution to this difficulty: If the Levite has a large amount of produce from which <i>terumat ma'aser</i> has not been separated, he may make this entire mixture <i>terumat ma'aser</i> for that produce and in this way, not suffer any financial loss.</i> If it fell into [produce from] the second tithe or consecrated property that was not redeemed, they should be redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">At which point they are considered as ordinary produce and the laws mentioned in Chapter 13, Halachah 2, apply.</i> and then sold to a priest with the exception of the worth of the <i>terumah</i>.
When a <i>se'ah</i> of impure <i>terumah</i> falls into less than 100 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce or into produce that is from the first tithe, or from the second tithe, or from consecrated property - whether [the latter three] are ritually pure or ritually impure - [the mixture] is <i>miduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Because there are less than 100 <i>se'ah</i> to nullify the <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i>.</i> Hence, the entire mixture is like impure <i>terumah</i> which is forbidden to everyone. Thus it is all forbidden and must be left until it rots.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">One would think that it should be used as fuel and thus the person will derive some benefit from it. The Radbaz explains that this is not allowed for the following reason. Since there is not enough other produce to nullify the <i>terumah</i>, the entire mixture is considered as <i>terumah</i>. Nevertheless, since it is not impure, it should not be burnt, because it is forbidden to burn pure <i>terumah</i>. This stringency is, however, only observed when there is no danger that the <i>terumah</i> will be eaten, as the Rambam proceeds to explain.</i><br>When does the above apply? With regard to an entity that is not eaten raw.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., wheat or barley.</i> When, by contrast, it is normal practice to partake of a type of produce uncooked,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., oil.</i> one should not set them aside lest someone encounter them and partake of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 12, which states that impure <i>terumah</i> should be placed in repugnant container so that no one will accidentally partake of it.</i> Instead, the entire mixture should be used as fuel, like impure <i>terumah</i> is used as fuel.
When a <i>se'ah</i> of pure <i>terumah</i> falls into less than 100 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce that is impure, the entire mixture should be sold to a priest with the exception of the worth of the <i>terumah</i>. The priest should eat this mixture which is <i>miduma</i> as roasted kernels<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, the impure grain does not cause the pure <i>terumah</i> to become impure, for produce does not become fit to contract impurity until comes in contact with one of seven liquids (<i>Hilchot Tumat Ochalin</i> 1:1-2). Since the kernels of the <i>terumah</i> are roasted without contact with water, they are never fit to contract impurity.</i> or he should make them into a dough using fruit juice which does not render produce fit to contract impurity,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Fruit juice is not one of these seven liquids. Hence dough made with fruit juice is not susceptible to ritual impurity (<i>Ibid.</i> 3; 13:13).</i> so that [contact with] the impure ordinary produce will not render the <i>terumah</i> as impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">It is not only that the priest will be suffering a loss because the <i>terumah</i> becomes impure. It is forbidden to cause <i>terumah</i> to become impure as stated above.</i><br>Alternatively, he should make this mixture that is <i>miduma</i> into a dough that is less than the size of an egg. [This is beneficial], because impure food does not cause other food to become impure until [the impure food] is the size of an egg.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Ibid.</i> 4:1, 12. Making the mixture into small loaves is the Rambam's interpretation of the term <i>nikudim</i> in the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 5:1).</i> Or he may divide the mixture that is <i>miduma</i> and place a portion of it that is less than the size of an egg in each dough so that the <i>terumah</i> in it will not become impure.
When a <i>se'ah</i> of impure <i>terumah</i> falls into 100 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce that is pure or a <i>se'ah</i> of pure <i>terumah</i> falls into 100 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce that is impure, it should be removed and the <i>terumah</i> is nullified, because the new mixture is 101 times [the size of the <i>terumah</i> that fell in. That <i>se'ah</i>] should be eaten as roasted kernels, or it should be made into dough with fruit juice, or into dough that is less than the size of an egg.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he should use the grain in a manner that will prevent it from contracting ritual impurity, as described in the previous halachah.</i> [The rationale is that] the <i>se'ah</i> which fell in is not [necessarily] the <i>se'ah</i> that was removed.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., and hence it is permitted to be eaten.</i>
When a <i>se'ah</i> of impure <i>terumah</i> falls into 100 <i>se'ah</i> of pure <i>terumah</i>, it is nullified because of the insignificant amount and the entire mixture should be eaten in a state of ritual purity.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">There is no need for any safeguards.</i> If it fell into less than 100, he should leave the entire mixture until it rots.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Halachah 11.</i>
There were two containers [of grain, each containing less than 100 <i>se'ah</i>]. A <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell into one of them and it was known into which one it fell.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">And the contents of that container was considered as <i>miduma</i>.</i> Afterwards, a second <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell into one of them, but it was not known into which it fell. [The rationale is that] we assume that the second <i>se'ah</i> fell into the same place as the first <i>se'ah</i>, for we associate the problematic issue with the [previous] problem.<br>If, however, the first <i>se'ah</i> fell into one of the containers, but it was not known into which it fell and afterwards, a second <i>se'ah</i> fell into one and it was known into which one it fell, we do not say that the first one fell into the same place as the second. Instead, both are considered to be problematic.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are both <i>miduma</i>. The rationale is that the problematic status of the two containers was established before the second one fell in and the fact that we know into which one it fell cannot resolve the existing problem.<br>Rav Yosef Korcus maintains that this is the interpretation of the Rambam's ruling. Nevertheless, he and the Radbaz both maintain that this law applies even if the first container contains 100 <i>se'ah</i> and the first <i>se'ah</i> is nullified. Since one <i>se'ah</i> has to be removed from it, it can also be considered as problematic.</i>
If there were two containers [of grain], one ritually pure and one ritually impure,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that this law applies whether the grain in the containers was <i>terumah</i> or ordinary produce.</i> and a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> fell into one and it was not known into which, we assume that it fell into the impure one.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we follow the same principle mentioned in the first clause of the previous halachah, because the impure grain is also considered as "problematic."</i>
When there are two containers, one containing pure <i>terumah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests that the text should read "impure <i>terumah</i>."</i> and one containing impure ordinary produce,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests that the text should read "pure ordinary produce." His rationale for these emendations is that if the ordinary produce is impure, it is not proper to say that it should be "eaten in a state of ritual purity." With these emendations, he resolves the objections of the Ra'avad. As will be explained, the Radbaz offers an interpretation that preserves the standard version of the text.</i> should a <i>se'ah</i> of pure <i>terumah</i> fall into one of them,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">But we do not know which.</i> we assume that it fell into the one containing <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">This represents the converse of the principle mentioned in Halachah 15, just as there, we associate the problematic issue with the existing problem, here we associate the produce that is of a positive nature (<i>terumah</i>) with the existing <i>terumah</i> (Radbaz).</i> The ordinary produce should, however, be eaten in a state of ritual purity like <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., according to one of the three suggestions given in Halachah 12. The intent is that we are not certain that the <i>terumah</i> did indeed fall into the container containing <i>terumah</i>. Were it to have fallen into the other container, it would be forbidden to prepare a dough from it in the ordinary manner, because that would cause the <i>terumah</i> to contract ritual impurity which is forbidden.</i>
[18-19]<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Our text is taken from the Shabsei Frankel printing of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> which is based on authentic manuscripts and early printing. The standard printed text is both redundant and problematic.</i> When a <i>se'ah</i> of impure <i>terumah</i> falls into one of the above mentioned containers, we say that it fell into the <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">For we assume that the <i>terumah</i> fell into <i>terumah</i>.</i> The ordinary produce should, however, be eaten as roasted kernels or made into dough with fruit juice.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">To prevent it from contracting ritual impurity as mentioned above.</i>
—
When there are two containers, one containing impure <i>terumah</i> and the other ordinary produce that is pure, and a <i>se'ah</i> of pure <i>terumah</i> falls into one of them,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Here also the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests inverting the words pure and impure in the text. Otherwise, this ruling would be a contradiction to Halachah 17.</i> we assume that it fell into the <i>terumah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the entire mixture is considered as impure <i>terumah</i>.</i> but the ordinary produce should be eaten as roasted kernels.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">So that the <i>terumah</i> will not be subject to contracting ritual impurity (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
If a <i>se'ah</i> of impure <i>terumah</i> fell into one of these containers, both of them are forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the leniencies of assuming that the problematic <i>se'ah</i> fell into the produce that was already problematic or that <i>terumah</i> fell into <i>terumah</i> are not granted, because, as the Rambam continues to explain, here a Scriptural prohibition is involved.</i> [The rationale is that when] there is a doubt [whether produce is] impure <i>terumah</i>, it is forbidden to be eaten, while when there is a doubt whether it is <i>miduma</i>, it is permitted. For the prohibition against partaking of impure <i>terumah</i> is Scriptural in origin,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachah 3. The impure <i>terumah</i> does not become nullified because it was mixed with a larger quantity of other produce.</i> while the prohibition against partaking of a mixture that is <i>miduma</i> is Rabbinic in origin<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Scriptural Law, as long as there is a majority of non-<i>terumah</i> produce, the <i>terumah</i> is nullified.</i> based on the principles explained in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 15:1-3, 13, 15.</i>

Chapter 15

A sealed barrel [of wine that is <i>terumah</i>] makes a mixture <i>miduma</i> whatever the ratio [between it and the other barrels].<br>What is implied? When a sealed barrel [of wine] that is <i>terumah</i> becomes mixed with several thousand sealed barrels [of ordinary wine], the entire mixture becomes <i>miduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the principle <i>devar shebiminyan lo betal</i>: "The presence of an object that is sold by number is never considered insignificant." Since these objects are sold by number, each one is considered important and it is not appropriate to say that one is insignificant (<i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 16:1-3).</i> If the barrels are opened, [the presence of the <i>terumah</i>] is nullified when 101 times its contents are present.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For once the barrels are opened, they are no longer considered as important. It is, however, forbidden to open the barrels, for this would be considered as purposefully nullifying a forbidden substance and that is prohibited (Radbaz).</i>
When a sealed barrel [that contains <i>terumah</i>] becomes mixed with 100 barrels and then one falls into the Mediterranean Sea, they are all permitted. We assume that it is the one that is <i>terumah</i> that fell in. This is not the case when a fig falls into 100 figs. In the latter instance, it is necessary to set aside one [fig].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">And give it to a priest. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Terumot</i> 4:7) records a difference of opinion concerning this matter between Reish Lakish and Rabbi Yochanan. Reish Lakish's opinion is the one cited by the Rambam. Rabbi Yochanan, however, differs and maintains that the same law applies even with regard to figs. The Rambam rules according to Reish Lakish's opinion, for the Babylonian Talmud (<i>Zevachim</i> 74b) mentions his view only and not that of Rabbi Yochanan. See also <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 7:10 where the Rambam issues a similar ruling.<br>The Ra'avad does not accept this ruling. He maintains that the Babylonian Talmud mentions Reish Lakish's opinion only in connection with the discussion of another minority view. Hence, it is not necessary to mention Rabbi Yochanan's view.</i> [The rationale for the distinction is] that a barrel that falls into the sea is noticeable. A fig and the like which fall are not noticeable.
When a sealed barrel [that contains <i>terumah</i>] becomes mixed with 100 barrels and one of them is opened,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Inadvertently. It is forbidden to open such a barrel intentionally.</i> he should remove one hundredth of it,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">More precisely, 1/101. I.e., the percent of the mixture that is <i>terumah</i>. This is given to a priest.</i> and then he may drink the barrel.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">It is true that this ruling is somewhat problematic. For if the barrel is in fact <i>terumah</i>, the entire barrel, not only the hundredth portion, must be given to the priest. And if it is not <i>terumah</i>, it is permitted to drink it in its entirety. Nothing need be given to the priest.<br>The <i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i> explains the reason for the stringency. Were this safeguard not taken, one might permit the barrels even when they were all opened.</i> The other barrels are, however, forbidden until they are opened. Whenever any one of them is opened, he should remove the percentage that causes the mixture to be <i>miduma</i> and drink the remaining hundred portions.<br>If a barrel [containing <i>terumah</i>] became mixed with 150 barrels and 100 of them were opened, one should remove the percentage that causes the mixture to be <i>miduma</i>, i.e., one barrel and drink [the remaining barrels]. The remaining 50 are still forbidden; we do not presume that the barrel that was <i>terumah</i> was among the majority [that were opened].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Although when considering those barrels, it is necessary to give a barrel to a priest because of the suspicion that one is <i>terumah</i>, we are still stringent regarding those remaining.</i> Even if there are several thousand barrels, they are all considered <i>miduma</i>. Whenever any one is opened, he should remove one hundredth of it and then he may drink the remainder [of that barrel]. The other barrels, however, are <i>miduma</i>,
We have already explained in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 16, Halachah 1.</i> that a leavening agent or a spice<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 16:26, the Radbaz states that this same law applies if <i>terumah</i> is used to cause cheese to harden.</i> causes a mixture to be forbidden regardless of how small the amount is mixed in.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the standard ration of 1/101 is not effective to nullify the <i>terumah</i>. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 2:4; <i>Terumot</i> 10:2), the Rambam explains that since the effects of the <i>terumah</i> are evident in the food - it rises or it is spiced because of it - we cannot say that the <i>terumah</i> is nullified.</i> Therefore if a person pureed an apple that was <i>terumah</i> and put it in a dough and it caused [the dough] to leaven, the entire dough becomes <i>miduma</i> and is forbidden to non-priests.
When an egg, even its yolk,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">We are speaking about a situation where the egg is cooked whole. Thus despite the fact that the yolk is on the inside and covered by the whites, a spice intended for it can cause it to be forbidden, because it absorbs (Radbaz, <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> was flavored with spices that are <i>terumah</i>, it is forbidden, because it absorbs.
When yeast that is <i>terumah</i> fell into a dough and it was removed and afterwards, the mixture became leavened, [the dough] is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since the yeast was removed, we do not say that it caused the dough to rise. Instead, we assume that the dough rose on its own accord (Radbaz).</i>
We have already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 13, Halachah 2; <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:1,6.</i> that if <i>terumah</i> becomes mixed with a substance of a different type, [the mixture is forbidden if the <i>terumah</i>] imparts its flavor [to the mixture]. Therefore, [the following laws apply when] a diced onion is cooked together with other food. If the onion is <i>terumah</i> and the other food is ordinary produce and it is flavored by the onion, the other food is forbidden to non-priests. If the onion is ordinary produce and the cooked food is <i>terumah</i> and the flavor of the cooked food was imparted to the onion, the onion is forbidden [to non-priests].<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">How do we know whether the flavor of the <i>terumah</i> was imparted to the mixture. We give it to a priest to taste (<i>ibid.</i> 15:29).</i>
[The following laws apply if] lentils were cooked and then a dry onion was placed among them: If the onion was whole, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 10:1), the Rambam explains that this applies whether the onion is <i>terumah</i> and the lentils ordinary produce or the reverse. In both instances, since the lentils are already cooked and the onion is whole, there is little likelihood that one will absorb the flavor from the other.</i> If it was diced, [the ruling depends on whether] its flavor was imparted.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Here also the same principle applies whether it is the onion or the lentils which are <i>terumah</i>.
