Since the onion is diced, it will both impart and absorb flavor easier.</i> If he cooked the onion with the lentils, whether it is whole or diced, we estimate whether it imparted its flavor.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since the onion is cooked, even if it is whole, it can both impart and absorb flavor easily.</i> With regard to the remainder of the cooked food,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in addition to the lentils.</i> regardless of whether the onion was placed upon it after it was cooked or cooked with it, or whether it was whole or diced, we estimate whether it imparted its flavor.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam proceeds to explain, lentils are considered as being unique. Once they are cooked, they do not absorb flavor from other foods easily. Other foods are more absorbent. Even after they have been cooked, they may absorb the flavor of the onion.</i><br>Why do we not estimate [whether flavor was imparted] when a whole onion was placed among cooked lentils? Because it does not absorb from them, for it is whole, nor does it impart [flavor] to them, because they have already been cooked. If the onions were soft,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests that the text should read <i>rabbim</i>, "many," instead of <i>rachim</i>, "soft;" i.e., one onion that is whole will not impart that much flavor, but several onions will.</i> it is considered as if they were diced. Similarly, if its tip or its outer shell<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Once the tip or the outer shell is removed, its flavor will be imparted easily even though it is still whole.</i> was removed or it was moist, it is considered as if it was diced. Wild onions, whether moist or dry, whether whole or diced, we see if they have imparted their flavor.
When one pickles vegetables that are ordinary produce with those of <i>terumah,</i> they are permitted to non-priests<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In certain contexts (see <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:34), pickling is considered as cooking. Nevertheless, in this instance, the Rambam rules leniently.</i> with the exception of onions, scallions, and garlic.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Because their sharp and pungent taste nature causes their flavor to be imparted to any vegetables pickled with them [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 10:10)].</i> [With regard to these species,] if one pickled vegetables that are ordinary produce with onions that are <i>terumah</i> or onions that are ordinary produce with onions that are <i>terumah</i>, they are forbidden to non-priests. If one pickled vegetables that are <i>terumah</i> with an onion that is ordinary produce, the onion is permitted to non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">For the onion will not absorb the flavor of the other produce.</i>
[The following rules apply when] olives that are ordinary produce were pickled together with olives that are <i>terumah</i>. If they were both crushed, or the ordinary olives were crushed, but those which were <i>terumah</i> were whole,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">When olives are crushed, they will absorb easier [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 10:7)].</i> or they were pickled in brine that was <i>terumah</i>, they are forbidden to non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For the brine imparts its flavor to the objects pickled in within it.</i> If, however, they were both whole or the olives that were <i>terumah</i> were crushed and those that were ordinary produce were whole, they are permitted, because those that are crushed absorb from those which are whole.
Water in which <i>terumah</i> was pickled or cooked is forbidden to non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">For it has absorbed the flavor of the <i>terumah</i>.</i>
The laws governing <i>terumah</i> apply to anise as long as it has not yet flavored a dish [of food].<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Anise is used as flavoring. Once it has flavored other foods, it is discarded. Hence, until it has flavored other foods, it is significant and the laws of <i>terumah</i> apply. Afterwards, it is considered as a wasteproduct (Radbaz).</i> Once it has flavored a dish, the laws of <i>terumah</i> no longer apply.
[The following laws apply when a person] removes a warm loaf of bread [from the oven] and places it over a barrel of wine that is <i>terumah</i>. If the loaf is from wheat, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the bread may absorb the fragrance of the wine, it does not absorb its substance and the fragrance alone is of no consequence. This is a general principle that applies with regard to all prohibited foods; see <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:33.</i> If it is from barley, it is forbidden, because it draws forth [the wine itself].<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">It is considered as the actual substance of the wine has been imparted to the bread.</i>
When an oven has been heated with cumin that is <i>terumah</i>, the bread is permitted. For it does not have the flavor of the cumin, only its fragrance and fragrance does not create a prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 16:22, the Rambam writes that when an oven is heated with shells of produce that is <i>orlah</i> or mixed species in a vineyard, whatever is cooked in the oven is forbidden. The commentaries note, however, that there is a fundamental distinction between the two instances. It is forbidden to benefit from - not only to eat - the prohibited substances mentioned in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot.</i> It is, by contrast, permitted to benefit from <i>terumah</i>.</i>
When barley that is <i>terumah</i> falls into a cistern of water, the water is permitted even if [the barley] spoils the flavor of the water, for food that imparts an undesirable flavor does not bring about a prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This concept also applies with regard to all the Torah's prohibitions; see <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:28.</i>
[The following law applies when] chilba<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">A pungent herb.</i> that is <i>terumah</i> and its plant fall into a cistern of wine. If the chilba seed itself<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., without the influence of the plant whose flavor is similar to that of the seeds. The rationale is that the plant is not considered as <i>terumah</i> [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 10:5)]. See also Chapter 2, Halachah 4.</i> is of sufficient quantity to impart its flavor to the wine, the wine is forbidden to non-priests.
When there are two cups of wine: one of <i>terumah</i> and one of ordinary wine<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">And the ordinary wine is less than 100 times the amount of <i>terumah</i> wine.</i> and they were each mixed with water and then combined, we consider it as if the ordinary wine was not present and as if the wine that was <i>terumah</i>was mixed with the water alone, for water is not of its type. If that water is sufficient to nullify the taste of the <i>terumah</i> wine in the mixture, the entire mixture is permitted to non-priests. If not, it is forbidden, for as we have explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 9, which explains that the water is not combined with the ordinary wine to create a mixture 100 times the amount of the <i>terumah</i>.</i> water does not nullify wine.
When wine that is <i>terumah</i> falls on produce, one may wash the fruits and they are permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz notes that in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 16:32, the Rambam writes a similar law with regard to wine associated with idol worship that fell on produce. There, however, he rules stringently, stating that the fruits are forbidden if the wine is poured upon them while they are broken open. The Radbaz explains that the Rambam did not mention the point here, because he relied on what he had stated earlier. Indeed, if the fruit was broken open and the wine improved its flavor, it should be sold to a priest at the price of <i>terumah</i>.</i> Similarly, when oil that is <i>terumah</i> falls on produce, one may wash the fruits and they are permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Even though oil is more likely to cling to fruit than wine, it is sufficient to wash the fruit. Generally, oil does not improve the taste of fruit (Radbaz).</i><br>When oil [that is <i>terumah</i>] falls on [ordinary] wine, one should make it coagulate<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">For in this way, the oil can be removed without it mixing with the wine. Similarly, oil impairs the flavor of wine (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> and the wine is then permitted to non-priests. If [oil that is <i>terumah</i>] mixes with brine, it should be made to coagulate, then the upper layer of the brine should be removed<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This stringency is necessary, because oil improves the flavor of brine (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> so that all the brine that has the taste of oil will be removed.
A pot in which <i>terumah</i> was cooked should not be used to cook ordinary produce. If it was cooked in it, it causes it to be forbidden if its flavor is recognizable. If he washes out the pot with wine or with water, it is permitted to cook in it.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad questions the Rambam's ruling, asking why this instance is different from the laws involving non-kosher food absorbed in a utensil. In that instance, it is not sufficient to wash out the utensil, one must perform <i>hagalah</i>, boiling water in the utensil to purge the absorbed matter. The Radbaz explains that the Rambam maintains that this concept applies with regard to sanctified foods and other prohibitions, but not with regard to <i>terumah</i>. The rationale is that if the priests consider the <i>terumah</i> insignificant - as would be the case with regard to <i>terumah</i> absorbed in a pot - it is no longer of consequence. He draws support for this explanation from the mishnah in <i>Terumot</i> 11:8 (quoted in Chapter 11, Halachah 15) which states that after one pours out the contents of a jug of wine of <i>terumah</i>, one can pour ordinary wine into the container even if there is a small amount of residue from the previous wine. This explanation is also given in one of the responsa ascribed to the Rambam in a response to a question concerning this subject by the sages of Lunil. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> also cites these ideas and states that the reason the Rambam requires that the pot be washed out is because the contents are being cooked. If they were not to be cooked, even washing would not be necessary.</i> If he cooked [<i>terumah</i>] in part of the pot, it is not necessary to wash out the entire pot, only the place where it was cooked.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This leniency applies only with regard to <i>terumah</i> and not to prohibited food absorbed in a utensil. The rationale is the same as in the previous note: Since the priests consider this <i>terumah</i> insignificant, we do not show concern about it (Radbaz).</i>
The great <i>terumah, terumat ma'aser, challah,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The portion of dough that must be separated and given to a priest.</i> and the first fruits are all referred to as <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10, Halachah 4.</i> With regard to <i>terumat ma'aser</i>, [Numbers 18:26] states: "And you shall remove the <i>terumah</i> of G‑d from it" and [<i>ibid.</i> 15:20] states that it should be "like the <i>terumah</i> of your grain heap."<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This verse refers to <i>challah</i>; or it is comparing <i>challah</i> to the great <i>terumah</i>. Perhaps a scribal error crept into the text and this was intended as support for the following clause. The Radbaz, however, gives an explanation why it was included here.</i><br>With regard to <i>challah</i>, [<i>ibid.</i>] states: "You shall separate <i>challah</i> as <i>terumah</i>." And [Deuteronomy 12:17]: "You may not eat in your gates the tithes of your grain, your wine, and your oil,... and the <i>terumah</i> of your hand." Now there are no substances that are required to be brought [to Jerusalem to be eaten] that are not explicitly mentioned in the verse except the first fruits. Thus the phrase "the <i>terumah</i> of your hand" can be applied to them. Thus we see that they are [also] called <i>terumah</i>.
Therefore the laws regarding partaking of all four types of substances and mixtures of them and other substances are the same. All can be nullified in a mixture of 101 times the original substance. They are also all combined with each other.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., to cause a mixture to be forbidden to non-priests.</i> If they become impure, they should all be burnt. The laws that apply to <i>terumat ma'aser</i> that is <i>demai</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Produce from which we are unsure that the tithes have been separated.</i> are the same that apply to <i>terumat ma'aser</i> separated from produce [from which the tithes were] definitely [not separated]. It is only that its violation is not punishable by lashes.
Anyone who partakes of <i>terumah</i> should recite the blessing for that particular food and then recite the blessing:<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">For partaking of <i>terumah</i> is a mitzvah and we are required to recite a blessing before the observance of all mitzvot (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> "[Blessed are You... who has sanctified us with the holiness of Aaron and commanded us to partake of <i>terumah</i>."<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hillchot Bikkurim</i> 1:2.</i> We have received the tradition and observed that people would recite this blessing even when partaking of <i>challah</i> separated in the Diaspora.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it is only a Rabbinic commandment.</i> For even partaking of sacred foods from the boundaries [of our Holy Land] is like service [in the Temple],<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesachim</i> 72b relates that one day Rabbi Tarfon did not come to the House of study. Rabban Gamliel rebuked him for his absence. Rabbi Tarfon answered that he was carrying out priestly worship. Rabban Gamliel asked him how that was possible, for they lived in the era of the Temple's destruction. Rabbi Tarfon replied that partaking of <i>terumah</i> was equivalent to serving in the Temple.</i> as [Numbers 18:7] states: "I have granted you your priestly service as a gift."
